HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

30-Year Bond Falls to 6%, Its Lowest Level in 2 Years
 The yield on the Treasury's benchmark 30-year bond closed at 6 percent, the lowest closing level in two years. A bond-market rally entered its fourth day on economic data that pointed to weak growth and on optimism that a Federal budget agreement was attainable in Washington. The long bond climbed 15/32 of a point in price, pulling its yield down from 6.03 percent on Tuesday. 
 Stocks were mixed in thin trading, but were buoyed by the lower rates. The Dow Jones industrial average was up 16 points before ending down 4.34, at 5,105.92. [D6.]
 Staff members of the White House and Congressional Republicans resumed discussions on balancing the budget as Federal employees grew restive. [A1.]
 
Fannie Mae to Buy Back Stock
 Federal National Mortgage announced plans to buy back as much as 9 percent of its common stock in one of this year's largest buybacks. The company, widely known as Fannie Mae, also said that it would split its stock on a four-for-one basis and give three million shares to its charitable foundation. The shares rose $5.25, to $125. They have risen 72 percent this year. [D2.]
 
Cordis Shares Tumble 11%
 Cordis shares plunged 11 percent after Johnson & Johnson delayed its $1.8 billion buyout, but some analysts said they doubted that the deal was in danger. Much of the drop was attributed to speculators who were getting out because of uncertainty, however slim, that the deal might fall through. Both companies treat heart disease. [D2.]
 
A Powerhouse Is Born
 Boston Scientific has swallowed five rivals in 18 months, for $2 billion of stock, in hope that will give it the power to survive. The stock has almost tripled this year. The company focuses on minimally invasive medical devices suited to a cost-cutting era. Market Place. [D6.]
 
Housing Sales Down 1.7%
 Sales of existing homes fell 1.7 percent in November, a realtors' group said, despite lower mortgage rates. Also, consumer confidence fell in December. [D2.]
 
Forte to Sell Assets to Whitbread
 Forte of Britain agreed to sell its restaurants and budget hotels to Whitbread for $1.62 billion, acting to thwart a hostile takeover bid from the Granada Group. Recently, Forte has raised about $1.7 billion through asset sales. Granada deplored the deal as bad policy. [D4.]
 
Disarray in Mexico
 Economic disarray, official corruption and other problems have pushed Mexico into crisis. The man at the center of the storm is President Ernesto Zedillo, who departs from Mexico's tradition of a strong leader. He promises a transformation to democracy, but some others fear chaos. [A1.]
 
Asian Orders for Boeing
 Boeing won an order from Philippine Airlines for eight 747's and is expected to win part of a $4 billion order to be placed by Malaysia's national carrier. In the last month or so, Boeing has also received big orders from Singapore and Taiwan. [D4.]
 
Europe's Monetary Problems
 A single currency and central bank won't solve all of Europe's economic problems. High unemployment and vast disparities in living standards would become harder to deal with. And lagging areas would no longer have the expedient of allowing their currencies to depreciate in order to regain competitiveness. Peter Passell: Economic Scene. [D2.]
 
Singing Northwest's Praises
 Tony Bennett's latest song is a 60-second commercial for Northwest Airlines. The pop singer is the latest of more than two dozen artists who have sung Northwest's praises in pop, jazz, blues, rock, country and zydeco, including B. B. King, left. Each of their sessions is used in an in-flight video and in television and radio commercials. Advertising. [D5.]
 
Natural-Gas Futures Prices Soar
 Natural-gas futures prices soared 16 percent as traders grew more concerned about falling supplies and below-normal temperatures across much of the country. [D16.]

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Gains in Light Turnover As Traders Await Budget Action

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
The dollar rose slightly yesterday in light trading devoid of any major trend.
Many traders in the United States and Europe remained on holiday and those who were working were reluctant to make big moves ahead of developments in the Federal budget stalemate. 
 "The dollar is in a very tight range," said Augie Floro, a senior trader with Bank Austria in New York. "There is no information to trade on. Everyone is just waiting for the holidays to be over and for something on the budget impasse situation."
In late New York trading, the dollar was quoted at 1.4335 German marks, up from 1.4330 marks on Tuesday. The dollar also rose to 102.84 Japanese yen, from 102.40 yen.
The British pound rose to $1.5594 in New York, from $1.5560 on Tuesday.
Some traders said the dollar might be benefiting from recent reports showing a sluggish economy, indicating that inflation remained under control. But others said the data could also point to a possible further decline in interest rates early next year, which might hurt the dollar's value by making dollar-denominated assets less attractive.
Sales of previously owned homes dropped 1.7 percent in November, the second straight decline, the National Association of Realtors said yesterday. And the Conference Board, a research group in New York, said its monthly barometer of consumer confidence fell in December.
On Tuesday, the Johnson Redbook Service, a prominent barometer of the retailing industry, reported a disappointing 3.9 percent rise in Christmas sales.
Still, some currency strategists dismissed the latest statistics, saying the foreign exchange market already expected further declines in American interest rates.
"The economic numbers didn't shake the market by any means," said Ken Nixon, a currency adviser with the Bank of Montreal.
There was little action to resolve the Federal budget impasse yesterday. President Clinton and Republicans in Congress remain stalemated in efforts to balance the budget in seven years.
In late New York trading, the dollar reached 1.1573 Swiss francs, up from 1.1521; 4.8980 French francs, down from 4.9050; 1,588.50 Italian lire, up from 1,585.75, and 1.3577 Canadian dollars, down from 1.3602.

HEADLINE: Mexico's 'Accidental' Chief Spurns Role of Strongman

BYLINE: By ANTHONY DePALMA 

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY, Dec. 27

 BODY:
The tall pines for which Mexico's presidential mansion is named filter the midday sunlight and cast gentle, dancing shadows. But such tranquillity is deceptive because inside Los Pinos a struggle is raging over the Mexican presidency and, in large part, Mexico itself.
Economic disarray, official corruption, an unresolved Indian uprising and several unsolved killings have pushed Mexico to what some here say is the brink of the most serious crisis of state since the 1910 revolution. 
 And all of it has passed through an airy second-floor office at a corner of Los Pinos where, often alone and wearing a well-used cardigan sweater, calmly sits the man at the center of the storm: President Ernesto Zedillo.
The economic and political decisions Mr. Zedillo has made in this office have affected all Mexicans and many Americans. But the one action likely to have the greatest long-term effect, both for Mexico and the United States, and the one that has put Mr. Zedillo in a quandary, is his effort to dismantle the imperial presidency he inherited.
Mr. Zedillo, who came to office as a kind of pinch-runner after his party's original candidate was assassinated in 1994, has refrained from using the almost unlimited powers his predecessors left for him and has tried to share control of the country with Congress, the courts, local governments, opposition political parties and the nearly 100 million people of Mexico in the form of honest elections.
Having just completed his first turbulent year in office, Mr. Zedillo, who turned 44 today, has not yet fully realized any of the goals he has outlined, and he may never do so. But the very process of trying to reach them has been wrenching.
Mr. Zedillo's style of leadership is so unsettling because it contrasts so greatly with Mexico's historic reliance on strongmen, or "caudillos," a tradition dating from the Emperor Montezuma and continuing through the past 66 years when Presidents have served a single nearly omnipotent six-year term and have had a virtual free hand in choosing their successors.
Previous Presidents have ruled over compliant congresses, dependent judiciaries and governors who had virtually no budget but what came from Los Pinos. A nod from the President was enough to launch a career, or end it, and the President and his family have been, until now, considered untouchable. Guards at Los Pinos would warn visitors to move quickly because "El Senor is arriving." "El Senor" refers to the President, and to God.
This is a country so used to having a strongma n lead that what Mr. Zedillo offers as leadership many see as weakness, what he thinks is democracy others see as drifting, what he promises will be a fundamental transformation they fear will become chaos.
 
Stability in Mexico Long a U.S. Goal
Keeping Mexico stable has long been the goal of American policy toward its southern neighbor. And since the powerful presidency has for most of this century meant the preservation of order, the unpredictability created by Mr. Zedillo's handling of his office is closely watched by Washington.
But it is on the streets and in the cities across Mexico that Mr. Zedillo's presidency is questioned most.
"He has the title of President but he's really not President," Emilio Espinoza Barrera, 52, a taxi driver from the central state of Guanajuato, said last month a few days after the Mexican stock exchange and the peso were badly battered because of unfounded rumors that the army had toppled the Zedillo Government.
Like many Mexicans, Mr. Espinoza Barrera has heard Mr. Zedillo pledge democracy and law and order, but he is hard pressed to believe that anything will happen.
"When I was a child in school and had to sing the national anthem, I always felt good about Mexico," he said. "But as an adult I lost that feeling. I realized that all the promises about democracy and fatherland are just a farce."
Others see Mr. Zedillo's odd style as far too lenient and laid back. Adolfo Aguilar Zinser, an independent congressman, said Mr. Zedillo was pulling apart the old system before Congress and the rest of Mexico's public institutions were ready to fill the power vacuum left behind.
"The President has sacrificed his leadership for a 'let it be' position and made himself prisoner of his own jail," Mr. Aguilar Zinser said in an interview on the floor of Congress.
Sweeping political reforms are now being hammered out by the three major political parties without the President's direct input. "Amidst all the tensions, Zedillo is sealing himself off in Los Pinos," Mr. Aguilar Zinser said.
 
Ex-Presidents Sniping From the Sidelines
The events of 1995 turned Mr. Zedillo's first year in office into a nightmare, but he has also had to deal with something no other Mexican President since the 1930's has faced -- interference from his predecessors.
Instead of retiring to raise tomatoes in their backyards, as they would have in years past, two former Presidents, Luis Echeverria and Carlos Salinas de Gortari, have made several critical statements and published openly hostile letters that have shaken the Government. Mr. Salinas even staged a bizarre hunger strike to put pressure on his successor, and is now involved in a corruption scandal with his brother Raul that Mr. Zedillo is having trouble handling.
Such open displays of infighting and scandal are seen by many here as a signal that the old political system by which one party has ruled Mexico for 66 years -- longer than any other single party still in power in the world -- is reaching its end.
Since 1929, the pendulum of power has swung from one sector of the Institutional Revolutionary Party (PRI) to the next without ever alternating to the opposition.
"The party was created as a catchall that counted on members to be united and unanimous," said Santiago Onate Laborde, PRI's president.
What kept the system functioning was Los Pinos and the power it represented. Mexican Presidents serve one six-year term, and are constitutionally barred from a second consecutive term. The Peruvian writer Mario Vargas Llosa called this system "the perfect dictatorship" because it maintains power but limits the powerful.
Until March 1994, Mr. Zedillo was a rising member of the Mexican political caste, an economist with a doctorate from Yale University who was good with numbers but lacking in political fire.
After President Salinas chose Luis Donaldo Colosio as the PRI candidate in 1993, Mr. Colosio picked Mr. Zedillo to be his campaign manager, a move that seemed to insure that Mr. Zedillo would become treasury secretary after Mr. Colosio took office.
But two bullets fired after a campaign rally in Tijuana on March 23, 1994, ended Mr. Colosio's life and the PRI's orderly march to another presidential term.
After a fierce struggle with the party, Mr. Zedillo was chosen to fill in for Mr. Colosio, and he arrived at Los Pinos without having made the long list of compromises and commitments that Presidents from PRI usually carry to their office. His American education, and his upbringing in Mexicali, on the border with California, gave him a clear view of American democracy that many analysts think influences his view of how to govern.
In a land where symbols of power are flashed freely, he has shown a common touch, perhaps typified by his sweater and the Ironman plastic wristwatch he often wears.
At a recent concert in Mexico City, someone noticed him in the presidential box at intermission and started to applaud. In time, most of the well-heeled crowd was clapping. Confused and unsure about what to do, Mr. Zedillo just sat there. Finally he stood up and acknowledged the crowd.
"It is amazing to think that it has turned out to be this unprepared, accidental President who faces the greatest challenge in Mexico since the revolution," the Mexican writer Carlos Fuentes said. "All of us hope he is up to it."
 
A Leadership Style That Unsettles Many
Most Mexicans are simply not comfortable with Mr. Zedillo's style. "We are used to seeing a form of governing in which intimidation by the powerful counts for more than the convictions of the just," said Humberto Roque Villanueva, the PRI president of the lower house of congress. "A certain part of the political class hasn't yet gotten used to Ernesto Zedillo's concept of using the power of the presidency."
Reviewing how Mexico has changed in the last year, it is easy to understand the politicians' sense of estrangement. Take, for example, the advances made by the National Action Party (PAN), a perennial loser that for 50 years has played second fiddle to PRI.
When Mr. Zedillo first put on the red, white and green presidential sash on Dec. 1, 1994, PAN governed 3 states and 118 municipalities in which 13.2 million Mexicans lived -- 16.5 percent of the population. That excludes the 20 million people in Mexico City, who do not elect the Mayor; he is appointed by the President.
Mr. Zedillo vowed to hold fair elections, even though the economic crisis was likely to cost his party dearly. As he ended his first year in office, PAN controlled 4 states and 218 municipalities. That brings to 24.7 million the number of people governed by the opposition, nearly 31 percent of the population, again excluding Mexico City.
"Nobody knows better than the residents and officials of each state, each town, each community their own problems and needs as well as the best ways to tackle them and solve them," Mr. Zedillo said in a typical speech recently in Jalisco state, which in February was swept by the opposition for the first time.
 
An Election Passes Without Conflict
Such sharing of power has not been easy to take for a party used to controlling everything. But the election process itself is being transformed from conflictive brinkmanship based on pressure and compromise to an orderly, though still flawed, example of democracy. Elections in November in the state of Michoacan, the stronghold of the leftist opposition, passed without conflict even though PRI managed to win the governorship.
During Mr. Salinas's six years in office, elections in Michoacan and many other states were all marked by conflict that was resolved only with interference by Los Pinos.
That is not the only way Mr. Zedillo benefits from comparison to his predecessor. Recent disclosures of corruption and illicit enrichment by Mr. Salinas's brother Raul (officials have found more than $100 million in overseas accounts) have enraged Mexicans. But they have also provided a lighting rod that diverts public resentment away from Mr. Zedillo.
Mr. Zedillo has also given Congress a greater role to play. A mutliparty commission is handling peace negotiations with the Indian rebels in Chiapas. In a vote on social security reform that Mr. Zedillo considered critical, some 60 modifications were on the floor of Congress before the bill was approved.
Without a strong President, the rules of the political game in Mexico all change. Under Mr. Zedillo's concept of a new federalism, the traditionally subservient governors of Mexico's 31 states gain power and independence. But with that comes the danger that they may once again become the kind of local bosses that in the past ruled with iron fists.
Already, the Governor of Puebla has tried to ignore federal election laws and the Governor of Tabasco has used Mr. Zedillo's hands-off policy as a pretense for flouting party unity.
Mexico's difficult times are likely to continue into the next century. Social conflict and street demonstrations could increase, especially if the economy does not start to turn around next year. PRI could lose its congressional majority in the 1997 midterm elections. Mr. Zedillo has said he will break with the most fundamental part of the presidential system and not select his successor for the election in 2000.
It is not at all clear that he would be able to do so even if he wanted to. He has not placed potential candidates in his Cabinet, as has been the practice, creating conditions for a political struggle the likes of which Mexico has not seen for more than half a century.
 
A Wobbling System Raises U.S. Stakes
With an increasingly strong opposition, the decomposition of the governing party and the demolition of the presidency by Mr. Zedillo, the Mexican system is wobbling, and that raises the stakes for the United States, whose Mexico policy has long been to maintain stability at all costs.
"We will have to be sophisticated enough to differentiate between the painful process of establishing a real democracy," and the agitation of a society going through a transition, said James R. Jones, the United States Ambassador. "And we will have to be sensitive enough to be willing to zig and zag a little bit on economic policy," so Mexico can ease the social burden of the economic restructuring.
Mr. Onate, the president of PRI, said a Mexico with a democratic balance of power would probably not be as predictable as the old pyramid of power, with Los Pinos at the top. "The only honest answer is yes," there will be more for the United States to be uncomfortable about, he said, because "democracy is not certainty."

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Philippine Airlines Gives Big Order to Boeing

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

DATELINE: MANILA, Dec. 27

 BODY:
The Boeing Company, continuing to win business in the quickly expanding Asian market, received an order from Philippine Airlines for eight 747's and is expected to win part of a $4 billion order to be placed by Malaysia's national carrier.
Jaime Bautista, chief financial officer for Philippine Airlines, said today that the airline planned to buy eight 747-400's, Boeing's largest long-distance aircraft, as part of a program to spend $2.7 billion to purchase 32 new aircraft from Boeing and Airbus Industrie in the next three years. 
 Mr. Bautista declined to say how much Boeing and Airbus would receive from the orders, which are expected to reach $3 billion when repairs and service are included. A 747-400, which can carry about 400 passengers, costs about $150 million.
The Philippine carrier will buy 4 of the A340-300's, 8 of the A330-300's and 12 of the A320's from Airbus, Mr. Bautista said.
A spokeswoman for Malaysian Airline System said today that the carrier would announce after the New Year the recipients of a $4 billion order for 25 new aircraft. The New Straits Times, a Malaysian newspaper, quoted aviation executives as saying that Boeing would receive more than $2 billion, mostly for 747-400's.
Boeing, based in Seattle, is the world's largest maker of commercial aircraft. The company has been competing with Airbus, which is a consortium of European companies, and the McDonnell Douglas Corporation of St. Louis to supply aircraft to carriers in Asia, where rapid economic growth has caused a boom in travel.
Singapore Airlines said last month that it would buy as many as 77 Boeing jets for up to $12.7 billion. China Airlines, Taiwan's top airline, said last week that it would purchase as many as 15 Boeing 737-800's for up to $750 million.
Airbus said last month that Cathay Pacific Airlines of Hong Kong had agreed to buy 2 more A330's, bringing to 11 the number of orders placed by the carrier for the planes, which sell for about $113 million apiece.
Philippine Airlines had been discussing an expansion and modernization of its fleet for some time. The resolution of a battle for control of the carrier last week cleared the way for the orders to Boeing and Airbus.
Government financial institutions agreed on Friday to let the carrier's chairman, Lucio Tan, a beer and tobacco magnate, obtain a majority stake in Philippine Airlines. An agreement with the financial institutions, which hold 48 percent of the airline, to reduce their ownership stakes must still be completed before the Boeing and Airbus orders can proceed, Mr. Bautista of Philippine Airlines said.

HEADLINE: Sales of Existing Homes Fall For 2d Consecutive Month

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 27

 BODY:
Sales of existing homes fell for a second consecutive month in November, an industry group said today, as a slowing economy reduced confidence about future job security.
The group, the National Association of Realtors, said resales of single-family homes dropped 1.7 percent in November, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.04 million units, despite lower mortgage rates. 
 Sales of previously owned homes declined a revised 1 percent in October, to an annual rate of 4.11 million; previously, October sales were reported to have fallen a steeper 1.9 percent. That followed five consecutive months of rising sales from May through September.
Analysts said that even after two months of declines, sales remained at a relatively healthy pace, in part because lower interest rates have made it easier for first-time homeowners to qualify for loans.
The realtors' group said the average interest rate for a 30-year mortgage loan in November was 7.38 percent. That was down from 7.48 percent in October and was well below the rate of 9.16 percent in November 1994.
November sales of existing homes were 9.5 percent above those in the corresponding month a year earlier, when they were running at an annual rate of 3.69 million.
But some buoyancy has clearly left the housing market, a development that was reflected in a separate survey yesterday by the Conference Board, a business research group based in New York.
The group said its consumer confidence index fell to 98.7 in December from a revised 101.6 in November. The index, based on a sample from 5,000 households, is calculated on a base of 100.
Edgar Fiedler, vice president and economic counselor at the Conference Board, noted that the index had hovered around the 100 mark throughout the year.
"The traditionally warm spirits of the holiday season, however, have not lifted the economic expectations of consumers above this moderately favorable level," he added.
After a strong third quarter in which the gross domestic product rose at a vigorous 4.2 percent annual rate, the economy has been on a slowing trend that is forecast to continue into 1996.
That is casting a shadow over future prospects, as shown in lackluster Christmas sales. The most recent figures for retail sales, published today by Mitsubishi Bank-Schroder Wertheim, showed a modest surge of 1.4 percent in sales at chain stores in the week that ended Dec. 23.
Although no official measure of fourth-quarter G.D.P. is available because of the continuing Government shutdown, most analysts anticipate an expansion of only about 2 percent.
"The crux of the matter at this point really is job insecurity," said Sung Won Sohn, an economist for the Norwest Corporation in Minneapolis. "Consumers are not sure about their job prospects in 1996 and consequently they're not really willing to spend a lot of money."
Art Godi, president of the realtors' group, said, "The closing of the Federal Government, coupled with the onset of the holiday spending season, may have caused some reluctance among consumers to get into the housing market last month."
Separately, the Labor Department said it would be forced to delay reports on December prices and employment that were originally scheduled for release in early January.
"At this point those things have been delayed," said Bill Barron, deputy commissioner at the Bureau of Labor Statistics, citing the partial shutdown of the Government because of the budget impasse.
Although the data for the December economic reports were collected before the shutdown, the furlough of Government workers has led to delays in the processing of the information, Labor Department officials have said.
The December employment and unemployment data were originally scheduled for release on Jan. 5. The report on the Producer Price Index was to be released on Jan. 11 and the Consumer Price Index report was scheduled for Jan. 12.

HEADLINE: Canadian Agency Is Accused of Spying on Citizens and Allies

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH 

DATELINE: OTTAWA

 BODY:
It looks like any other nondescript Government building, in a city full of them, until you notice that the nine-foot-high fence surrounding it is topped with barbed wire and there is only one way to get in -- through what looks like a guardhouse.
The four-story Sir Leonard Tilley Building, named after an obscure 19th-century Canadian Finance Minister, houses Canada's most secretive intelligence agency, the Communications Security Establishment, a remnant of the dusky world of cold-war electronic espionage. 
 The agency, the branch of the Defense Department that gathers signal intelligence, used to attract little public notice. But now, after much unwanted publicity from disgruntled former employees who accuse it of spying on Canadian citizens and Canada's allies, the agency finds itself the focal point of debate about intelligence gathering in Canadian society.
Bill Seward, 50, the mechanic at Petro-Canada's Hoggs Back Service Center a couple of blocks away, gives vent to some of the new concern about the agency, which intercepts foreign phone, radio, fax and other communications. "I think they're overstepping their authority," he said, "and it would probably be better if they kept their hands off."
Jane Shorten, 38, worked for the agency as an intelligence analyst from 1986 to 1994, when she was laid off in a belt-tightening move.
Recently she touched off minor international incidents after disclosing in a television interview that Canada had spied on friendly countries like South Korea and Mexico.
One of her projects gathered economic, military and security intelligence on South Korea. With high-powered electronic listening devices, she eavesdropped on secret discussions among South Korean officials about purchasing nuclear reactors.
"Anything having to do with the South Korean diplomatic corps was of interest," she told the CTV television network. She also monitored communications from the Mexican Embassy during the negotiations that led to the North American Free Trade Agreement among Canada, Mexico and the United States.
The Mexican Government presented a diplomatic note to Canada expressing "surprise and concern." The South Korean Foreign Ministry said it would take "appropriate measures" after an investigation.
Although the Soviet Union and its allies were once the prime targets of surveillance, after the cold war the Communications Security Establishment has shifted its attention to economic espionage in friendly countries, Ms. Shorten said.
Ottawa says it will look into the new allegations. After reports this year that the United States Central Intelligence Agency had spied on the Japanese delegation during auto trade talks, Solicitor General Herbert Gray was asked whether Canada carried out similar operations.
"Countries like Canada have understandings that they don't carry out these activities against each other," he told Parliament in October.
Interception of private communications in Canada is illegal. The agency says it targets only foreign communications, but a gray area apparently exists with Canadians working for foreign embassies. Ms. Shorten expressed dismay at having to make it the Government's business to eavesdrop on one Canadian employee of a foreign embassy in discussions with her gynecologist.
Deputy Prime Minister Sheila Copps promised an official review of the Shorten allegation, adding, "If, in fact, there has been illegal activity going on, we'll want to deal with that in as public a way as possible."

HEADLINE: Pension Board to Pay $100 Million for Failed Plans

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 27

 BODY:
The Federal agency that guarantees many private pension plans settled the largest class-action lawsuit ever brought against it today, agreeing to pay nearly $100 million to 40,000 retirees who were covered by pension plans that failed.
Today's settlement by the agency, the Pension Benefit Guaranty Corporation, resolves cases involving 11,000 failed pension funds that imposed stricter rules than allowed by Federal law for the number of workers qualifying for benefits. The pension agency had previously made payments only to workers who met the very strict standards set by these pension funds. 
 But the agency has now agreed to make back payments with interest to workers who met the Federal standard but not their employers' rules.
The decision delighted Mary E. Collins, a 70-year-old retiree in Medford, Mass., who was a plaintiff in the lawsuit and stands to receive up to $50,000. "This is really a lifesaver," she said.
Martin Slate, the pension agency's executive director, accepted today's settlement. "It is our job to protect their pensions," he said in a statement.
But the settlement puts a further burden on the Pension Benefit Guaranty Corporation. The agency already lies at the center of a political battle over the extent to which American companies should set aside money now for the eventual retirement of their workers, particularly workers in the baby boom generation.
The Republican budget bill would have allowed companies to remove billions of dollars from pension funds that hold at least 25 percent more money than their estimated future payments to retirees. President Clinton vetoed the budget bill, and White House and Congressional officials have not yet agreed on a new bill.
The pension agency had accumulated a deficit of $1.2 billion by the end of last year, the most recent figure available. Companies with defined-benefit pension plans support the agency by paying insurance premiums, but there have been growing worries in Congress that the Federal Government might someday have to bail out the agency if the economy enters another recession and many companies go bankrupt.
A defined-benefit pension plan makes fixed, regular payments to retirees, using money typically contributed by their employer and managed by professionals. By contrast, workers put their own money into defined-contribution pension plans like 401(k) plans, although companies may make matching contributions. The payout depends on how adeptly the employee manages the money over the years.
The Pension Benefit Guaranty Corporation insures most of the private sector defined-benefit pension plans in the United States.
Today's settlement is subject to approval by the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, after which the pension agency would have 38 months to find the beneficiaries and pay them. Court approval in such cases is common but not assured.
The settlement results mainly from a change in Federal law that took effect at the beginning of 1976.
Until then, many companies' pension plans had rules requiring many years of service before employees qualified for benefits. The new law required the inclusion of employees who had worked for 10 or more years, a so-called vesting requirement that has since been reduced to five years.
Many companies and other employers were slow to change their rules after the new law took effect, and there were a variety of regulations in effect at the time that allowed them to do so in many cases. About 11,000 underfunded pension plans terminated from 1976 through 1981 without bringing their rules into compliance with the new law, and today's settlement covers these plans.
Stephen R. Bruce, one of the lawyers who brought the cases resulting in today's settlement, said that the settlement calls for the pension agency to make about $70 million in payments to the retirees. The pension agency will also spend up to $24 million to pay for commercial companies to track down the retirees, he said.
Retirees who think they may qualify for payments can check by calling (800) 316-8857, a number maintained by the lawyers who filed the case.
The settlement also calls for Mr. Bruce and the other lawyers on the case to receive various legal fees, which are still under discussion. Mr. Bruce predicted that the lawyers would ask for fees of up to 12.5 percent of the payments to retirees, or nearly $9 million. The pension agency will pay up to $2 million of these fees and the retirees will pay the rest, he said.
Ms. Collins said that her employer, the M&M Transportation Company, a trucking business in Cambridge, Mass., had filed for bankruptcy protection from its creditors in 1977. She was 52 years old then and had spent 33 years with the company, but did not receive a pension because the company did not award pensions to employees who left before the age of 55, she said tonight in an interview from her home.
Ms. Collins had accrued a monthly benefit of $309, and received none of it. Retirees with smaller monthly benefits, or who received partial payments, could get considerably less money than Ms. Collins, Mr. Bruce said.

HEADLINE: Party Leader May Signal Shift in Japan

BYLINE: By NICHOLAS D. KRISTOF 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Dec. 27

 BODY:
Ichiro Ozawa, a highly unusual Japanese politician who calls for a virtual revolution in his nation's politics, economics, society and foreign policy, was elected leader of Japan's main opposition party today.
Mr. Ozawa, who would almost certainly put relations with the United States on a new footing, thus moves one step closer to the Prime Minister's office. 
 His platform calls for sweeping deregulation in almost every sector, a far-reaching restructuring of the educational system and much greater willingness to use military force.
Still, it is far from clear that Mr. Ozawa will become Prime Minister after the next general election is called. And even if he were to become Prime Minister, it is uncertain how much impact he would have.
In any case, though, his rise to lead the main opposition party, the New Frontier Party, underscores a generational transition toward politicians who aim to be leaders as well as Prime Ministers. Mr. Ozawa and others of his generation -- he is 53 -- are far more self-confident than those who have ruled Japan for 50 years.
"I will fight to my last breath, with all my power, for the New Frontier Party and for Japan," a beaming Mr. Ozawa told a victory celebration of supporters who cheered him with shouts of "Banzai!" meaning "10,000 years," or "long life."
If Mr. Ozawa were to become Prime Minister, there would inevitably be some strains in relations with Washington, for his vision is of Japan as a full partner of the United States rather than as its caddie. Yet Mr. Ozawa also favors the kind of broad deregulation in Japan that Americans have yearned for.
Moreover, Mr. Ozawa places enormous emphasis on military cooperation, and he would be far more willing than any other Japanese politician to back up United States forces in a conflict with North Korea or another country.
The election of Mr. Ozawa means that the next Prime Minister is likely to be either he or Ryutaro Hashimoto, the leader of the Liberal Democratic Party, Japan's biggest political group. Mr. Hashimoto is also a new-style politician, unusually assertive and ready for Japan to exercise power, although he does not favor the same kind of sweeping changes.
Mr. Hashimoto today called Mr. Ozawa "a superb politician" and said, "I look forward to being able now to hold a fair and aboveboard debate on the issues."
That was a dig at Mr. Ozawa's reputation as a king-maker, forging back-room deals without stepping forward to engage in public debate. One reason Mr. Ozawa is not more popular is that he is widely seen as embodying precisely the tradition of behind-the-scenes machinations that he says he is determined to end.
The party election process broke new ground. Normally it is primarily members of Parliament who choose a party's leader. But the New Frontier Party allowed any Japanese over 18 to vote for a $10 contribution, and 1.7 million people voted.
A general election must be held by July 1997. Mr. Ozawa has a reputation as a brilliant campaign strategist -- he masterminded his party's stunningly good performance in July elections for the upper house of Parliament -- and the next election will be held under a new system of single-seat constituencies that make the outcome difficult to predict.
Mr. Ozawa faces two other obstacles: his health and his prickly personality. He has had heart problems, although it is not clear how severe they are now. What is clear is that he is a difficult person to work with, often as prone to pummel egos as to stroke them.
A broader challenge would be how to prevail over an entrenched bureaucracy. Two years ago another bold young politician, Morihiro Hosokawa, became Prime Minister and talked of far-reaching change. He lasted less than a year as Prime Minister and left little mark on the nation.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Ichiro Ozawa was presented with flowers after his political victory. (Reuters)

HEADLINE: Stocks Mixed in Slow Day Amid Hope for a Rate Cut

BYLINE: By LEONARD SLOANE 

 BODY:
Stocks were mixed yesterday in thin trading, amid continuing expectations that the Federal Reserve would again reduce interest rates.
The Dow Jones industrial average, which at one point gained more than 16 points, ended down 4.34 points, at 5,105.92. The Nasdaq composite index dropped 1.24 points, to 1,048.13, but the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index inched up 0.23 point, to 614.53. 
 "It was a fairly directionless day," said Hugh A. Johnson, senior vice president of the First Albany Corporation. "The trading was largely tax driven."
Reports yesterday of declines in the Conference Board's consumer confidence index and in the National Association of Realtors's tally of home resales underlined the signs of economic slowdown in recent months. These announcements bolstered the bond market, where the bellwether 30-year Treasury bond jumped almost half of a percentage point in price and its yield fell to 6 percent.
"This was somewhat encouraging to the stock market," Mr. Johnson said. "The strength in bonds prevented stocks from going into a swoon."
Advancing issues outpaced declines on the New York Stock Exchange, by 1,328 to 985. Big Board volume climbed to 252.3 million shares from the unusually slow 217 million shares on Tuesday.
Some sectors, notably finance, rose on the anticipation of lower rates. When rates dip, lenders usually benefit from a greater spread between what they receive from their assets and pay for their liabilities.
Federal National Mortgage surged 5 1/4, to 125, after declaring a 4-for-1 stock split and saying that it will sell $1 billion of preferred to repurchase common shares. PNC advanced 1 3/8, to 31 1/2; Bankers Trust increased 1 1/4, to 67 1/2; and First Chicago rose 1 5/8, to 39 7/8.
Tobacco stocks, which typically move higher in a slowing economy, finished on the upside yesterday. Philip Morris closed at 90 3/4, up 1 1/4; UST Inc. closed at 34 3/4, up1/8, and American Brands closed at 44 3/4, up 1/8.
However, the drug stocks were led downward as traders rotated away from the group. Merck declined 1 3/4, to 64 7/8; Eli Lilly fell 1 1/4, to 55 1/4; and Schering-Plough dropped 5/8, to 54 7/8.
A broad range of economically sensitive technology stocks also moved lower. Included were semiconductor stocks like Intel, down 1/2, to 58 7/8, software companies like Microsoft, off 1 3/8, to 88 7/8, and computer systems like Compaq, down 1 1/8, to 48 3/4.
Aluminum stocks were weak, apparently because of worries that demand will fall in 1996. Alcoa slid 1 3/4, to 49 3/4 and Reynolds Metals lost 1, to 53.
Among the special situations, Cordis slumped 11 1/4, or 11 percent, to 95 1/4, after Johnson & Johnson extended the review period for its planned $1.8 billion acquisition of the company.
Safety First, which projected a fourth-quarter loss, dropped 4 1/8, to 15 1/8. And Ventritex declined 2, to 18 3/4, after J. P. Morgan Securites projected a loss for the company of $1.80 a share in the fiscal year ending next June.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Long-Bond Yield at 6%, A 2-Year Low

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
The yield on the Treasury's bellwether 30-year bond dipped briefly below 6 percent yesterday, then closed at an even 6 percent, its lowest in more than two years, as the market rallied for a fourth consecutive day on favorable economic data and investor optimism that a Federal budget agreement was attainable.
Some market participants have said that as long as President Clinton and Republican leaders are still talking, there is a chance that the deadlock will be resolved in the near term. The market's price rise was limited, however, by bouts of profit taking. 
 The 30-year bond rose 15/32 , to a price of 112. The yield of 6 percent was down from 6.03 percent on Tuesday and was the lowest since Oct. 29, 1993, when it was 5.97 percent.
Donald H. Straszheim, chief economist at Merrill Lynch & Company, said that he thought a budget agreement was coming but that he did not expect a breakthrough from the scheduled talks on Friday.
"The process has been ugly, but we remain confident that a deal is coming and will put the Government on a fundamentally more conservative fiscal path," he said. "That will be -- despite all the concern and questions -- good news for the debt and equity markets, good news for the dollar and good news for the economy."
Few economic statistics have been available from Washington since the start of the partial Government shutdown, but the market rose yesterday after a report from a private source. The National Association of Realtors said yesterday that existing-home sales fell for the second consecutive month in November, suggesting that declining mortgage rates have not been enough to overcome the concerns of potential buyers about the slowing economy.
Thomas E. Donne, vice president and a senior portfolio manager at the Banc One Investment Advisers Corporation in Columbus, Ohio, said that while recent economic news had been relatively positive for the bond market, he would not read too much into the gains yesterday, given the thin volume.
"Most everyone, including the fund managers running sizable amounts of money through the market, set their positions for the rest of the year almost a week ago," Mr. Donne said. "As a result, there was very little trading in the long maturities, with some buying and selling in the intermediate and shorter maturities.
"Getting below the 6 percent yield on the long bond is not something I think is sustainable," he added. "But I think that there is a sense in the market that the additional information it is looking for will not be long in coming. And the sooner the Government statisticians are back to work, the sooner we will get that information."
For now, Mr. Donne said, his trading position is "on the long side of neutral," meaning that he could make a modest commitment to invest in more government securities, depending on market conditions.
Bill Gamba, senior vice president in charge of the government trading desk at Cowen & Company, predicted that yields would fall further once there was a budget agreement. "The market is dying to do better, with the direction of interest rates headed down," he said.
"What we need now is a credible budget deal to bring about a comfort level to entice fresh buying," he added, "and that will give the market the ability to maintain levels under 6 percent in the long end."
In futures trading, the March Treasury bond contract hit 12031/32 , matching its Dec. 8 high, Mr. Gamba noted. The low was 12010/32 , but that was in overnight trading. At the close, the contract was 12023/32 , up 13/32 on the day.
"I think any decline in the market can now be used as a buying opportunity," he said.
Stewart Taylor, a fixed-income market analyst at Thomson Research of Boston, said last week's bond rally brought prices back to levels of early December. "I suspect that the rally is a retest of those early December highs, but I do not expect any significant activity until after the new year," he said.

HEADLINE: A Shaky House of Plastic With No Quick Fix in Sight

BYLINE: By SAUL HANSELL 

 BODY:
Jeanne Hill has a good heart, but to her credit card companies, she's simply "a bad."
"When my dog came down with lymphoma, I maxed out two credit cards on chemotherapy trying to save her," said Ms. Hill, 54, who lives in San Francisco. 
 The dog didn't make it, but the $2,000 in veterinary bills pushed Ms. Hill's total debt to $12,000. When she lost her job as a legal secretary and developed knee problems, her $900 monthly card payment overwhelmed the $1,188 she was receiving from disability insurance.
Thus Jeanne Hill became, in the cruel language of the credit card industry, "a bad" -- someone who, for whatever reason, doesn't pay back what he or she borrowed.
The card companies have learned to assume they will have 2 or 3 bads for every 100 cards they issue -- more in a recession -- and they charge high enough interest rates to the 97 "goods" to cover their expected losses, as dutiful bill payers will gloomily note in the course of paying off holiday shopping charges.
But now the number of bads is on the rise -- along with credit card delinquency rates and personal bankruptcies -- even though the economy continues to expand.
The recent surge in credit card delinquencies -- in part because it is concentrated among relatively few people -- does not pose any immediate threat to the economic expansion. But experts warn that credit standards have become so lax in recent years that when the economy does get bad, losses for banks -- and the level of insurmountable debt for many consumers -- will be far more than ever before. Indeed, Lawrence B. Lindsay, a member of the Federal Reserve Board, recently warned that Americans' growing bad debt was already a "troubling" sign.
Credit card companies will send out nearly 2.7 billion pitches this year, with most households receiving three or four of them a month, according to Behavioral Analysis, a research firm in Tarrytown, N.Y. With response rates to these mailings falling rapidly, the companies are seeking customers who would have been seen as too risky before. And they are trying to steal each other's good customers with lower rates, higher credit limits and invitations to transfer balances to new cards -- offers that boomerang for the lender when they are accepted by someone who turns out to be a bad.
Consumers are taking advantage of the easy credit and low rates to borrow more than ever before. Americans ran up card balances by 19 percent this year, to $305 billion. That is after a 28 percent increase in card debt last year. For the first time this year, banks offered more gold cards, which carry a minimum $5,000 limit, than standard cards.
The sheer pace of this growth raises the question of whether credit card lending is following in the checkered tradition of loans to third world countries and speculative real estate developers.
"Over time, I've learned to look for the speculation, where people are feeling too good, because that's where the problems come," said Gary Gordon, a credit card company analyst at Paine Webber. "Unsecured credit card lending right now has the most activity and the fastest growth."
By that measure, the biggest question in the industry is the quality of the huge portfolios built by four specialized companies -- Advanta, Capital One, First USA and MBNA -- that have doubled their share of the credit card market to 20 percent in just three years. First USA will mail as many credit card offers this year as Citibank, long the industry leader.
In particular, Advanta, Capital One and First USA have won business by combining low teaser rates with sophisticated computer models intended to identify prospective good and bad customers, based not only on their credit history but hundreds of other factors such as the magazines they subscribe to and the cars they drive.
Richard Greenawalt, president of the Advanta Corporation, argues that the company's years of experience with these models will help protect Advanta from credit problems. Capital One and First USA executives declined to comment.
Mr. Greenawalt, echoing the concern of many bank stock analysts, wonders whether the regional banks that have more recently stepped up their credit card mailings without the extensive data bases may be more vulnerable.
"Not everyone knows what they are doing, and some people will get hurt," he said. "There are thousands of details in what we do that are hard to copy."
Yet even the specialized companies -- like their customers -- are living closer to the edge than ever before. They bolster profits from their relatively low-rate offers by using an unusually high amount of debt relative to their equity. This leverage could pose trouble if they are wrong about their computer models.
Even with the economy in its current state, which is far from a recession, credit counselors say the number of consumers asking for help with card bills has hit record highs.
"Normally, December is a slow month for us, because people want to make it through Christmas before facing their debts," said Joanne Budde, executive director of the Consumer Credit Counseling Service of San Francisco. "But we've been busier this December than any month this year. I'm increasing my staff by 40 percent to handle things when the Christmas bills come in."
Jeanne Hill was one of those who sought a hand this month from the service, which helped her negotiate lower interest rates and a payment schedule she could afford. Many other people in similar circumstances are simply choosing to declare bankruptcy, which accounts for nearly half of all bank credit card losses.
Personal bankruptcies are up sharply this year, and in the third quarter 4.2 percent of credit card balances were more than 30 days late. That is up from 2.9 percent a year ago.
To be sure, many credit card executives argue that the rise in delinquencies and bankruptcies is simply a return to normal. That would be expected, they say, after a period of brisk economic growth accompanied by low rates that allowed homeowners to ease their payment burdens by refinancing mortgages.
Yet some bankers see a new type of danger that is not evident in the statistics. With most card issuers mailing blank checks to consumers urging them to transfer balances from other cards, consumers are being encouraged to play a shell game with their debt. And the banks no longer have any early signal that customers are in trouble.
There is a sharp rise in what bankers call "straights," consumers who never missed a payment, then go straight to bankruptcy.
"It's like being hit with a nuclear weapon you didn't know had been launched," said Seamus McMahon, a credit card expert with the First Manhattan Consulting Group.
As a result, some banks, including Chase Manhattan, First Chicago and the Bank of New York, have tightened their credit standards. And Norwest, the huge Minneapolis banking company, completely abandoned offering credit cards in direct mail solicitations, after its losses shot up.
Bankers are split as to whether they should feel any responsibility for their customers' problems.
"There may be a group of people we are tipping over because we have been so aggressive in balance transfers," said Ronald N. Zebeck, head of the credit card unit at the Fingerhut catalogue company.
But Michael Auriemma, an industry consultant, is unmoved. "There are people who go to the supermarket and buy too much food and get fat," he argued. "It's not the supermarket's fault."
Still, there are signs that the card issuers may well have taken a strategy intended to avoid bad credit and pushed it too far.
When the specialized card companies like First USA began offering free gold cards with low teaser rates about five years ago, they stole millions of the best customers from big banks like Citibank, a unit of Citicorp, and Chase, which were so occupied by their real estate problems that they could not afford to lower their rates. But now, with low-rate offers common, many worry that the same strategy is getting a disproportionate number of the worst customers.
"The day you get fired is the day you accept every card offer you get," said Alan D. Shiffers, a former Citibank executive who is now a consultant.
It is a perilous environment in which to market. The most sophisticated companies use their data bases to divide prospects into hundreds of distinct segments, each offered a different combination of interest rate, credit line and marketing pitch. If any of these variables is wrong, the consequences could be a deluge of bads and not enough goods.
"If I offer a 10 percent rate, and all my competitors offer 9 percent, I will get killed on credit no matter how good my underwriting," said Aviad Broshi, the head of credit policy for Chase's credit card unit, because only those most in need will take the 10 percent card in addition to the 9 percent card. Similarly a test by Chase showed that in otherwise identical card offers, one that charged a fee had three times the number of bads as one that was free.
To get out of this trap, card companies are trying to cull the bads from their account rolls before the losses mount. This is difficult because for all the data in their computer models, the companies do not know whom many of their customers work for or how much they earn; since most cards come from "pre-approved" mailings, many customers never fill out applications.
The big card companies have started getting credit bureau reports on all of their customers every few months to look for people who have suddenly taken on a lot more debt or are missing payments to others.
To the annoyance of some of these customers, the companies are lowering credit lines, imposing fees or raising rates on those who appear to be risky.
These moves may cut some losses, but they will not erase mistakes of the past.
"If you didn't do something to tighten your credit last year, it's over," said John Ward, head of credit cards at Chase. "The tidal wave is forming, and if you aren't already on dry land, you are going to drown no matter how fast you swim."

HEADLINE: Essay;
Office Pool, 1996

BYLINE: By WILLIAM SAFIRE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
Only two picks right last year, a record for the 22-year history of this much-imitated pool; now let's go for a clean sweep.
1. Whitewater will (a) spawn a scathing Senate report titled "Doubting Thomases"; (b) be a fizzle with no new independent counsel convictions; (c) reveal petty crimes in Arkansas and serious wrongdoing in Washington; (d) be concluded by a post-election Presidential pardon of Web Hubbell and any others indicted or convicted, except Hillary. 
 2. The economy will (a) sail serenely onward and upward; (b) run into recession-deflation fears and 10 percent market drop; (c) grow under 2 percent for three quarters, then boom and break 6000 on the Dow.
3. Surprise political best seller will be (a) "First Do No Harm," by Laurence O'Donnell Jr.; (b) "The Angry American," by Susan Tolchin; (c) "Why I am a Democrat," by Ted Sorensen; (d) "Ain't You Glad You Joined the Republicans?" by John Calvin Batchelor.
4. Dividing the Republican Convention will be (a) aborting the abortion plank; (b) renouncing affirmative action; (c) going nativist on immigration; (d) term limits; (e) ban on assault weapons.
5. Political stunner of the year will be (a) abandonment of G.O.P. by women; (b) abandonment of Dems by blacks; (c) stay-home revolt of the angry white geezers; (d) waning of third-party movement.
6. Longest lines on a celebrity book tour will be for (a) Hillary Clinton's "Politics and Parenting"; (b) Princess Di's "Windsors and Losers: My Battle Royal"; (c.) O. J. Simpson's "Confessions."
7. Nobel Peace Prize will go to (a) Irish peacemakers; (b) Peres and Assad; (c) Warren Christopher; (d) Wei Jingsheng in jail.
8. Hollywood will blunt cultural criticism by Academy Awarding (a) Jane Austen's and Emma Thompson's "Sense and Sensibility"; (b) Barry Sonnenfeld's "Get Shorty"; (c) Edward Burns's "The Brothers McMullen"; (d) Oliver Stone's emotionally true "How J.F.K., L.B.J. and Nixon Did In Vince Foster."
9. Republican ticket will be (a) Dole and Whitman; (b) Dole and Powell; (c) Dole and Nunn; (d) Alexander and Lugar.
10. Biggest '96 election upset will be in (a) Russia, where the Communists won't win; (b) Israel, where Peres won't win; (c) America, where Clinton won't win.
11. In Congressional races, (a) the mellowing of Speaker Gingrich will result in the retention of a G.O.P. House majority; (b) the demonization of Gingrich will cost Republicans the House; (c) a Clinton sweep will take the House and narrow the Republican Senate majority; (d) a Dole triumph will result in the first one-party government since the glory days of the Carter Administration.
12. Comeback Kid of the year will be (a) Mario Cuomo; (b) Heath Shuler; (c) Hazel O'Leary; (d) Rudy Boschwitz.
13. The Supreme Court will (a) not apply the racial "strict scrutiny" standard to state-supported military schools that exclude women; (b) strike down the Colorado amendment prohibiting localities from enacting gay-rights statutes; (c) end gerrymandering of voting districts that discriminates against white voters; (d) turn down New York City's attempt to adjust Federal census to increase power of urban areas; (e) bid farewell to Chief Justice Rehnquist in the event of a G.O.P. Presidential victory.
14. The C.I.A. revelation will be (a) the cost of Ames to our operations in Italy, France and Latin America; (b) the discovery of the Second Mole high up in C.I.A. by the F.B.I.; (c) our losing war with French intelligence; (d) the Agency's secret victories over terrorists from Iran.
15. By year-end in Bosnia, (a) peacekeeping will be judged a success, with NATO strengthened and bipartisan U.S. leadership hailed; (b) guerrillas will harass American troops, leading to ignominious withdrawal and resurgence of isolationism; (c) withdrawal takes place with fingers crossed, as balanced Balkan forces prepare to resume fighting.
My picks: 1-c, 2-b, 3-b, 4-a, 5-d, 6-b, 7-d, 8-a, 9-b, 10-all, 11-d, 12-d, 13-all, 14-b, 15-a.
Winners of the Office Pool will be given the opportunity to invest in any of three newly issued debentures: the Gingrich bond (no maturity), the Dole bond (no interest), or the Clinton bond (no principal).

SECTION: Section A; Page 20; Column 3; Editorial Desk

HEADLINE: In New York, Local Management of Welfare Is Democratic

 BODY:
To the Editor:
As a county executive and the president of the New York State Association of Counties, I must address your Dec. 19 editorial opposing Gov. George Pataki's block-grant proposal to grant counties and the City of New York more authority to manage welfare and Medicaid programs. 
 You argued that local governments, which are closest to the people served by these programs and cared for New York's neediest citizens long before the state government entered the social services arena, should not be entrusted with more responsibility. Such a view implies that local voters cannot be trusted to elect local officials with enough wisdom and compassion to meet the needs of their own residents, and that only bureaucrats in Albany and Washington can determine the appropriate level of welfare benefits for each community.
The Governor's proposals allow counties to begin crafting programs that fit specific local needs, including the need to make social services more cost-effective. This is not some "misguided reform principle." It is democracy at work. It is built on the fundamental assumption, borne out by experience, that local elected officials are responsive to the needs of their constituents.
The concern that "counties will be tempted to cut poverty programs, including Medicaid coverage, and pass along the savings . . . to businesses and families" is likewise misplaced. In New York we spend more than twice the national average on Medicaid. We also spend more than almost all other states on welfare. Given flexibility from state and Federal mandates, counties and New York City can reduce welfare and Medicaid spending while still meeting the needs of their residents.
Perhaps it is advisable to rein in excessive spending and direct more tax relief toward working middle-class families, and the businesses that employ them, so that they can afford to stay in New York. Shouldn't New York State and local governments both try to break out of the economic death spiral of ever higher taxes to pay for ever higher spending, which leads to more businesses and families fleeing our state, and places even more pressure on the few hardiest taxpayers who remain?
Governor Pataki, a former mayor whose roots are in local government, has grasped the essential principle that the best government derives its authority from those it governs. His trust in local government is well placed.
 
ROBERT J. GAFFNEY
Suffolk County Executive
Riverhead, L.I., Dec. 22, 1995

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Time Warner Seeks Action on Ameritech

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: COLUMBUS, Ohio, Dec. 27

 BODY:
Time Warner Inc. has asked Ohio regulators to intervene in its negotiations with the Ameritech Corporation over plans to offer a competing local telephone service.
Ameritech, the regional Bell company that provides local phone service for much of the Great Lakes area, is either unable or unwilling to reach an agreement to provide connections on calls from Time Warner's proposed network, Time Warner said Tuesday evening. 
 "While the parties have clearly reached agreements on all technical issues necessary for interconnection, the same cannot be said with respect to the economic aspects of interconnection," the company said in a letter to Craig Glazer, chairman of the Public Utilities Commission of Ohio.
An Ameritech spokeswoman, Anne Bloomberg, said this morning that the company was surprised by Time Warner's complaint.
"We certainly thought the negotiations were going well," she said, noting that the sides met as recently as late last week. "We thought all issues were on the table. Suddenly, Time Warner raised some new ones."
Time Warner filed an application last year with the Public Utilities Commission of Ohio to offer local telephone service in 37 of Ohio's 88 counties.
The company was granted a provisional certificate in August by the commission to proceed, but first it must reach interconnection agreements with Ameritech and other Ohio phone companies so customers can call anywhere, regardless of which company they use.

The New York Times

SECTION: Section D; Page 6; Column 4; Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 1246 words

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Boston Scientific Cheered by Market

BYLINE: By GLENN RIFKIN 

DATELINE: NATICK, Mass.

 BODY:
Even in the midst of the current mergers and acquisitions frenzy in corporate America, the Boston Scientific Corporation has raised eyebrows with its voracious appetite this last year and a half.
The company, a maker of medical devices, has swallowed five significant competitors since August 1994, in stock swaps totaling $2.08 billion. The hypergrowth strategy has caused revenues to more than double, from $450 million in 1994 to more than $1 billion this year; meanwhile, the company's work force has leaped from 1,800 a year ago to more than 7,000 by year's end. 
 But if Wall Street is worried that Boston Scientific has bitten off more than it can chew, it doesn't show it. [The company's stock has climbed steadily through the year from a low of $16.75 a share to $47.125 at Wednesday's close.] Earnings per share have jumped from 81 cents in 1994 to $1.24 this year.
From its start in 1979, Boston Scientific has been a bet on medical care evolving toward the sort of cost consciousness that has fully taken hold only in the last few years.
Most analysts rate the stock a strong buy, saying that Boston Scientific is building a powerhouse in its markets for minimally invasive medical devices -- catheters used for treatment of coronary, gastrointestinal and urological diseases.
The company's founders, Peter M. Nicholas and John Abele, expected that the high financial and physical cost of major surgery would lead the medical community to embrace tools for the least damaging and dangerous methods of diagnosis and care.
Viewing the human body as a series of "biologic cylinders" that could be explored, purged and otherwise repaired using catheter-based devices like balloons poked through the skin, Boston Scientific developed products that quickly became standards among physicians. The beauty of the products is, for example, in angioplasty, in which doctors insert a catheter with a balloon fitted to the end that is inflated and used to clear clogged arteries. An alternative treatment might require something as drastic as open heart surgery.
"Using these devices allowed a physician to perform intervention much earlier in the disease cycle, with lower cost and lower trauma," said Mr. Nicholas, the company's chairman, chief executive and president. "The alternative, which was further down the disease cycle, was major surgery, which was often worse than the disease itself."
With the company's sudden growth over the last year, Mr. Nicholas said his biggest worry was losing the "richness and crispness" of Boston Scientific's culture, a culture characterized by risk-taking highly devoted employees, many of whom hold large financial stakes in the company's future through generous stock options.
Mr. Nicholas is optimistic that the integration of the new companies will be smooth. And analysts, though wary, say if any company can pull it off, Boston Scientific can.
"It's a pretty daunting task," said Thomas Gunderson, a medical technology analyst with Piper Jaffray in Minneapolis. "Can they juggle five balls? Nobody has been able to do it yet. But they are one of the best managed companies I know of. They have a lot of capacity and are very attentive to details."
The kind of growth undertaken by Boston Scientific reflects what Mr. Nicholas believes will be required for a company to survive and thrive in health care. With the accelerating consolidation of hospitals and other health care providers, purchasers of medical devices suddenly have tremendous economic clout, he said.
And where the decision-maker was once the physician, today's buyer tends to be a corporate finance officer or purchasing executive.
"For us to be effective, we need to have the broadest product line within each of our businesses," Mr. Nicholas said. "We also need a leading share of the market; we have to be the low-cost, high-quality producer and we need access to and control of technology that will impact our business in the future."
In acquiring Scimed Life Systems Inc., Heart Technology, Cardiovascular Imaging Systems Inc., Meadox Medicals and EP Technologies, Boston Scientific calculated that it was, in effect, buying product lines rather than companies. Scimed, for example, sells a broad line of angioplasty catheters. Heart Technologies makes a catheter with a football-shaped burr at the end that spins like a router device, at 190,000 rotations a minute, to clear out plaque from arteries.
As hospitals merge or are swallowed up by chains, the pressure has increased for device manufacturers to consolidate as well, making it tougher for small players to compete. For example, Johnson & Johnson's initially hostile takeover of Cordis, another catheter maker, is likely to mean more aggressive pricing strategies in the industry. Companies like Seattle-based Heart Technologies, Mr. Nicholas said, had a single high-quality product line "which made them vulnerable; they couldn't survive alone, so now they become a product line in our company."
Attention to detail has characterized Boston Scientific's management. Frederick Wise, an analyst with Bear, Stearns, lauded Mr. Nicholas and Mr. Abele for their ability to work together: Mr. Nicholas commanding the strategic business and marketing side and Mr. Abele tending to the technical needs of the health care market.
"They are a very rare demonstration of a co-chairmanship that has worked for 16 years," Mr. Wise said.
Now, the pair will be tested beyond anything they have yet experienced.
"They're a good company but they are at a very critical stage, moving from a moderate-sized company to a big company," said Dr. Robert Z. Gussin, corporate vice president of science and technology at the rival Johnson & Johnson. "To assimilate all those businesses and move as quickly as they have in the past will be difficult. The jury is still out."
Moreover, a changing regulatory and business environment threatens Boston Scientific's continued commitment to risk-taking and leading- edge research, according to Dr. Jeffrey M. Isner, chief of cardiovascular research at St. Elizabeth's Hospital in Boston and a longtime customer.
"They have a lot of factors in health care reimbursement working against them," Dr. Isner said.
He cites the company's willingness to build catheters for cardiovascular human gene therapy. "They are the only ones willing to do that, so the risk-taking instincts are still alive there," Dr. Isner said. "But will the market allow them to do that a year or two from now?"
In its formative years, Boston Scientific earned a reputation In the medical community for forging close ties to physicians. Mr. Abele, whom many in the industry call a visionary, became a fixture at hospitals and medical gatherings, soaking up new ideas and seeking input from doctors. "John is a great tinkerer, constantly talking to anybody who had an idea," Dr. Isner said.
Boston Scientific had quietly remained private and richly profitable until 1992, when it reluctantly went public to pay off a 10-year-old financial obligation to Abbott Labs, which had bought 20 percent of the company in 1982.
Now, Boston Scientific is squarely on the Wall Street radar screen.
"Over the next 10 years, you won't see any field of medicine grow as fast as minimally invasive procedures," said Dean Kamen, president of Deka, a Manchester, N.H., medical products research and engineering firm. "Boston Scientific is very well positioned in all those areas."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Boston Scientific's founders and co-chairmen, Peter M. Nicholas, left, and John Abele, see expansion as necessary in the new health market. (Michael Quan for The New York Times)
 
Chart: "Aggressive Moves" lists the five companies Boston Scientific has acquired in the last 18 months.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Gingrich Won't Compromise On Reopening Government
The House Speaker, Newt Gingrich, on the eve of scheduled negotiations with President Clinton to balance the budget, said that until an accord was reached, Republicans would not agree to any temporary measures to reopen shuttered Government offices.
In another disagreement with the Democrats, Mr. Gingrich and Bob Dole, the Senate majority leader, expressed unhappiness with a proposal to overhaul communications laws, contending that several provisions amounted to a giveaway of a crucial part of the airwaves to broadcasters. Last week it appeared that the White House and Republican leaders were in accord on rewriting the 61-year-old laws. [Page A1.]
 
Compuserve Censors Access To Groups Violating German Law
Compuserve has blocked access by its subscribers around the world to more than 200 sexually explicit computer discussion groups and picture data bases, because a German Federal prosecutor in Munich said the material violated German pornography laws.
The action, the most far-reaching instance yet of Internet censorship, underscores the extent to which diverse national, cultural and political values are coming into conflict with the essentially borderless technology of the global Internet computer network. Compuserve, a unit of H & R Block, described its move as temporary while it studies its legal options. [A1.]
 
Rate on Long Bond Below 6%
The interest rate on 30-year Treasury bonds closed at less than 6 percent for the first time in more than two years, reflecting a growing view that the economy was much weaker than previously thought, and will remain slack into the Presidential election year. [A1.]
 
The price of the Treasury's bellwether long bond rose 11/32 , to 11211/32 , for a yield of 5.98 percent, down from 6 percent on Wednesday, the lowest since Oct. 29, 1993. [D13.]
 
Head of CBS News Is Leaving
Eric W. Ober confirmed that he was being replaced as president of CBS News, and would leave on Jan. 12, after five years in the job. His replacement was expected to be Andrew Heyward, the 45-year-old executive producer of the "CBS Evening News." [D4.]
 
Dean Witter and Paine Webber?
Wall Street brokers and bankers were chattering about the possibility that Dean Witter, Discover had its eye on the Paine Webber Group -- a merger that would create the largest retail brokerage house in the United States. But some analysts who follow the situation carefully gave it less than a 50 percent chance of happening. [D2.]
 
Cirrus Stock Tumbles 26%
Cirrus Logic shares fell $7, to $19.875, after the maker of audio and video chips said for the second time that earnings would not meet expectations. Investors feared that profit could be hurt for several quarters. [D3.]
 
Isis Ends Work on Genetic Drug
Isis Pharmaceuticals called off work on an unusual genetic drug that was the first of a new class of compounds, known as "antisense molecules," ever to be injected into humans. While holding promise, the molecules have proved difficult to turn into drugs. But Isis is continuing work on similar drugs, which are now in clinical trial. [D2.]
 
Echostar's Stock
Up With Satellite
Echostar Communications launched its first satellite, capping a nervous couple of months for the company's management. The satellite will offer customers 75 channels of entertainment by means of an 18-inch home satellite dish. Investors pushed Echostar's stock up $3.375, to $24. Market Place. [D6.]
 
Stocks Drift Lower
The stock market drifted lower in slow and somewhat aimless trading. The Dow Jones industrial average fell 10.12 points, to 5,095.80, with such big blue chips as I.B.M., Walt Disney and Philip Morris declining. The Nasdaq composite index dropped 5.91 points, to 1,042.22. [D6.]
 
Natural Gas Futures Slide
Surging natural gas futures prices reversed course, tumbling on forecasts for milder Northeastern weather. [D5.]

LENGTH: 511 words

HEADLINE: Stocks Drift Lower in Another Slow Day

BYLINE: By LEONARD SLOANE 

 BODY:
The stock market drifted lower yesterday in slow and somewhat aimless trading.
"The day was quiescent," said Edwin D. Walczak, chief investment officer of Vontobel U.S.A., describing the inactivity among traders. "You could call it a day of gestation in anticipation of vacation." 
 Even the bond market's rally did not have much of an impact on stocks. The yield of the 30-year Treasury bond, which moves in the opposite direction of its price, slipped to 5.98 percent, marking the first time it ended at les than 6 percent since October 1993.
The Dow Jones industrial average fell 10.12 points, to 5,095.80, for its second consecutive decline. The major blue-chip losers were I.B.M., down 1 7/8, to 90 1/8; Walt Disney, down 7/8, to 59 3/8; and Philip Morris, down 7/8, to 89 7/8. Among the broader indicators, the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index eased 0.41 point to 614.12 and the Nasdaq composite index dropped 5.91 points, to 1,042.22.
But advancing issues outnumbered declining ones on the New York Stock Exchange by 1,249 to 1,088. Turnover on the Big Board amounted to 288.7 million shares, up from 252.3 million shares on Wednesday, but sharply below the three-month daily average of around 371 million shares.
Technology stocks stumbled yesterday, led by Cirrus Logic, which plunged 7, to 19 7/8. On Wednesday, the computer chip maker trimmed its profits forecast for the second time in two months because of reduced demand in the fiscal third quarter.
This warning depressed the stock prices of other semiconductor manufacturers and pulled other technology issues down as well. Intel fell 1 3/4, to 57 1/8; Motorola dropped 1/2, to 56 7/8, and Micron Technology lost 1 1/2, to 40 1/4.
Airline shares fell after major carriers announced a new round of fare reductions, and that weighed on the Dow Jones transportation average, which slumped 5.98 points to 1,953.49. UAL, the parent of United Airlines, fell 2 5/8, to 174 3/4; Delta lost 5/8, to 72 1/8; AMR, the parent of American Airlines, slipped 3/8, to 73 1/4, and USAir closed down 1/4, at 13 1/8.
After Michaels Stores said that it expected to report significantly lower profits for the quarter ending on Jan. 31, its shares dropped 5/8, to 14 3/8. Other retailers that ended the day at a lower level were Home Depot, off 1/2, to 47 1/4, and Fingerhut, off 1/4, to 14 3/8.
In contrast, some utilities advanced and the Dow Jones utilities average gained 0.70 points, to 224.57. American Water Works rose 1 1/8, to 38; Brooklyn Union added 1/8, to 28 3/4; and Duke Power increased 1/8, to 47 1/2.
Selected insurance stocks, considered defensive issues that perform well in periods of slow economic growth, also turned higher. Reliastar closed at 41 3/4, up 7/8; Lincoln National closed at 53 1/8, up 5/8; and Horace Mann closed at 30 5/8, up 1/2.
Wells Fargo, which has made an offer to acquire First Interstate, advanced 2 3/4, to 216 5/8. But Federal National Mortgage, which surged 5 1/4 on Wednesday, gave up 1 1/4, to close at 123 3/4 yesterday on profit-taking.
