SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 3; Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 1184 words

HEADLINE: ON WALL ST., SIGNS OF SLOW GROWTH FOR THE ECONOMY

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE 

 BODY:
The interest rate on long-term Treasury bonds closed at less than 6 percent yesterday for the first time in more than two years, reflecting a growing view that the economy is much weaker than most experts thought just weeks ago and will remain slack into the Presidential election year.
What is notable is that no one is challenging this view of Wall Street's traders, who buy and sell huge quantities of securities, manipulating the nation's interest rates in the process. They contend that a budget deal will soon emerge that shrinks the deficit, and a smaller deficit, combined with mild inflation and data signaling weaker growth, all justify lower rates. 
 But almost no one is predicting a recession, which would be a huge obstacle to the re-election of President Clinton. So far, not even the most pessimistic of the 50 prominent forecasters polled each month by Blue Chip Economic Indicators see a recession in 1996.
The Administration's view, offered over the last two weeks by various officials, is essentially this: Economic growth is stronger in some quarters of the year than in others and right now we are going through one of the weaker periods. "But there is no fundamental weakness in this economy," an Administration official said yesterday. "Hopefully the economy will be a positive for the election campaign."
Certainly investors are bullish. With markets rising sharply in value, they put more money into mutual funds in November than in any month since January 1994, a trade group said yesterday. [Page D1.]
Then the economy was booming, largely in response to rates almost as low as they are today. Not since October 1993 has the 30-year Treasury bond's rate -- or more accurately the amount of money the bond earns, or yields, for investors -- slipped to less than 6 percent. The yield fell to 5.98 percent yesterday from 6 percent on Wednesday and 6.11 percent 10 days ago. [Page D13.]
Wall Street traders, economists, and officials at the Federal Reserve -- the other big player in setting interest rates -- all point to a different set of circumstances from that of two years ago to explain the steady decline in rates without a corresponding pickup in the economy.
Low rates on Treasury securities in 1993 translated into the lowest rates in years for mortgages and car loans. Many homeowners refinanced mortgages, freeing billions of dollars for consumer spending that otherwise would have gone into their house payments. Home construction and car sales, helped along by low rates, also recovered smartly from the 1990-91 recession. None of that is happening now.
"You have to get rates lower than they were two years ago in order to get the same response," Lawrence H. Meyer, an economist and consultant in St. Louis, said. "Most of the people who might have acted on rates at today's levels did so in 1993. You need to go down another one-half to one full percentage point to draw in people again and get the same sort of refinancing and spending."
Falling rates on Treasury securities -- and they have fallen this week all along the spectrum, from 90-day bills to 30-year bonds -- eventually show up as lower rates on home equity loans and commercial loans, as well as lower rates on mortgages and auto credit. All that is happening, and these consumer rates might in fact get lower than they were in 1993, led down by further declines in the rates for Treasury securities.
Since late September, the 30-year Treasury bond has been the only Treasury security trading above 6 percent. But that rate began to crack on Dec. 19 when the Federal Reserve decided to lower a key short-term rate that it controls -- the Federal funds rate -- by one-quarter of a percentage point. Bond traders interpreted that move as signaling the strong possibility of further cuts in the months ahead.
The Federal funds rate governs short-term loans that banks and other financial institutions make to each other. What they pay for money governs, in turn, what they charge for loans.
"Basically, the market is of the view that the Federal funds rate is going to continue to decline, and it is going to stay at a new lower level for a good while," said William Dudley, a senior economist at Goldman Sachs & Company.
Unexpectedly weak Christmas sales seemed to ratify the Fed's move, in the eyes of bond traders. So have big stockpiles of unsold merchandise, weakness in industrial production and capital spending, and a drop in inflation for November -- once again confirming a widely held view that inflation is not likely to pick up soon. All suggest that the economy needs less-expensive credit to stimulate spending. And Fed officials have done nothing to discourage this view.
"The most reasonable prediction is for near-term growth very close to trend," Alan Blinder, the Fed's vice chairman, said yesterday, suggesting optimistically that the Christmas season downswing in the economy was temporary and that economic growth would soon return to an annual rate of 2.5 percent, if it was not already there now. "But the risks," he warned, "are on the downside."
Deficit reduction appears to be playing a big role for the moment in encouraging bond traders to keep rates low, and thus avoid the risk of a recession. These traders are notorious for their swings in sentiment -- and sentiment has clearly swung to the view that the Administration and the Republican congressional leadership will reach an agreement that will shrink the deficit.
"One way or another, the deficit is going to be cut," said Peter L. Bernstein, an economist and market analyst in New York. "That is the reality; the argument is over the details, not the process."
Such thinking puts pressure on the budget negotiators to reach an agreement. If they fail, dashing the current expectations on Wall Street, interest rates could rise in response.
Bond traders are not usually optimistic about deficit reduction and the lower interest rates that smaller deficits encourage. Nor are they given to warning about weaknesses in the economy. Their interest lies much more in warning about strong growth and the increased inflation that such growth can engender. Their customers, the people who hold trillions of dollars in bonds, stand to lose money when inflation picks up. That reduces the buying power of the money these investors have locked up in bonds and similar Treasury securities. In anticipation of this danger, Wall Street traders soon demand higher interest rates to offset this loss.
Given this pressure, these traders rarely see weakness in the economy turning into a recession. Much more often than not, they say that the economy has hit a temporary lull and that strong growth lies ahead. This lull is no exception.
"When we get to midyear, the economy will turn out to be in good shape," said Stephen S. Roach, senior economist at Morgan Stanley & Company, echoing a view that showed up in half a dozen interviews with traders and Wall Street economists. "As we get to midyear, wage pressures are going to start to rise and the inflation rate will begin to rise."

HEADLINE: BATTLE OVER THE BUDGET: THE DETAILS;
Showdown and Shutdown: The Basics Over the Budget

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 28

 BODY:
It seems irrational. Some Government offices are closed, but many more are open. Some Government workers have been furloughed, but they have been promised they will be paid for not working. Others are working at full pay. Still others are at work but will draw only half a paycheck this week.
Congress, meanwhile, is in recess. Many top Administration officials are on vacation. President Clinton is planning his annual New Year's weekend in South Carolina. Speaker Newt Gingrich says no break in the deadlock over the Federal budget is possible until the middle of next week at the earliest. 
 The plot is so convoluted, the developments are so incremental and the characters seem so agitated when they speak that even avid followers of the political script often have trouble figuring out what is going on and who is at fault. If you are confused and want to know the basics about the budget conflict, here they are:
 
 The Shutdown
Six annual spending bills have not been enacted -- that is, have not been passed by Congress and signed by the President, or have not been passed over his veto. They hold the money to operate nine Cabinet departments and many other Government agencies in the fiscal year that began on Oct. 1.
In the departments and agencies without funds, only employees designated essential are on the job, although in some offices that is all or most of the work force.
The White House and the Capitol are open, and their employees working because laws providing their funds have been enacted. The same is true of the Departments of Defense, the Treasury and Transportation.
Three bills that have not been enacted -- those for the District of Columbia, foreign operations and the Departments of Labor, Education, and Health and Human Services -- are stuck in Congress for one reason or another.
Three other annual spending bills have been vetoed by the President: one for the Departments of Commerce, Justice and State and the Federal judiciary, another for the Interior Department and the third for the Department of Veterans Affairs, the Department of Housing and Urban Development and agencies like the Environmental Protection Agency that are not part of any Cabinet department.
The President has also vetoed the vast long-term bill that is the keystone of the Republican Congressional majority's plan to balance the budget in seven years. This legislation would limit spending for Medicare, end the 60-year-old Government guarantee of Federal assistance to the poor, turn over Medicaid and most other welfare programs to the states, scrap many existing farm programs and cut taxes for middle-income families with children and for investors.
The Republican majority in Congress is much too small to override the vetoes.
When annual spending bills have not become law by the beginning of the fiscal year, Congress routinely approves a temporary measure called a continuing resolution that keeps the departments and agencies functioning until legislation providing their funds can be enacted. That has already happened twice this fall.
But Republicans are using the current Government shutdown as leverage to pressure Mr. Clinton into signing their long-term budget bill. If the budget bill does not become law, much of their revolution will be thwarted: taxes will not be cut, and benefit programs like Medicare will continue without change.
For some Federal workers, the effect of the partial shutdown will be felt in paychecks on Friday. In offices without funds, even those who have been working cannot be paid for the days since the shutdown began. But because the shutdown started midway through many employees' pay period, these employees -- whether working or ordered not to work -- will draw one week's pay in what would normally be a two-week paycheck.
Congressional leaders promise that any pay lost during this period of turmoil will be restored once the impasse is eventually broken.
 
The Negotiations
Before the 1996 fiscal year began on Oct. 1, Congress passed and the President signed a continuing resolution extending the Government's spending authority through Nov. 13. When that measure expired, the first partial shutdown began. It lasted for six days until, on a Sunday night, negotiators from the White House and Congress struck a deal on a new temporary spending resolution, to last through Dec. 15.
The agreement that reopened the Government was intentionally ambiguous. Both sides agreed to work on legislation to balance the Federal budget by 2002 as measured by the calculations of the Congressional Budget Office. They also agreed to "provide adequate funding" for Medicare, Medicaid, education and other programs the Clinton Administration favors.
Since then, the two sides have done little but shadowbox. Republicans have argued that by not using the Congressional Budget Office's economic assumptions in drawing up his own latest plan to balance the budget in seven years, the President reneged on his part of the agreement. The President has blamed Republicans, declaring that they failed to offer enough money for programs like Medicare.
On Dec. 19, four days into the latest shutdown, President Clinton and the Congressional leaders -- Mr. Gingrich and Senator Bob Dole -- seemed to reach an accord to open the Government through the holidays.
But at a televised news conference, Vice President Al Gore appeared to contradict the Republican account of the accord, which was that the President had agreed to negotiate, by the end of the year, a balanced budget based on Congressional Budget Office calculations. It is not clear whether Mr. Gore misspoke or was intentionally trying to throw sand in the gears.
In any case, on Dec. 20 Republicans in the House scuttled the agreement reached the night before. They maintained that the Vice President's statement had made clear that the White House would not negotiate in good faith, although this may have been mostly an excuse to justify their hard line.
The President and the Republicans who control the Senate favor reopening the Government while the political dispute is being resolved, but they have been unable to do so because of the rigid position taken in the House.
Occasional talks have gone on at various levels since then, but nothing of substance has been accomplished. Congress is basically in recess until Jan. 3, and Leon E. Panetta, the White House chief of staff and main Administration budget negotiator, has been on vacation.
The President and Congressional leaders are supposed to meet on Friday. But as best anyone on the outside can tell, they are no closer to resolving their differences than they were when the fiscal year began three months ago.
 
The Issues
The President insists on preserving Medicare for the elderly and the social safety net for the poor. Congressional leaders demand a balanced budget in seven years. If all that were at stake was the amount of money separating them, they could certainly split the difference and be done with it.
But beneath the political oratory, the impasse is grounded in one fundamental, possibly insurmountable disagreement. The Republicans want to diminish the importance of the Federal Government in American life. The President does not, and, in some instances, would expand the Government's role.
Thus, the Republicans want not only to restrain spending but to cut taxes as well. They want to convert Medicare eventually to a voucher system in which the Government would give retirees money with which to purchase their own health insurance. They would also give the states primary responsibility for Medicaid and cash assistance, limit Federal contributions to other programs for the poor like food stamps, cut spending for education, weaken a variety of environmental safeguards, permit much more commercial development and use of Government land, cut Government research programs and give the Government less say about what farmers produce.
President Clinton opposes all those policies. He favors only a modest tax cut. He would basically maintain Medicare and Medicaid as they are now, and he has promised to veto a welfare bill already approved by Congress. In the case of education, environmental protection and technological development, he supports a substantial expansion of the Federal Government's spending and influence.
Listing the basic disagreements understates the extent of the divide. In each area, there are dozens of specific policy differences between the two sides. In the case of Medicare, to take just one example, they range from major issues like how much premiums should be increased to relatively minor ones like the payment for administering blood-clotting factor to hemophiliacs.
The Politics
Politics is usually like a sporting event in which one team is ahead and the other is trying to catch up. The budget impasse is one of the rare instances when both sides believe they have the political edge.
Republicans, especially those in the House, believe they were elected into the majority last year because they had promised fundamental changes in the ways of Washington. They see compromise and deal-making as elements of the old ways that the public was fed up with. As long as they stand fast, they believe, they will come out ahead politically, not just in the long term but in next year's elections as well.
The President and his Democratic allies have returned to the well that has sustained the party for half a century. Most Republicans opposed Social Security in the 1930's and Medicare in the 1960's, and Democrats have never let them forget it. As they have countless times in the past, Democrats have positioned themselves this year squarely on the side of the elderly.
In next year's election campaign, expect each side not so much to defend its own stand as to attack the other's.
Republicans will describe Democrats as supporting ever higher taxes and opposing a balanced budget because they cannot control their appetite for profligate spending. Democrats will picture Republicans as calloused fat cats who want to gut Medicare for the elderly and the disabled to pay for tax cuts for the affluent.
The election results may well turn on which side can make the charge stick.
Public opinion polls show that the President's standing has improved as the budget war has dragged on. But poll results are notoriously ephemeral. The dominant public view in December 1995 could well change by January, to say nothing of November.
 
The Outlook
Here are three safe bets:
*The Government will stay partly closed at least until next Wednesday, when Congress returns from its holiday.
*Sooner or later, Government offices will reopen, furloughed workers will be paid for their days off, and those whose pay has been docked will be made whole.
*When that happens, each side will blame the other for the shutdown and the inconveniences it entailed.
Beyond that, little can be predicted with confidence.
Behind the scenes at the White House and in Congress, some strategists favor compromise to resolve the budget differences. Others, as in a game of chicken, favor sitting tight and waiting for the opposition to swerve.
Even if both sides decide that compromise is the best course, it could take weeks to sort out the myriad issues that divide them. The best that can probably be hoped for in the short run is that the White House and Congress will agree to a basic framework and use that as the basis for putting the Government back to work while the meat and the muscle are put on the skeleton.
For the long term, it is hard to imagine Republicans relenting on their demand for policies that can be shown on paper to lead to a balanced budget in seven years. It is equally difficult to imagine the President relinquishing his hole card: a basically unchanged Medicare system.
If neither side blinks, the President and Congress may be able to find some way for the Government to operate in 1996 while all the issues are fought out on the campaign trail. But how they can do that is anyone's guess.

HEADLINE: Foreigners Flock to Slopes to Work, Not Ski

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE 

DATELINE: SILVERTHORNE, Colo. Dec. 22

 BODY:
Snowshoes adorned the ski chalet wall, a snow-clad Rocky Mountain peak dominated the bay window, and Sidy Ba paused in his cleaning chores to talk about the mountains back home.
"In Mauritania, we have mountains, too, but it's the desert, the Sahara," the 32-year housekeeper said, his body lithe and his French precarious, since he rarely used his second language during a nomadic youth spent herding goats along the Senegal River in West Africa. 
 West Africans in the Rockies are part of the rapidly changing face of the ski resort work force as the classic American ski bum, immortalized in movies and magazine covers, becomes scarce. With American college students shunning low-status jobs, and high housing costs scaring away American blue-collar workers, hotels, resorts, and restaurants are increasingly trying to ease an acute labor shortage by hiring immigrants from Africa, Latin America, Europe, and the former Soviet Union.
The new ski resort worker is more likely to be looking for a long-term job that will pay enough to help support the family at home, employers say, and less likely to take up the standard resort offer of a free season pass for the ski slopes.
"Ski?" asked Fatou Toure, a 20-year-old Ivory Coast woman and a charter member of a new group, The Association of Africans in Colorado. "I'm here for a precise goal: to make money. I see all those people slipping around; that's not for me."
Yet even with the influx of immigrant labor, as winter embraces the nation's most popular skiing state, Colorado's mountain resorts are still struggling to fill openings for chambermaids, janitors, sales clerks and other low-status jobs.
Scarce and expensive housing in the resort areas may be partially to blame. But some employers say the problem is caused by the confluence of long-term trends: Jobs in the ski industry have increased while there are about the same number of American college students, who have traditionally filled those jobs.
Also, employers say, those students have lost interest in these jobs, even if the perks include free skiing time. The limited number of students who do want to work in resorts find they can nearly always choose from among better paying, cleaner jobs.
"Generally, the American college kid doesn't want to write home and say he is cleaning toilets," said John W. Reilly, personnel manager for Ralston Resorts Inc.
So employers are looking to workers like Sidy Ba to fill the gap.
"In the 1970's, the labor market was Denver, in the 1980's, it was national, and in the 1990's, it went international," said Jim Felton, a spokesman for Ralston, whose resorts include Arapahoe Basin, Breckenridge and Keystone. Mr. Felton estimated that almost 10 percent of the workers at those areas were foreigners, including European ski instructors, Australian parking attendants and Costa Rican maids.
Gregorievich Harut, a 49-year-old former judo trainer from southern Russia, works in the laundry at the Hampton Inn here.
"We're from Russia, Belarus, Lithuania, Ukraine," Mr. Harut said. "We're all educated people -- a psychologist, a teacher, an economist, a lawyer."
In recent years, the United States Department of Labor has certified local shortages of ski instructors and motel janitors, allowing Colorado resorts to import workers on temporary visas. This season, for the first time, one Colorado resort won permission to import nannies, lift attendants and restaurant workers.
Because of the shortage of workers, mountain resorts have taken to a variety of tactics to court those looking for jobs.
"The Best Place to Work!" one ski area, Copper Mountain, implores from one of three pages of help-wanted advertisements in today's Summit Daily News, a newspaper here. Breckenridge, a competitor, woos prospective workers with a list of "incredible benefits" that includes "a free ski pass for eight mountains (that's 4,144 acres) of world class skiing." And the 7-Eleven chain offers workers at their store here and in Vail "a $1/hr. bonus for employees who work the entire ski season."
"Applicants interview us," groaned Barbara LeRoy, who largely hires Eastern European housekeepers at the Hampton Inn here.
Despite a month of job fairs and heavy fall advertising in college newspapers, the ski season has started with about 10 percent of Colorado's 86,000 ski industry jobs unfilled, said John Frew, president of Colorado Ski Country USA, a resort group. "All the resorts are looking for employees," he said.
In Colorado ski towns, wages generally start at $7 an hour, almost double the national minimum wage of $4.25. In Idaho, another major skiing state, one resort has raised all salaries by 35 percent in four years.
But Lalo Delgado, a counselor in Denver for Mexican immigrant workers, said, "The problem in the resort areas is not the salaries -- it's that there are no houses."
Describing the housing outside Aspen for Mexican workers, Sheriff Bob Braudis of Pitkin County said, "We have visited trailers that are wall-to-wall mattresses.
"People tell me, 'Bob, you've got to clean up the valley.' I tell them: 'Who cooked your breakfast this morning? Who cleaned your toilet?' "
In Silverthorne, one classified advertisement for a ski shop worker starts simply: "Housing available."
To ease the housing shortage, the Roman Catholic diocese has built low-income housing and has urged resorts to house their own employees. Here in Silverthorne, for example, Keystone and Arapahoe provide housing for 1,100 employees -- about one-third of their combined peak season total.
Some Coloradans say greed has created a situation in which workers cannot afford to live in the communities they serve.
"Before they search overseas for new janitors, ski resorts first should mop up their own mess at home," Mark Obmascik, The Denver Post's environmental columnist, wrote recently. "Resorts created a problem when they sold million-dollar trophy homes around the base of their ski slopes but neglected to provide basic shelter for the laborers who actually make the resorts run."
A casualty of these economic forces has been the growing shortage of American college students interested in skiing, parties and work, usually in that order.
"For the standard ski bum, skiing is top of the list -- 'four or more, and out the door,' " said Mr. Reilly of the Ralston Resorts, repeating the adage about ski resort workers who vanish after four inches of new snow blankets the slopes.
In contrast, the new workers from Africa and other southern climates spurn free ski lessons for a second job.
"We have a lot of relatives in Africa who don't have anything," said Mr. Ba, who cleans rooms at Keystone during the day, then moonlights at a Wendy's restaurant. "We can't live the life of America. We can't go to nightclubs, go to restaurants, go to basketball games. We eat together. We send money home."
All the West Africans and Eastern Europeans who were interviewed said that they had legal documentation to work in the United States.
But the Denver office of the Immigration and Naturalization Service estimates that the number of illegal aliens in Colorado has grown to about 22,000 today, from 17,500 three years ago. The Federal agency has 17 field agents to control illegal immigration in this state.
California's recent moves to strip illegal aliens of social service benefits have diverted some Mexicans to labor-short Colorado, said Joe Greene, district director of the Denver office of the Immigration Service. The number of Mexican workers in the resort towns increases by thousands yearly, according to social service workers.
Facing an influx of illegal workers, the agency last summer conducted a workshop in Vail to teach mountain employers about forgeries of Social Security cards and about the fines of up to $10,000 they can face for knowingly hiring illegal aliens.
Earlier this month, the agency followed up on its word, conducting raids throughout Vail and Aspen. Forty-five illegal workers were detained at 12 businesses, largely construction companies and restaurants. The workers, all Mexicans, were deported.
"This will not be the only visit we make up there this season," Mr. Greene promised from Denver.
Meanwhile, in the lee of the 12,777-foot-high peak of Buffalo Mountain, Silverthorne's fledgling community of about 50 West Africans is gingerly coming to grips with winter sports. Fofana Bakary, a one-time member of the Ivory Coast Olympic boxing team, ventured out recently on a snow board.
"The first time I did, I fell, and it hurt all over," the 29-year-old said in French, before dissolving into Dioula, a West African trading language, to crack jokes with Miss Toure.
Turning to the English he has picked up at the Sav-O-Mat gasoline station here, he said, with the confidence of a proven athlete, "Now, I want to buy a board -- and go fast."

HEADLINE: Answering the March's Call;
More Community Involvement by Black Men

BYLINE: By JONATHAN P. HICKS 

 BODY:
After the march, one man was so moved that he joined a church in Harlem and committed himself to helping its young members. Another decided to organize a group of men to adopt a high school in the Bronx, to instill images of successful black men in students' minds. A teen-ager, shy beforehand, was emboldened to set his sights on becoming an actor.
More than two months have passed since a group of 51 men boarded a chartered Tour Kings bus outside Wells Restaurant in Harlem in the predawn chill of Oct. 16 to attend the Million Man March in Washington. And now that the huge crowd, the passionate speeches and the feeling of camaraderie are just memories, many of the men who rode together on that bus say they are answering, in their own ways, the march's call for black men to take more responsibility for their communities. 
 That single bus contained some laureled men: the columnist Earl Caldwell and Billy E. Jones, the former head of the city's public hospital system, for example. It also carried men of no particular renown, including teachers, government workers, salesmen and students. There was one homeless man.
But interviews with nearly two dozen of the men suggest that they have one thing in common: they have been profoundly affected by their experience on that October day in ways big and small.
"There has been a marked difference in my life," said Jacques A. DeGraff, a 46-year-old head of a Manhattan construction and development company, who chartered the bus. "It has renewed my commitment to some of my existing activities and has led me to take on new challenges."
Specifically, Mr. DeGraff said, he has worked on a program, scheduled to begin in February, under which members of One Hundred Black Men, a civic group he belongs to, will volunteer to serve as mentors for students at Roosevelt High School in the Bronx.
Mr. DeGraff also said he has set up a meeting for January with leaders of One Hundred Black Men and representatives of other black civic and fraternal groups to discuss the need for black foster parents.
"We will introduce everyone to the problems of going through the bureaucracy of becoming foster parents," Mr. DeGraff said. "We want to make sure that we follow up on the commitments we made at the march."
Michael J. Garner, a 35-year-old manager in a city agency, said he was moved by the calls at the march for black men to support black civic and fraternal organizations. In just the last month, he joined the N.A.A.C.P. and began spending three hours each Saturday morning helping high school students in Harlem prepare to take the Scholastic Assessment Test.
"I left there thinking I had to do something more than I was doing,"said Mr. Garner, a former basketball player at the State University in Buffalo.
While it is impossible to say whether the responses of the men on the bus from Harlem reflect the impact the rally made on the rest of the hundreds of thousands who attended, anecdotal evidence suggests that many others also felt changed by the experience.
Leaders of several black fraternities in New York say that since the rally, their members have been clamoring to establish new programs for young people, especially those that would help develop the skills of black teen-age boys. The Urban League in New York reports record numbers of new volunteers, particularly for a program involving juvenile offenders.
The march has been the topic of seminars in high schools throughout the area, and many black churches have sought to channel the energy of the marchers by encouraging men to join their congregations or participate in church volunteer programs.
At the time it took place, the Million Man March was the focus of intense controversy and some criticism because one of the main organizers was Louis Farrakhan, the leader of the Nation of Islam, who has often been assailed for his anti-Semitic comments. But none of the members of the Harlem group who were interviewed belonged to the Nation of Islam before the march, they said, adding that the rally had not persuaded them to join. Officials of the Nation of Islam in Harlem and Chicago, where the group is based, did not return phone calls to discuss how the event had affected their organization.
On the trip back from Washington to Harlem, many men on the chartered Tour Kings bus said their journey had been an eye-opening pilgrimage. One by one, they stood at the front of the bus, clutched the microphone and testified to others about their feelings of renewal.
Many also admitted later that they had silently wondered how long those feelings would last, and of course it is impossible to say what the long-term effects of the march will be. But leaders of a number of civic, fraternal and community organizations say the effect so far is clear.
"I think the march has had impact on segments of the population who want to give something back but who were too busy in their professional lives to take the time," said Dennis Walcott, president of the New York Urban League. "We have been getting a record number of calls from people who have wanted to be volunteers since the march. We believe that it's because of the Million Man March."
There have also been numerous follow-up meetings of men who attended the march throughout the country and in every sector of New York City.
"That march really had an impact on me," said David F. Reeves, a 55-year-old businessman who owns D & J Bookstore in Laurelton, Queens, who has attended follow-up meetings in his area. After returning from the march, Mr. Reeves decided to build a community center in the basement of his store that could be used for job-training programs for unemployed neighborhood men.
"We are building a community center here to house 300 people to develop study groups and job-skills groups," he said. "After the march, I felt a renewed sense of hope. Now, I'm trying to do my part."
Some of the men on the Harlem bus, however, have not joined groups or participated in any new initiatives. Rather, they said, the impact of the march was more deeply personal, affecting their outlook and behavior.
"When I got back, it seemed that I took on a different attitude," said Arthur I. Golden, a 69-year-old resident of St. Albans, Queens, who runs an insurance agency with his wife. Mr. Golden said he had not made up his mind to make the trip to Washington until a few hours before the bus left.
"I've never done anything of that nature in my life," said Mr. Golden, the father of a museum curator and a systems analyst. "My children were proud of me."
But, he added: "Going to that march gave me a different perspective on life. I see a difference in my demeanor."
He said he was so impressed that "there were a million men out there with not a marijuana cigarette or whiskey bottle in sight. It reaffirmed my confidence in the goodness of people. I think I'm a lot calmer. I look at people with more respect."
Anthony D. Montgomery, a sophomore at Hackensack High School in New Jersey, said that he was moved not only by the march, but by the encouragement he got from the men he talked to on the long bus ride. "I got a chance to talk with some people who showed a real interest in me," he said.
"When I thought about all the things these men had accomplished, it made me think seriously about what I wanted to do with my life," he added. "So I decided to become an actor, which is not an easy thing to do. But after the march, I believe that anything is possible if you set a goal and work to achieve it."
Donald Hodo, a 48-year-old accountant who lives in the Bronx, said: "Since the march, I've been affected in a number of ways that are small, but still important. For some reason I now find myself speaking to other black men that pass on the street or at work.
"The march made me feel much more of a sense of unity with others. That may seem small to some, but to me, it's important."

HEADLINE: Russia Warms Up an Old Romance

BYLINE: By STEVEN ERLANGER 

DATELINE: MOSCOW

 BODY:
Stung by perceived humiliations in its relationship with Washington, Russia is pushing a more energetic Asian policy, deepening relations with China, its onetime ally and longtime rival.
Russian officials deny that any kind of joint policy is in the making against the United States, as in the 1950's. They say such a policy now would contradict Moscow's foreign-policy aims and Beijing's vow to avoid new alliances. 
 But China's relations with the United States are much worse than Russia's, and Beijing is using its improving ties with Moscow to strengthen its overall international position, a senior official said. "It's already a fact of life," he said.
For two years now, there has been a growing nationalist, anti-Western mood in Moscow. In the first years after the breakup of the Soviet Union in 1991, said Grigory Loginov, head of the Foreign Ministry's First Asia Department, "Russian diplomacy was accused of leaning too much toward the West." He added, "Now there is an objective balancing."
Yevgeny Bazhanov, deputy director of the Foreign Ministry's academy for training diplomats, thinks China's effort to combine market reform with ideological purity and authoritarianism will produce conflicts with its southern neighbors and with the United States.
"It will naturally push Beijing to seek out strategic partners, the most logical of which is Russia," he wrote recently. China also remains a natural market for Russian technology, machinery and arms.
But Russia's push for warmer ties with China, senior Russian officials say, also stems from deep worries about instability after the death of Deng Xiaoping, China's paramount leader, who is 91. They regard China as an uneasy, prickly neighbor with superpower ambitions.
Russia is an Asian power as much as it is a European one, and if Russian officials are pushed to identify any serious external threat in this confused, post-Soviet period, it is from a China that spins out of control or splits apart.
Faced with predicting whether China will experience a period of stability or chaos after Mr. Deng, a senior Russian Foreign Ministry official said, "It's hard to choose the most probable."
"An unstable China is bad for Russia, of course," the Kremlin official said. But if China lashes out "at some external enemy to try to consolidate the country, we hope it isn't Russia, and we expect it toward the south, in the South China Sea, rather than north."
Unlike the United States, with which China has badly strained relations over issues ranging from trade and Taiwan to human rights and the spread of weapons, Russian and Chinese officials describe their current relationship as good and becoming better.
Beijing thinks Washington wants to stop China from becoming a superpower, the Russians say. Russia takes a more pragmatic position toward Beijing, careful not to interfere in China's internal affairs.
When President Boris N. Yeltsin was hospitalized on Oct. 26, a meeting in Beijing between Mr. Yeltsin and the Chinese President, Jiang Zemin, was postponed, and with it the signing of a treaty setting out the two countries' new relationship.
The meeting was to be the culmination of a series of meetings between senior officials of the two countries that began when Mikhail S. Gorbachev, the last Soviet President, broke the ice in 1989, even as pro-democracy demonstrators occupied Tiananmen Square in Beijing.
Mr. Yeltsin was to have signed, along with Central Asian and Chinese leaders, a major agreement on political relations and nonaggression; to conclude negotiations on mutual troop reductions and pullbacks along the 2,700-mile Chinese-Russian border, and to continue to elaborate detailed confidence-building measures, including better mutual communication, to try to reduce border incidents and tensions.
By September, the two countries agreed to stop aiming nuclear weapons at one another. On Dec. 1, the last border questions were resolved, with Russia ceding about 3,700 acres in the Far East.
A senior diplomat from a Southeast Asian country is more anxious about Russia's temptations. "In the face of NATO expansion, Russia is looking for allies, and that won't be Japan," he said, noting that the Russian Defense Minister, Gen. Pavel Grachev, talks of "allies to the east."
Strong rivalries and suspicions remain between the two countries. But there are also strong economic and domestic reasons for improved relations. As many as 500,000 Chinese live illegally in Russia, despite recent efforts to organize the border with passport and customs controls. Two-way trade, which had reached $8 billion in 1993, dropped to some $5 billion in 1994 as the border market, never very big, became saturated with cheaper Chinese goods. But this year, trade is up again.
China is one of the four biggest markets for Russian arms. The Russians have sold three diesel-powered submarines, 24 SU-27 fighter-bombers and the S-300 antimissile defense system to Beijing.
"There has been no transfer of technology," a senior Russian official said. "We are quite aware that we should be careful, considering all the possibilities for the future."

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Long Bond's Yield Falls Below 6%

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
The yield on the Treasury's bellwether long bond fell below 6 percent for the first time in 26 months yesterday as Treasury securities gained in thin holiday trading.
The price of the 30-year bond rose 11/32 of a percentage point, to 112 11/32 ; its yield, which moves in the opposite direction, dropped to 5.98 percent, down from 6 percent on Wednesday. Yesterday's yield was the lowest since Oct. 29, 1993, when it was at 5.97 percent. It was quite a feat considering that most dealers and investors elected to stay on the sidelines ahead of the year's end. 
 Traders said the non-Government economic data released yesterday had only a mild impact on the market. Trader sentiment was mainly supported by the dollar, which gained against most European currencies on optimism that the Administration and Congress would agree on a budget plan, and by weaker commodity prices.
Some traders are concerned about what they see as an overbought condition of the market and by the inability of the long bond to move below 6 percent in a much more forceful manner. Because so many participants were not actively trading, few players dared bet against the bullish sentiment by shorting the market.
Short sales involve selling borrowed securities, hoping to buy them back later at lower prices, thereby pocketing the difference. But if prices are rising instead of falling, there is little chance of making a profit.
The budget stalemate in Washington has frustrated market participants in recent days, but with talks scheduled for today, cautious optimism has returned. Analysts said, however, that the market remained vulnerable until a deal was done.
Some of the privately generated reports yesterday included the regional business conditions index of the New York arm of the National Purchasing Management's; the index stood at 52.8 in December, down from 55.4 in November. Also, the Conference Board released its November help-wanted advertising index, which fell to 127, from 131 in October.
Conference Board data pointed to growing concern that the economy might slow enough to spark fresh layoffs. It also reported weakness in the industrial core of the American economy.
The impasse between the Administration and Republican leaders over reducing the country's budget deficit has caused a partial shutdown in Government operations. Yesterday was the 13th day of the partial shutdown, and the closings will delay release of several economic indicators of interest to the market, including jobless data and producer and consumer prices.
The Commerce Department has also said that its data on November construction spending, single-family home sales and factory orders will be delayed.
Joseph Battipaglia, managing director of Sterling Advisers, a New York money management firm, said the lack of Government information had led people to make their own forecasts that the economy was limping along. "So when the Government does get back to releasing economic data on a regular basis," he said, "the bond market will have a better sense of where the economy stands. And perhaps it will not be as bad as feared."
Trading was thin early yesterday and lightened further as investors headed toward the end of a holiday-shortened week into today's early close before another three-day weekend.
"The possibility of good news from a balanced-budget program has certainly been priced in," said Mr. Battipaglia, who added that recent signs of slow growth and low inflation had been good news for bonds prices, coupled with the recurring notion that foreign investors will find United States Government securities more attractive than the debt securities of other countries.
"The new year will hopefully start with the release of new economic data," Mr. Battipaglia said. "And the Fed, I believe, is prepared to cut short-term interest rates further." He predicted that during the first half of next year the yield on the long bond could fall as low as 5.5 percent.
James Hale, a fixed-income analyst with MMS International in San Francisco, said, "The best explanation for the steady rise in Treasury prices today, and for that matter, all week, is that no one wants to be the fool who sells right before a budget accord is consummated, in which case they might miss the easiest 6 to 7 points of their trading career."

HEADLINE: In America;
Firing Their Customers

BYLINE: By BOB HERBERT 

 BODY:
The miserable retail sales figures from the holiday season should be taken as an S O S, a distress signal from American consumers who are loaded with debt and deeply insecure about employment.
The quintessential symbol of corporate profits in the 90's is the pink slip. The number of announced layoffs since 1989 will reach three million by New Year's Eve, according to the outplacement firm Challenger, Gray & Christmas. 
 "Downsizing has become the standard operating procedure for many companies today," said John A. Challenger, the firm's executive vice president. "You can no longer count on a job lasting for a long time."
With consumers maxed out on credit, and with many millions unemployed or underemployed, there is a danger that the nation's collective consumer buying power has been seriously compromised. Simply stated, there are dangerously high numbers of Americans with little or no money to spend.
This is a structural problem, not a cyclical one like a recession. It harbors the potential for an economic disaster.
"What these companies have been doing since the mid-1980's is firing their customers," said Eric Greenberg, director of management studies for the American Management Association. He noted that a consumer-driven expansion cannot be sustained when even high-salaried personnel -- professionals, middle managers and technicians -- are being thrown out of work.
Mr. Greenberg's comments echoed those of Henry Ford, who was hardly a champion of the rights of workers but realized that at the very least they had to earn enough to buy his cars.
Jeremy Rifkin, the author of "The End of Work," sees disturbing, even eerie parallels between today's economic situation and that of the 1920's, another period in which enormous numbers of workers were let go because of productivity gains that resulted from improvements in technology. To make up for that loss of buying power, those who remained employed in the so-called Roaring 20's were urged to spend far beyond their means by plunging into the wonderful new world of easy credit.
In the 20's, as now, said Mr. Rifkin, corporate leaders remained blind to the need to share productivity gains with working people. Instead, profits were pocketed and disaster loomed.
Mr. Rifkin, who heads the Foundation on Economic Trends, said business leaders were becoming increasingly concerned about the implications of their wage and employment policies but felt handicapped by the bottom-line needs of their individual corporations.
"They can see, at the macro level, the loss of purchasing power," he said. "But nevertheless, at the micro level, they are rewarded for reducing labor, for putting in the technology and the automatic processes, for improving the bottom line."
Thus the downsizing goes on, with no serious companion strategy (in the corporate world or in government) for creating the kinds of new employment opportunities necessary to maintain the levels of affluence to which so many Americans have become accustomed.
It's not that tough times are ahead; they are already here. The wages, salaries and benefits of American workers rose just 2.7 percent in the year that ended Sept. 30. It was the smallest increase on record and below the rate of inflation. The amount of family income going to creditors is approaching record levels. Credit-card delinquencies are up. Personal bankruptcies are up. And all of this is occurring as the economy, technically, is expanding. What will happen when, inevitably, a recession rolls in?
The bleak Christmas holiday season for retailers was part of a much bigger, much bleaker reality for the retail industry as a whole. At least eight major retailers filed for Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection this year. That is a huge number, and more are expected in 1996.
The economic warnings are coming from many directions, loud, insistent and increasingly dire.
We can heed them now by encouraging business, government and civic leaders to get their act together, to roll up their sleeves and engage these problems seriously, in a spirit of mutual cooperation and responsibility for the common good.
We can do that, which would be extremely tough.
Or we can wait.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

HEADLINE: DESIGN REVIEW;
What New Yorkers Wore When (The Wealthier Ones, Anyway)

BYLINE: By ROBERTA SMITH 

 BODY:
It is always rewarding when one of New York City's lower-profile museums pulls out all the stops, startling the public with a renewed sense of its hidden treasures. Such is the start provided by the Museum of the City of New York with its fashion-as-history exhibition, "Uncommon Threads: 300 Years of New York Style." Call it an extravaganza on a shoestring.
This crowded, unpredictable, almost mind-bogglingly rich show presents the tip of the iceberg, if not the creme de la creme of the museum's extensive costume collection. It ranges through garments of all description: sumptuous evening dresses, handsome costumes for opera and theater, and everyday clothing from pre-Revolutionary to post-hippie times. (Thus, for example, among the exhibition's opening cluster of mannequins, a Quaker dress from the 1840's nearly rubs shoulders with a pair of bell-bottom pants topped by a T-shirt printed with tattoo designs.) 
 Among the show's countless accessories are all manner of hat and shoe, as well as fans, purses, canes and jewelry. And its sundry bits and pieces of memorabilia include nearly 50 portraits, most of them of the ersatz, but nonetheless telling, society variety. These portraits allow for some wonderful juxtapositions of object and image. A feathered, bejeweled casque once owned by Anita Loos is gazed upon by Charles Baskerville's 1934 portrait of the writer. An evening dress in white net with cambric roses that Ethel Barrymore wore in the 1901 production of "Captain Jinks of the Horse Marines" is displayed near a Sargentesque portrait of the actress in the selfsame garment by Sigismond De Ivanowski.
All told, this exhibition gives a scattered yet often remarkable accounting of nearly three centuries' fashion trends in one of the world's great cities while also touching on New York's cultural, political and economic history, at least as far as the rich and famous are concerned. The exhibition's progress is somewhat jumbled, and reading the many labels is highly recommended. They provide a modicum of order as well as the show's subtext, for they state not only who designed each garment, but also who wore it (and when and where) and who donated it to the museum.
Although highly diverse, the displays are nonetheless consistent in their luxuriousness; hence the exhibition's title. After all, the survival rate of common threads -- say, those of newsboys, scullery maids and Bowery bums -- is a lot lower than that of expensive, special-occasion items. Thus, among the highlights of "Uncommon Threads" are the lavishly embroidered velvet jacket and knee pants worn by John Gerard Coster to George Washington's inaugural ball in 1789, as well as the costumes, based on 18th-century French court styles, in which Mr. and Mrs. Cornelius Vanderbilt 2d welcomed guests to the first ball at their freshly completed Fifth Avenue mansion nearly 100 years later. Among such highlights, the show-stopper is surely a white satin fancy dress costume worn by Miss Kate Brice to a ball at the Waldorf in 1897. Broad of bustle, profuse of sleeve and banded in galon d'argent, it is a remarkably accurate replica of the dresses worn by the Spanish Infanta Margarita when she was painted by Velazquez.
In purely stylistic terms, the show traces the shift from the narrow lines of the Neo-Classical empire dresses of the early 1800's to the increasingly small-waisted, large-skirted affairs of the century's second half, circling back to the slimmer, straighter, freer shapes of Paul Poiret and his successors, including a wool jersey dress by Claire McCardell and a suit possibly by Chanel. Repeatedly, one can measure the rage among successive generations for gowns by Worth of Paris, Paquin and Poiret, not to mention the expenditures. Sometimes the evidence is loosely assembled, but the point is made, as when, for example, a metallic-lace evening blouse from Hermes that once belonged to Mrs. Harrison Williams is displayed against a many-itemed bill in her name, from Balenciaga. (A 1928 portrait of Mrs. Williams, one of the best-dressed women of her time, hangs nearby.)
This show is freighted with information and trivia that maybe only a New Yorker could love: for starters, a tweed sport jacket that Edward I. Koch so treasured that he had it relined not once but twice; the threadbare white gabardine suit worn by Ezio Pinza in the original production of "South Pacific," and the olive drab wool uniform of James Jarrett Jr., better known as Buster, the longtime doorman at Henri Bendel during the store's 57th Street prime.
But for the most part, the garments justify themselves visually as well as historically, as with the splendid banyan robe in brocaded silk taffeta of John Cruger Jr., the Mayor of New York from 1757 to 1766, and the frothy white lace gown worn by Helen Hayes in "Victoria Regina."
Perhaps the most unexpected confluence of art, craft, culture and politics is to be found in an extraordinary waistcoat once owned by the theater critic Alexander Woollcott. Skillfully embroidered by Mrs. Theodore Roosevelt Jr. in 1939 according to designs by her son Quentin, it is dotted with bloody severed heads and corpses. Whether these motifs represent the victims of Woollcott's pen is for historians of the theater to decide. In the meantime, this horrific garment is one of the most startlingly beautiful things in a largely startling and beautiful show.

 "Uncommon Threads: 300 Years of New York Style" is at the Museum of the City of New York, 1220 Fifth Avenue, at 103d Street, through Feb. 11.

GRAPHIC: Photo: An 18th-century apron, paste buttons and shoes, a court suit worn at George Washington's inaugural ball. (Museum of the City of New York)

HEADLINE: Inside Art

BYLINE: By Carol Vogel 

 BODY:

Gifts to Museums
Retailers aren't the only ones for whom a successful Christmas depends on the economy. Museums, too, are in this category, since year-end gifts are more than simply a matter of beneficence. Tax laws and fluctuations in the stock market are among the factors that can seriously affect how much (and whether) patrons give.
The picture changes seasonally. "I don't think there's a tax reason people are giving, but there is certainly a profit reason," said Ashton Hawkins, executive vice president and counsel to the trustees at the Metropolitan Museum of Art. "People have made a lot of money this year, and they want to offset their gains." 
 Museums aren't reporting the last-minute rush of gifts they did in 1991, when a so-called tax "window" allowed donors of major gifts to nonprofit organizations to deduct the property's current market value, rather than its original purchase price, as a charitable contribution. Since that window has stayed open under President Clinton's 1993 tax bill, all gifts of appreciated property can be fully deducted at fair market value any time of the year.
"Now what people choose to give to museums has to do with the vagaries of life," Mr. Hawkins said. This season, besides donations of art or objects, people are also making cash gifts (instead of stock) because they anticipate a change in the law to lower tax rates on capital gains.
Museums like the Metropolitan have received both art and cash. Mr. Hawkins said it received a total of $35 million worth of gifts from 133 donors this year. George Repaire, a San Francisco-based gun dealer, and his wife, Butonne, gave the museum a Colt revolver engraved and inlaid with gold and decorated with patriotic imagery. It is believed to have been presented by the inventor Samuel Colt to the Sultan of Turkey in 1854.
Walter H. Annenberg, a longtime trustee and one of the museum's most biggest donors, has given the Metropolitan a half-interest in van Gogh's "Women Picking Olives" (1889-90). This isn't the first time Mr. Annenberg has donated half a painting. The arrangement enables him to keep it in his home in Rancho Mirage, Calif., half the year and at the museum the other half.
Paul and Bunny Mellon, also well-known museum benefactors, gave 3 paintings, 18 drawings and 2 sculptures to the Virginia Museum of Fine Arts in Richmond. Vuillard's "Young Woman Trying on a Hat" (circa 1900) is perhaps the most coveted work in the group. The gift also included works by van Gogh, Corot, Delacroix, Gauguin, Matisse and Picasso. In Toronto, the Art Gallery of Ontario received 16 Old Master paintings from Joey and Toby Tanenbaum, museum board members. It is the museum's largest gift of Old Master paintings. Among the works are examples of French, Italian, Spanish, Flemish and Dutch pictures dating from the 15th to the early 18th century by artists including Giordano, Ribera and Verhaecht. While the museum would not disclose the value of the paintings, experts say they are worth about $3 million.
Under Canadian tax law, a gift must be appraised by the Canadian Cultural Property Export Review Board to certify its value, and the board then determines the donor's tax credit. (The credit is calculated as 29 percent of fair market value and can apply to as much as 100 percent of a family's net income. Any benefit from increased value of the art is exempt from capital gains.)
Cash and securities, not art, made the Art Institute of Chicago happy this season. Larry Ter Molen, vice president for development and public affairs, said that while there was no increase in the amount of artwork the museum received, it was given significantly more stocks. "People are paying off commitments from our capital campaign drive," he said, "and year-end is a popular time to do it."

 
 
Castle Howard
 More art and objects from Castle Howard keep finding their way to auction. The Palladian-style home in Yorkshire, England, known to television viewers as the place where "Brideshead Revisited" was filmed, has a wealth of Old Master paintings, English and French furniture, and decorative objects. Until recently it also had an exceptional collection of Ballets Russes costumes, but they have just been sold at Sotheby's in London. Simon Howard, who inherited the castle from his father, Lord Howard of Henderskelfe, in 1984, needs cash to maintain the stately home and to insure that it stays open to the public.
In 1991, Sotheby's sold the contents of the castle's attic and storerooms in a three-day sale that brought $3.5 million. This month, Sotheby's in London sold Gentileschi's "Finding of Moses" (circa 1633) to a dealer for $7.7 million. Mr. Howard also sold Guercino's "Rinaldo and Armida" privately to the J. Paul Getty Museum in Malibu, Calif., reportedly for $7 million.
The costumes, collected by Mr. Howard's father and sold as property of his estate, were bought primarily by museums. The sale totaled $445,166 and included designs by Matisse, Braque and Picasso. "Any one of these pieces can stand alone as a work of art," said Carol Krute, the curator of costumes and textiles at the Wadsworth Atheneum in Hartford, one of the biggest buyers. It spent $66,125 on 27 costumes.

 
 
Resigned
 The end of the year is traditionally a time of change, and this month these three well-known art-world personalities resigned:
*Phillip M. Johnston is leaving as director of the Carnegie Museum of Art in Pittsburgh, where he has been for 13 years. Mr. Johnston said he was not sure what his next move would be.
*Renato Danese resigned from C&M Arts, the Upper East Side gallery where he had been a partner for a year and a half. He is starting his own contemporary-art gallery with Sasha Newman, former Seymour Knox Curator of European and Contemporary Art at the Yale University Art Gallery. Mr. Danese said his gallery would represent living contemporary artists.
*Bill Arning, who has been director of White Columns for 11 years, is leaving that alternative space in the West Village. He will be replaced by Paul Ha, who has been its associate director. "I've made about 11,000 studio visits," Mr. Arning said. "I definitely think it's time to do something else, probably more outside curating."

HEADLINE: Federal Shutdown Hits Hard In Area Around Yellowstone

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE 

DATELINE: WEST YELLOWSTONE, Mont., Dec. 28

 BODY:
A high mountain sun burned brightly, a fresh snowpack crunched underfoot, and, outside the office of Yellowstone Adventures, a row of new snowmobiles beckoned with the latest conveniences -- from foot warmers to heated handlebars.
"This time last year, all 130 snowmobiles were out every day," fretted Ken Takata, manager of the rental agency. "This week, we are averaging 40 to 50." 
 The Federal Government's partial shutdown, which has closed Yellowstone National Park, is choking this village of its livelihood at the holiday peak of its winter tourism season. Business leaders calculate that since the Dec. 18 closing of Yellowstone, whose 2.2 million acres make it by far the largest of the 39 national parks in the 48 contiguous states, this community of 990 people, just outside the park's western entrance, has been losing $1 million a day.
To any American inclined to see the deadlock over the Federal budget as only a paper problem in faraway Washington, Yellowstone's closing should serve as "the poster child of the shutdown," as one national parks official put it the other day.
"Our hotels are running at 50 percent occupancy, when normally they are at 100 percent at this time of year," said Leif M. Johnson, president of the West Yellowstone Chamber of Commerce.
Inside the park, the two hotels that are usually open in the winter have lost $800,000 so far. With no end to the Federal shutdown in sight, the concessions company that owns the hotels has stopped paying salaries there, providing nothing more than room and board to the hotels' 300 employees, who are now the only lodgers.
"This has been devastating to us -- we were completely sold out," said Jim McCaleb, executive director of the hotels' owner, TW Recreational Services Inc. "We are losing better than a third of our total winter season."
Summer has always been the peak season for Yellowstone. Still, this town, the center for snowmobile tours of the park, helped to build winter tourism there to 140,000 visitors last year, a 40 percent increase over the 1990 level. Until the shutdown, Yellowstone was headed toward record annual attendance, having attracted 3.12 million visitors through Nov. 30. Montana as a whole has come to rely on tourism as its second-largest industry, after agriculture.
But now, of a park staff of 400, only 70 employees designated essential are working. And of Yellowstone's network of 370 miles of roads, only 70 miles are open. These are two stretches that connect towns bordering the park. Visitors who travel these roads may stop to take a picture or two, but nothing more.
The effect on this region has been unkind to business and labor alike. Here in West Yellowstone, as in towns at the park's four other entrances, layoffs have spread fast as sales have plummeted.
On Thursday, tempers boiled in subfreezing temperatures as about 150 people on foot, on snowmobiles and in snowcoaches -- vans that move on tracks -- converged on a thin green line of park rangers guarding the park's western entrance here.
"Public lands belong to the people, not the political parties," proclaimed one protest banner. Another, referring to President Clinton's vacation at Yellowstone in August, asked, "Is vacationing in the park just for the Clintons?"
Inside a red snowcoach, David A. Ritchey, a manager of the Yellowstone Alpen Guides Company, complained: "Usually we have taken 300 to 400 people into the park by now. So far we have taken in zero. This week alone, we refunded $21,000."
Ted Kuhlenschmidt, a tourist from Indiana who had gone ahead with plans for his family's vacation here, largely because he had been unable to get a refund on airline tickets bought in October, stamped his feet in the cold and said he had hoped that someone in government would "do something."
West Yellowstone's Mayor, Glen Loomis, echoed Mr. Kuhlenschmidt. "They need to resolve the problem between the Republicans and Democrats," Mr. Loomis said, "and not on the backs of a few."
Some protesters jeered the rangers, noting that all Federal employees had been promised that any pay lost to the shutdown would be restored once the budget impasse was broken, but that business people and their employees would have no way of making up their own holiday-season losses.
Still, financial frustration was evident among the rangers as well, whose next paychecks, like those of many other Federal workers, will be only half as large as usual and who will not be paid at all after that until there is an end to the stalemate in Washington. "I just applied today for unemployment compensation," said one ranger, Ann Marie Chytra.
Keeping out of sight to avoid inflaming the protesters' passions, Dan R. Sholly, the chief ranger, sat in the back office of a gatehouse and explained to a reporter why he could not use Yellowstone's own income to reopen the park.
During the winter holiday season, some 2,000 people a day pay $4 each to enter the park. About $15,000 more is earned daily from a 25 percent tax on receipts of the two hotels.
But Yellowstone is not legally empowered to run on its own income. It sends its earnings to Washington, then depends on Congress to send money back. Because the annual appropriations bill for the national parks' parent, the Interior Department, has not been enacted, Yellowstone is financially marooned.
"It would be illegal for us to be operating," Mr. Sholly said.
Of activity permitted along the two stretches of road that remain open, he said: "We are trying to be reasonable. People can stop and take a picture. But they can't stop and have a picnic or take out their cross-country skis."
Many people in Montana and in Wyoming, the state where most of the park lies, had hoped that their Governors would follow the lead of a couple of other Western states, which are using state money to keep Federal parks there open.
In Arizona, Gov. Fife Symington has used a special state parks appropriation to pay $17,625 a day in ranger salaries to the Interior Department to keep the Grand Canyon partly open. Arizona has also assumed all insurance liability for accidents at that park. But state money is now running out, and Governor Symington is appealing for private donations.
New Mexico is using a similar state-private alliance to keep open parts of its largest Federal tourist attraction, Carlsbad Caverns National Park. The state has been able to obtain private insurance.
In contrast, the Montana-Wyoming negotiations with the Federal Government over Yellowstone broke down last week, in part because of the steep payments demanded by the Interior Department: almost $50,000 a day, a sum largely reflecting the necessity to maintain roads for snowmobilers along Yellowstone's vast stretches.
Another sticking point in the talks was the problem of dealing with accident liability.
"Every year we get 60 to 100 tort claims, from a few hundred dollars to $10 million," said Mr. Sholly, the chief ranger at Yellowstone. "Right now we are in the middle of a $3.5 million claim" for an injury that a moose inflicted on a tourist.
The Federal Government refused to accept the two states' proposal for a system in which visitors would have signed liability waivers before entering the park. A search for private insurance led nowhere.
Montana's Republican Governor, Marc Racicot, complained in a telephone interview that Washington's demands had become "a moving target."
"We just kept running into one obstacle after another," Mr. Racicot said.
As the frustration mounts, tourists continue to arrive at the park's entrance here, assuming that America's oldest national park is not truly closed.
"I wanted to see the geyser, Old Faithful," said Benyamen Nico Supit, an Indonesian postgraduate student visiting from California, expressively waving his parka-padded arms in the air. Noting that he and his family would move back to Jakarta next summer, he concluded glumly, "This is my first chance to see Yellowstone, and maybe my last chance."

HEADLINE: Helen H. Miller, 96, Biographer, Is Dead

 BODY:
Helen Hill Miller, a prolific biographer and journalist, died on Tuesday at George Washington University Medical Center in Washington. She was 96, and had homes in Washington and Kitty Hawk, N.C.
Mrs. Miller wrote more than 20 books. Her biographies included "George Mason: Constitutionalist" (1938), "Captains From Devon: The Great Elizabethan Seafarers Who Won the World for England" and "Colonel Parke of Virginia: The Greatest Hector in Town" (1990). She also wrote six books on Greece and several works on foreign policy. 
 Mrs. Miller was born in Highland Park, Ill. She received a bachelor's degree from Bryn Mawr College in 1921, a diploma in economics from Oxford University in 1922 and a doctorate in political science from the University of Chicago in 1928.
After working as a staff writer for the Agriculture Department in the late 1930's, she became a Washington correspondent for The Economist and Newsweek from 1940 to 1952. From 1958 to 1966, she was a contributing editor of The New Republic. She also contributed to Harper's Magazine and Esquire.
At the time of her death she had just completed a memoir, "Washington Observed."
She is survived by two sons, Andrew, of Washington, and Robert, of Elizabethtown, N.C., and nine grandchildren.

HEADLINE: A Drought In Spanish Olive Oil;
Old-Style Producer Tilts at Modernity

BYLINE: By NATHANIEL C. NASH 

DATELINE: JAEN, Spain

 BODY:
Shoppers looking for extra-virgin olive oil may not think they have much in common with Juan Antonio Armentero Ruiz.
Mr. Armentero, 75, wears a dirty black beret and has a shiny pink face and a mouth with only a few teeth, one of which is gold. And he is convinced that the United States is exploding nuclear bombs under the nearby Andalusian hills here in southern Spain. 
 But whatever his politics, his problems may soon converge with those of the upscale consumers who have spurred a worldwide boom in olive-oil consumption.
On the first harvest day in the hills around Jaen, 185 miles south of Madrid and the biggest olive-producing region in the world, four men on Mr. Armentero's crew thrash the branches with sticks, sending olives falling into plastic nets below. Two women, on their knees, pick up what misses the nets.
But the crew is small. The trees "look like they've already been picked," Mr. Armentero said. "Fact is, they just don't have any olives. We're living through a disaster."
Though farmers in Spain, as elsewhere, are known to overstate their troubles, and Mr. Armentero certainly enjoys hyperbole, that is not much of an exaggeration. Spain -- normally the world's largest olive-oil producer -- is in its fifth year of drought, and the 1995 olive harvest is about a third of normal, with many farmers in this region saying that their harvest will be more like 10 percent.
The shortage catches the Spanish olive business in the cleft between the inefficiencies of peasant European agriculture and a struggle to modernize. Farmers in recent years have invested in more modern equipment, hoping to make their share of the olive-oil boom commensurate with the high quality of Spanish produce. But their efforts are foundering on that most basic of reasons, the cruelty of the elements.
And since agriculture accounts for a hefty part of Spain's economy, the growers' woes help leave the country with one foot in modern Europe and the other firmly in the past.
For the world's consumers -- who spend more than $5 billion a year for olive oil, because of its flavor and a variety of health claims -- the Spanish drought assures higher prices.
Industry officials say that global production will fall 25 percent short of demand this year. Italy and Greece, the No. 2 and No. 3 producers after Spain, are enjoying plentiful harvests. But Tunisia, No. 4, has also been parched by drought and has lost half its crop.
Importers in the United States say that prices, already up 40 percent this year, could rise a further 30 percent or more early in 1996. "It's the worst Spanish crop I have ever seen," said Richard Sullivan, president of the North American Olive Oil Association in Matawan, N.J.
Though American customers will pay higher prices -- some, no doubt, griping about laying out $5 for a half-liter of oil -- the consequences are more biting for the Spanish growers. The drought spells deeper poverty in a region that is already among this country's poorest.
"The money I get from my olives is what I usually use to make sure I eat well during the winter," said Jose Carlos Oro Perez, 76, as he walked his white donkey down a country road. He estimated that his crop would be about 8 percent of normal. "So I guess it will be hard this year."
To be sure, donkeys are not the common form of transportation for olive farmers. That happens to be dusty, rusting Land Rovers that are 15 to 20 years old, towing small, crude trailers. But the image of a donkey is not far off for an industry whose technology has changed little.
For thousands of years, olives have been harvested by beating the branches with poles. Only recently has new equipment been introduced -- hydraulic tractor arms that grip a limb and shake the tree violently to make the fruit drop. And few farmers can afford such machinery.
Modern irrigation hardly exists. Farmers hook narrow plastic tubes to buckets of water, stick the other, sharpened end into the bark of an olive limb, and hope that this drop-by-drop transfusion will help.
Over the last few years, cooperatives have invested millions of dollars in new plants that press the olives. They have begun marketing efforts to increase the visibility of Spanish oil in worldwide markets. But with the drought, it is one step forward and another back. Now they find themselves deep in debt -- a drag, for the present, on the broad Spanish economy.
And that, too, has some qualities of anachronism. "Spain clearly combines aspects of a developed society and aspects of a relatively undeveloped society," said Jose Luis Alzola, a Salomon Brothers economist in London.
But while the country's problems seldom reverberate beyond the Iberian peninsula, its olive troubles are certain to.
Since the early 1980's, olive consumption in the United States has risen fourfold, to almost 130,000 tons in 1994 from 30,000 tons in 1983. Many consumers found olive oil more flavorful than corn oil -- this while the popularity of Italian, Greek and other Mediterranean cuisines heavy in olive oil has grown greatly.
The oil also got a lift from concern about fat and cholesterol, since its fat is a type often associated with a lower risk of heart disease.
But its price has always made it economically vulnerable. In Spain, the cost of producing olive oil is five times that of sunflower oil. On the grocery shelf, it is often three times the price of vegetable oil.
A year ago, Mr. Sullivan of the olive-oil trade group said, extra-virgin oil was selling for as little as $2.99 a half-liter. Today, it is $4.59. This has put a dent in consumption, he says, even before any 1996 price increases. In the last six months, grocery purchases have fallen 14 percent, and in the last 12 weeks, they are down 22 percent from the comparable period a year before.
The biggest worry for farmers here is that prices will rise so high that consumers will permanently switch to cheaper vegetable or seed oils. In part, the olive farmers are cushioned by big subsidies from the European Union, but if there is scant demand for their oil and their trees wither, there is little other work in this region for them.
Scanning the fields around Jaen, one can look to the horizon and see nothing but row after row of olive trees carpeting the countryside.
"It's hard to find anyone in the area who does not have olives," said Jose Carlos Marzal Fernandez, head of the Foundation for the Development and Promotion of the Olive and Olive Oil. The foundation estimates that olives account for almost half this area's economic activity, or about $1 billion.
In a normal year, Spain produces about 600,000 tons of oil, but output is likely to be no more than 200,000 tons this year. Some say that even those figures are exaggerated to qualify for greater European subsidies.
Moreover, for the first time in memory, the effects are being seen on trees. Few have shriveled up entirely, since olive trees are able to withstand even the cruelest of climates. But five years of drought are taking their toll, with many losing their deep green color and taking on a burnt yellow-green look.
Perhaps most striking is to see almost no one in the fields at the peak of harvest, with thousands of trees bearing no olives at all. Processing plants in small towns, where lines of tractors normally wait to unload olives, are almost deserted.
Hope for a change is hard to find. Rain began to fall in the middle of November, making some predict better days ahead. But farmers are doubtful, warning that even with good rain, the trees cannot quickly recover from so long a drought.
Farmers who are members of one local cooperative have a hard time believing in the name of their processing plant -- the Cooperativa de Aceites Nueva Esperanza, part of which means "new hope."
"We've had a third of the rain in the last three months that we had in the year before, so don't tell me things are going to improve," said Juan Contreras Martinez, the cooperative's manager. "Don't believe what they tell you that the worst is over."
