SECTION: Section 9; Page 9; Column 1; Real Estate Desk 

LENGTH: 1301 words

HEADLINE: Commercial Property/Top 10 Deals;
New York City Holds Its Own Against the Suburbs

BYLINE: By CLAUDIA H. DEUTSCH 

 BODY:
IN one sense, it is just a list, nothing more, nothing less: The 10 biggest deals of 1995. Not necessarily the most complicated or the most lucrative; not even necessarily indicative of anything other than the square footage leased, or important to anyone other than the parties involved and those with a Lettermanesque addiction to Top 10 lists.
But in another sense, the deals yield insights about how the city fared in the year that ends today. They show a city that, at worst, successfully treaded water, and held its own against tenant-hungry suburbs and nearby states. As Carol Nelson, executive vice president of the Edward S. Gordon real estate firm, put it, "The exodus of companies out of New York finally stopped, and in that sense we gained a lot more than we lost this year." 
 First, a word about the list itself. It is tenant oriented -- that is, it is looking at where some of the biggest space users decided to locate in 1995.
Deals that involved new owners, albeit not yet new occupiers, for entire buildings -- say, Donald J. Trump's purchase of the 70-story 40 Wall Street in early December -- are not included, even if they involved larger square footages than those that made the cut. Also missing are cases where buildings changed hands but tenants stayed put. For example, the list ignores New York Life's sale of 63 Madison Avenue, at 27th Street, to George Comfort & Sons, since New York Life immediately leased back the space it had occupied all along.
Now, what it all means.
T HE list depicts a year of mixed blessings. On the down side, newcomers to the city are conspicuously absent from the top deals.
For the most part, the list is made up of companies that are renewing leases or expanding slightly. Their deals yielded big winners and losers in the landlord community, but they netted out fairly evenly for the local economy.
After all sorts of off-again, on-again negotiations and 11th-hour panics, the New York State Dormitory Authority finally completed the deal to buy 550,000 square feet of condominium space in 361 Fifth Avenue, the old B. Altman building on 34th Street. The building will be the new home of the City University of New York's Graduate Center.
That deal, which ranked as the year's third largest, led directly to the list's sixth-ranked deal. The Graduate Center currently occupies the state-owned building at 33 West 42d Street, and the B. Altman deal would not have made fiscal sense if the move would have left that space vacant. The best way to make the numbers work was to move the optometry college from 315 Park Avenue South, the 24th Street building that for two decades had been its home, into the Graduate Center's space.
The problem is that this leaves a gaping hole in 315 Park Avenue South, where the optometry school's lease was supposed to run another eight years. Not surprisingly, the building's owners are suing the state, but few people expect that to stop the move.
O THER top-10 deals were less controversial but also fell short of providing new revenues for the city or reasons for optimism for the real estate industry. CS First Boston's move to 11 Madison is a vote of confidence for Midtown South, but as far as New York is concerned, all it did was free up space at 55 East 52d Street, the Park Avenue Plaza that had been its home.
Similarly, Equitable Life may have sopped up a lot of excess space at 1290 Avenue of the Americas, but it is leaving a huge chunk of emptiness at 787 Seventh Avenue, the headquarters it built for itself just 10 years ago. (At least part of that chunk will soon be filled: Willkie Farr & Gallagher, the law firm that jumped into the news when Mario M. Cuomo joined it earlier this year, is moving in.)
Still, cheery tidbits and perhaps even trendlets emerged from the biggest-deals list, too. Two of the companies in the Top 10 list expanded significantly. Viacom's new lease brings it to well beyond 500,000 square feet at its Times Square headquarters. And Depository Trust added about 180,000 square feet to the 500,000 square feet it already occupied at 55 Water Street. That was the sort of help that downtown Manhattan, an area still mired in vacancies, sorely needed.
And as for the other companies on the list, even though some of them took cheaper space, or even less space, at least they stayed in Manhattan. That fact alone yielded heartfelt we-can-finally-breathe-out-now reactions among city boosters. Many of them, after all, were still shellshocked from 1994, when Swiss Bank announced it would move its headquarters to Stamford, Conn., and when Mastercard's new chief reversed his predecessor's decision to stay in New York and moved the financial services organization to less expensive space in Westchester.
This year, the big companies that threatened to leave the city -- either seriously or as a bargaining chip -- decided to stay.
Colgate-Palmolive, which owns undeveloped land in New Jersey, nonetheless renewed for nearly 520,000 square feet at the Park Avenue building that bears its name. Avon Products, whose oh-so-expensive lease was running out at 9 West 57th Street, had looked quite closely in Stamford and in Westchester. Instead, the company took 226,200 square feet of low-floor space at the former Exxon Building.
(Interesting sidelight: In a smaller but still noteworthy deal that tallied in as 23d largest for the year, Avon's top brass are moving to about 121,000 square feet of tower space at 1345 Avenue of the Americas -- the 55th Street building that Mastercard was planning to move to before its 11th-hour change of heart.)
But the averted defection that had the most far-reaching implications for the city, if not for the real estate industry, was that of the United Nations Development Program (not to be confused with the United Nations Development Corporation, the quasi-governmental group that handles United Nations real estate). The big United Nations agency was a hotly wooed tenant, with suitors from as close as New Rochelle, N.Y., and as far away as Bonn. Many politicians and local business people worried that the departure of a huge United Nations agency would have dealt a mortal blow to the city's reputation as the premier international center.
The agency is staying, in condominium space that the development corporation purchased at 633 Third.
All those deals sound like discrete incidents. But, look a bit further down the biggest-deals list and a picture begins to emerge of how mutually interdependent many of 1995's biggest deals were.
Take Viacom's expansion in 1515 Broadway. To do that, the big entertainment company had to take over space that had long been occupied by the New York State Urban Development Corporation. The corporation was willing to vacate that space, provided it could strike a good deal elsewhere.
This year the corporation bought some 154,000 square feet of space at 633 Third -- a transaction large enough to rank as No. 16 on the biggest-deals list. And that, in turn, made the United Nations deal a lot more palatable.
In its own transaction, the United Nation's real estate arm had agreed to buy the space that New York State was considering, if the Urban Development Corporation's deal fell through. The United Nations Development Corporation then would have had to find another United Nations tenant to put into the space.
Thomas Appleby, the development corporation's president, never seemed to find that prospect daunting.
"We could certainly find other U.N. agencies to take that space," he said last year, when the U.D.C.'s plans were still in question.
Probably so -- but then, where would those other agencies be moving from? If the pieces had not fallen into place, the musical space chairs game could well have been extended into 1996.

SECTION: Section 3; Page 1; Column 1; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 2237 words

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
Baby Boomers Hit 50. Now What?

BYLINE: By DAVID CAY JOHNSTON 

 BODY:
THE roughly 76 million baby boomers, raised to expect lives of unlimited possibilities, start turning 50 tomorrow. And as their future becomes their now, the older boomers are beginning to gray and wrinkle, to wear bifocal contact lenses, to marvel at how young the police are -- and to discover that while many of them will live long, few will prosper.
As a group, boomers earn so much that whole industries -- cars being the most expensive example -- made themselves over to win their business. But as individuals, most boomers struggle from paycheck to paycheck, even when there are two breadwinners. 
 Two out of five owe more than they own. Even most of those in the black could not go 90 days without a job. And as many jobs are down-sized, right-sized and re-engineered out of existence, not only are the days of steadily rising pay behind them, their jobs may be, too.
So, while they're living longer, they cannot count on working as long as their forebears did -- though, to pay the bills, they may have to.
Not every boomer struggles. More than half attended college, and half of those graduated. About 2 percent of boomers -- about 1.5 million of them -- are worth $1 million or more (beyond home equity), according to a Merrill Lynch survey to be released soon. An additional 17 percent have $100,000 to $1 million.
Dr. Alan Manevitz, 36, a psychiatrist at New York Hospital, said opportunities for boomers "are not in the future, they are in the now."
So what, if anything, can aging boomers do to resolve the economic dissonance between what they have and what they're told they need to retire?
Here are issues that some older boomers, and experts who study their attitudes and actions, suggest are worth pondering:
WORK Benefits experts and financial planners say boomers should not assume that their jobs will last beyond age 58. That means the roughly 3.4 million boomers turning 50 this year, who had envisioned having 15 years or so to save for their golden years, may have about half that.
"Look at I.B.M.," said Judy Lau, a certified financial planner in Wilmington, Del. In trimming its work force, I.B.M. made retirement available earlier, "so 55 became the magic age instead of 65."
"People really are looking at how to make it between 55 and 65," she said.
Edward M. Goldman, who turns 50 in July, feels comfortable these days as general manager of WBZ in Boston, one of the nation's largest radio stations. He has been salting away money since he became a father at age 29, and when he got a big promotion a decade ago, he decided to save more, not spend more. Now, even if he suddenly could not work, he said, "I would not be destitute."
But he also knows his hard-earned comfort could vanish in a heartbeat.
"When you are an employee, even at a senior level," Mr. Goldman said, "who comes in over you, or what the company is going to buy or sell, or whether you strike somebody the wrong way can mean it's suddenly over."
Few boomers, though, are in his enviable position of being prepared financially.
"People have this resignation, as I do, that it is what it is," Mr. Goldman said. "I do the best I can to maintain my health -- I exercise, run, lift weights" -- because he may need to work for a long time.
But that work may not be there. So in addition to putting money in tax-deferred retirement accounts, boomers should also be building up nest eggs they can crack open before age 59 1/2 (the age at which retirement accounts can be tapped without penalty).
SOCIAL SECURITY Don't count on it for much, even though people pay a fortune for it, roughly a seventh of pay when the matching amount charged to employers is added in. Already the law requires all boomers to reach 66 or 67 to collect in full, and some may have to work until 70, or even 72.
B. Douglas Bernheim, a Stanford University economist, says the better-off boomers should expect nothing from Social Security. "We will see means testing before most of the boomers retire," he said. "A lot of people will be means tested out of the program."
RETIREMENT Don't count on any company pension to be enough. Those jobs that people held in their 20's probably did not qualify them for a pension, and the pension from jobs they left after a dozen years at 40 will be paid in dollars that will not be adjusted for inflation.
The experts say that while the first career may end at 58, boomers should expect to work into their 70's, even if it is part time.
Many who lived through the Depression and World War II looked on old age as a time not to work. But boomers may be different.
The experts all say boomers need to be saving 10 percent or more of gross earnings for retirement. Merrill Lynch's retirement index shows the boomers need to save at about three times their current rate of about 4 percent. The most pessimistic model -- developed by Mr. Bernheim -- suggests that boomers need to save at 9 times their current rate, which would mean socking away a third of take-home pay. "One of the most disturbing findings," Mr. Bernheim said, "is that the boomers are not accelerating their rate of savings as they get older."
"Their retirement years will be much more focused on work, and less on leisure," said J. Walker Smith, a managing partner at Yankelovich Partners who studies boomers.
But that work may be on their terms. Mr. Smith points to casual dress policies on Fridays at some button-down corporations as a small but symbolic indicator of how, as boomers acquire power, they remake institutions to fit their values.
"The boomers have changed the rules every time they have entered a new time of their lives," Mr. Smith said. He expects boomers to press for more flexible hours and more working from home.
"Boomers are going to confront retirement in a very different way than their parents, confront it more as a work style than a life style," Mr. Smith predicted.
One thing's for sure. They'll regret it later if they don't take full advantage of a retirement plan like a 401(k) -- at the very least, to the point where a company matches part of what is put in.
And people should think of ways to make some money in their 60's and 70's, and look at retirement as they would death, something to be put off as long as possible.
INHERITANCE Don't count on it. Chances are two in three that parents will die leaving debts or so little money it won't pay for a vacation to Disney World. That $10 trillion that will pass from one generation to the next is highly concentrated in what Donald Trump calls "the lucky sperm club."
ESTATE PLANNING Even those with modest means need a will, especially if they have children, said Carlyn S. McCaffrey, a lawyer who specializes in estate planning for wealthy clients of Weil, Gotshal & Manges.
For those who have put off a will, fearing that the mere thought will bring them closer to their head stone, Mrs. McCaffrey offered some chilling thoughts on what happens when people die without wills.
"If there are still young children, courts will end up supervising assets," she said, and money that could have sent the kids to college may end up paying legal fees.
So get to a lawyer, or at least get a boilerplate will, available on paper or computer shareware at many stationery stores.
RESPONSIBILITIES Some boomers worry that they will become the sandwich generation, pressed between demands to support parents who have outlived their retirement savings and superannuated children who won't leave the nest. It will be worse than that for some boomers, who will have to care for grandparents. And some boomer grandparents may have to step in and raise grandchildren.
Dee Lee, a financial planner in Harvard, Mass., runs retirement seminars for government agencies and corporations, and she has a small but steady stream of men and women in their 50's who want advice about careers and grandchildren.
"Their kids have screwed up through drugs or alcohol or a bad marriage, and the grandparents have to raise the kids," Ms. Lee said. "People think this is an issue only for welfare mothers. It's not.
"I personally have seen this in two instances, where women in their mid- to late 50's quit their jobs to care of their grandchildren. Not smart. Better to keep working and pay for child care."
If people want to make a gift to a grandchild, but don't trust a grown child to keep hands off the money, do it through the Uniform Gift to Minors Act and make yourself or someone you trust the custodian.
INSURANCE Boomers in their 50's whose children are grown may need little or no life insurance. But those who started late, or started a second family, need to provide for their dependents. And even though many boomers will live long lives, insurance costs a lot, because 10 percent of men at age 50 will be gone by 65, according to actuaries at William M. Mercer Inc.
The appropriate amount of life insurance varies with family circumstances. Those with grown children would do better to build an investment portfolio than to pay insurance premiums. But for families that need insurance, the right amount depends not just on savings and debts, but also on the support that could be expected from relatives and the ability of survivors to adapt.
Whatever the need, prices of term insurance -- which has no investment feature -- have never been lower.
RELATIONSHIPS Breaking up is hard to do, but when you're older it's expensive, too. Older boomers often have houses, pensions, 401(k) accounts, not to mention picture albums and other treasures.
Linda P. Giering, 48, earned a doctorate in physical organic chemistry while having two children, and for years has traveled the world as a marketer for Du Pont Merck. Then her husband of 26 years left.
Now she has a 6,600-square-foot house in Harvard, Mass., two grown children and a lot of time on her hands, but still she can't face up to the daunting task of dealing with the mechanics of divorce.
"Getting the divorce final and settling who gets the retirement money and the house and whatnot has not been a priority, partly because it costs a lot," Ms. Giering said.
As the major breadwinner in her marriage, Ms. Giering expects to make it fine. But typically women in divorces find their assets and income slashed.
Just as divorce can wreak havoc with the best-laid financial plans, making marriage endure can avoid ripping a big hole in the wallet.
STRESS Most boomers are doing fine psychologically, but the stresses that afflict many of them will worsen with time, predicted Mr. Smith of Yankelovich Partners.
"Boomers started their careers when the future had a very optimistic view to it," Mr. Smith said. "They felt it was more or less their birthright to spend a lot of time in creative leisure pursuits, to focus on self-fulfillment and self-experience, an attitude very different from their parents and their children and, it turned out, very different from their experience in the economy in the 70's and 80's."
After that, "many boomers developed a real sense of defeatism," he said.
Harold Hodgkinson, director of the Center for Demographic Policy in Washington, says some boomers will decide, beginning in their late 50's, that they just cannot cope and will decide to check out.
"A statistically significant rise in suicides is not unreasonable," Mr. Smith said, "and the issue for these boomers will be whether they can deal with the stresses in their lives.
"Reported stress levels, concerns for personal security, for economic security and trust in every institution -- religious, economic, political, media, you name it -- are at all-time lows."
Barbara Steif, 41, a clinical psychologist at Provident Medical Center in Portland, Ore., said: "Many boomers have not had life experiences which bring suffering into their lives. It may be a surprise when they hit 40, 45 or 50 that everything is not in their control.
"We see a number of people here who come in suicidally depressed," she said, with causes including bankruptcy, college bills or health care problems.
She said boomers need to think about "whether competing with your peers for the right house, the right car and the right Cuisinart makes sense."
SPENDING Credit cards and cash machines are a major reason that boomers do not save more, said Jack Blankenship of Blankenship & Foster, financial planners in Del Mar, Calif.
"You can easily spend 20 percent of your money just in cash taken $100 or $150 at a time from a teller machine," Mr. Blankenship said.
He tries to persuade new clients to carry a notebook and record every cash expenditure. Then he has clients put the data into their Quicken or other personal finance software so they can see where their money goes.
"Once people reach age 50, when they start thinking about mortality and how are they going to pay for retirement, they start coming in our door looking for help," he said.
PERSONAL GOALS Looking back, boomers should ask themselves if they're satisfied with what they've done with their lives so far, and if not, whether it's really too late to make big changes.
For some, the years ahead may be the last chance to, say, be a Big Sister or Big Brother, being a mentor to someone who really needs one, to plant flowers in a neglected neighborhood park, to try a new adventure.
Even if the money is limited, the possibilities aren't. "Being 50," Dr. Steif said, "is a lot younger than it used to be."

SECTION: Section 3; Page 4; Column 1; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 1118 words

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
In a Soaring Market, Looking for Good Luck at Low Altitudes

BYLINE: By REED ABELSON 

 BODY:
AFTER a year of dizzying new heights for the market, investors may despair of finding any good stocks left. One natural strategy for these worried rummagers is to look among the year's most disappointing equities.
And, despite the stellar market, there were disappointing stocks in 1995, and they included many large and well-known companies. According to an analysis done by Standard & Poor's last week, the 15 worst performers in the S.& P. 500-stock index this year lost between 28 percent and 57 percent of their value. Among them are Stride Rite and Tandem Computers, which disappointed shareholders with sharply lower earnings; Advanced Micro Devices and Reebok International, which failed to introduce the right products, and Inland Steel and Navistar International, which seemed to run smack into slowing demand for their goods. 
 Though it seems improbable, investors who examine these and other ugly ducklings may discover a swan or two. Take Sears, Roebuck. In 1994, it was down in the dumps with lower profits and a falling stock price. Investors who had faith in its big plan to reinvent itself, however, watched the stock climb 66 percent this year, when the S.& P. 500 sprinted 34 percent.
But beware: a broad-gauge approach does not work in this chancy area of investing. Collectively, the year's worst performers "don't necessarily give you a group of stocks you want to own," said Robert Stovall, president of Stovall/21st Advisors in New York. Most of last year's dismal stocks are unlikely to have more success this year, simply because they will be unable to fix what was broken: high costs, unappealing products or a misguided strategy.
In fact, buying a basket of market laggards is "the worst-performing strategy there is," contended Jim O'Shaughnessy, who heads his own money-management firm in Greenwich, Conn. According to Mr. O'Shaughnessy's study of more than 40 years of data, investors who bought $10,000 of the worst performers among large-company stocks every year since 1951 would have ended up with less than half the amount they would have earned investing in the S.& P. 500 during that time.
Of course, investors must examine price-earnings and price-book ratios and other standard data for these companies before plunking down any money. But it is even more important to analyze the companies' current difficulties and any corrective efforts. Here are some questions to help determine if recovery is in the cards.
* Is the company in a viable business?
The Handleman Company, well-known as a wholesaler of music products that still comprise half its revenue, also supplies retailers with videotapes, books and software. But this is a business in which it is becoming harder to make money. "They are very specialized middlemen. That puts them in a kind of dangerous niche," said Ron Petrie, an analyst with Roney & Company. One giant customer, Wal-Mart, is beginning to order directly from manufacturers, and other retailers are squeezing Handleman more to improve their own profits. "It's a business where the margins have disappeared," Mr. Petrie said.
Investors who expect a recession in 1997 -- as some forecasters, like Standard & Poor's, do -- might also want to avoid companies that are too dependent on the overall economy, noted Joseph Tigue, managing editor of Standard & Poor's Outlook. Despite bigger market share and higher profit, Navistar International may be no bargain, for example. The company is already predicting that demand for its heavy-duty engines and trucks will sharply decline over the coming year.
* Is there a new management team?
Investors might think twice about a company run by the same people who got it into its current mess. Reebok's chairman, Paul Fireman, has been greatly criticized about his company's woes, for example, but few observers expect him to step down.
And at places where there are new managers, like Stride Rite, Kmart and Charming Shoppes, investors should look for signs of success. At Charming Shoppes, the new president and chief executive is Dorrit J. Bern, who was credited with improving the women's apparel division at Sears.
"I think she's very capable," said Harry A. Ikenson, a senior director at Rodman & Renshaw, a brokerage firm. She has cut costs and made the retailer more fashion-conscious, and Mr. Ikenson thinks a result will be profitability sometime next year. "All the ingredients for a turnaround are there," he said.
* Are the company's products compelling?
Companies need sales, or the prospect of them. Because Advanced Micro failed to develop a chip to compete with Intel's new Pentium, it has lost market share and seen sales of its older chip plummet. "It's a classic example of a technology company missing a product cycle," said Raj Rajaratnam, an analyst at Needham & Company, a brokerage firm. While Advanced Micro has said its new chip is still on the way, investors won't find out whether the company succeeds for at least six to nine months.
Lack of a strong seller has also hurt Reebok, while Nike, its rival, can't keep its pricey shoes on the shelves. Investors might want to stay away from Reebok until they are convinced that the company can come up with a shoe that will dazzle consumers. "They're not leading-edge," said Jennifer Black Groves of Black & Company, a research firm in Portland, Ore. "It hasn't been the cool shoe to wear."
* Is the company likely to go bankrupt or go out of business?
The good news here is that most companies offer investors plenty of warning of impending demise, said Lewis Altfest, a financial planner in New York. Two red flags are debt-laden balance sheets and repeated annual losses. The exception are retailers, which can file for bankruptcy quickly if their suppliers start to panic. "Nobody thought Caldor was a goner," he said, alluding to the discounter which filed for Chapter 11 protection this year.
Kmart, however, shows the uncertainties that can make rummaging among losers occasionally lucrative. While there are many rumors of a looming Chapter 11 filing, others judge the talk unfounded. "The stock has gotten way oversold," said Mr. Petrie, who belongs in the latter camp. "Take away the rumors, and the stock would be selling at three times its current level."
* Most important, is the problem fixed?
Few companies that do poorly do nothing about it. The Brown Group, maker of Buster Brown shoes, is shutting down plants and boarding up retail stores. Navistar plans to slash costs by $112 million in 1996. Advanced Micro Devices has made a key acquisition.
For the bottom-fishing investor, therefore, the big nail-biter is: Will the changes be enough to turn a company around?

HEADLINE: MARKET WATCH;
Casinos May Find Adversity A Boon

BYLINE: By JAMES STERNGOLD 

 BODY:
By the end of this year, it seemed appropriate that the most powerful company in one of the wealthiest industries in the country -- gambling -- was called Mirage Resorts.
After gambling grew rapidly in the early 1990's, the industry's success started to vaporize in 1995. The spread of casinos to new towns screeched to a halt because of popular resistance, Congress threatened new regulations, gambling stocks got clobbered and the Harrah's Jazz Company, which was building a big new casino in New Orleans, filed for Chapter 11 bankruptcy in November. The stock market seemed like the only place left for an honest wager. 
 But those who study the proclivities of gamblers, at the slots as well as in the market, say the odds for 1996 look good. Considering that this is an industry with a stock market capitalization of more than $30 billion, that is important news for investors, whether or not they buy casino shares.
In a way, analysts see the political environment -- in which some conservative politicians, including several Presidential candidates, have called for a halt to the spread of casinos -- as a blessing in disguise.
The growth of gambling in the last five years has created enormous competition along the Mississippi River, on Native American tribal lands and in small towns across the country. The costs of running casinos have risen sharply and management at the companies has been stretched thin. A lull in the opening of new jurisdictions will allow the better-managed companies to improve existing operations, particularly in Las Vegas, Nev., and Atlantic City.
Even threats to throw casinos out of some of the newly opened areas -- some expect Louisiana to consider a prohibition because of corruption scandals -- do not worry some analysts.
"The healthy part is that this will be the time to regroup," said Lee Isgur, an analyst at Jeffries & Company. "In the major markets -- Las Vegas, Atlantic City and Mississippi -- there are few regulations and a good operating environment. They won't be affected by a rollback."
Salomon Brothers estimates that gambling revenues at casinos will rise to $18 billion next year, from $16.1 billion in 1995 and $14.1 billion in 1994.
But most analysts predict that because of the peculiar economics of the business, the more moderate growth will help only the biggest companies, not the dozens of smaller gambling concerns that have gone public in recent years. Gamblers need a new thrill, and so the companies will have to erect ever larger casinos, even at the old gambling sites, to increase the crowds.
That, in fact, will be happening in Las Vegas. In the next year, three new casino-hotels, costing nearly $1 billion, are scheduled for completion: the Monte Carlo, the Stratosphere and New York-New York, which will bring a faux Manhattan skyline to the desert. But only the wealthiest companies can make such hefty investments.
"There'll be a real tiering in 1996," said David Wolfe, an analyst at Oppenheimer & Company. "The companies have got to put up new projects to grow, and not that many companies have the ability to finance new projects."
In other words, the iron rule of gambling will hold: the house, in the form of the biggest casino operations, will win.
"Nineteen ninety-six," said Scott M. Renner, an analyst at Salomon Brothers, "is going to be the year when the rich get richer."

SECTION: Section 13; Page 4; Column 1; The City Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 1996 words

HEADLINE: THE YEAR IN BUSINESS.;
Hail to the Pet Taxi and the Deli Poets

 BODY:
WE sculpt alloy in SoHo lofts and scour the streets for edgy Nikon images and fill sleek galleries with retro, po-mo P.C. TV installations. But the true art of New York is more prosaic. It lies in making a buck.
Like most art, a business scheme is an alchemy of varied elements. Think of the refrajo, a drink that mixes champagne sodas and beer and is carefully marketed to the Latin American population of Queens. Or the cybercafes, blending Silicon Valley with Seattle grunge to attract Flatiron 'Net surfers. Or the Korean deli that has poetry nights. Or the Indian restaurant that goes kosher. 
 The city is a gridiron of tiny entrepreneurial skirmishes, furtive forays into vacant niches, with grand plans to fill, or create, demand. One move affects another, creating new forms, like the caterpillar of an office supply store transforming into a butterfly of a supply store-boutique-espresso bar.
On the principle that business reveals the city more than politics -- for example, immigrants enter the economic mainstream well before the political -- we bring you highlights of the entrepreneurial world of 1995.
 
THE PET TAXI Lawrence Reilly started Pet Taxi because it's tough to get a taxi in the city if you're carrying a cat or a dog. He decorated a yellow minivan like an old Checker cab. One client moved a 15-pound turtle and some baby sharks.
 "I have a hard time finding a regular taxi willing to stop," said a customer, Nikki Alvarez. "I have to trick them and have someone hold the dog while I flag down the cab; then they complain that they don't want to take us."
 
THE MEGADELI The Penn Plaza Deli opened, taking up a full city block between 33d and 34th Streets on Eighth Avenue, with pizza, fried plantains, sushi and Mongolian barbecue and multiple serving stations, piped-in music, large television sets and a blocklong counter with stools.
Chung Hoon Shin, the manager, described the concept: "Have the best food and the biggest place. We have everything, so you don't have to go to a lot of different places."
 
THE PEDICAB George Bliss, an actor, started Pedicabs of New York. For 50 cents a minute, you get a ride in a 200-pound fiberglass vehicle attached to a tricycle. It took him two years to find liability insurance.
"This is the transportation of the future," Mr. Bliss said. "It isn't just a tourist attraction."

BALDIES WITH ATTITUDE Lorraine Lagalla of Ridgewood, Queens, hired ex-street criminals to sculpture hair for blowouts, Jersey fades baldies, and intricate hair designs. Found them the most qualified.
 "I'm going to jail," said a barber, Harvey Garcia, who had been arrested on a gun charge. "I got one to three years. I'm not happy about it."
 
KASHRUT KEBABS Pradeep Shinde, owner of Madras Mahal, specialized in kosher Indian food to stand out in the heated lunch competition in Curry Hill, around Lexington Avenue in the 20's.
 "Quite a few restaurants have gone out of business around here," Mr. Shinde said, "but I can pay my bills sometimes because of the Jewish community."
 
THE EDGE IN GRAFFITI TATS Cru, three South Bronx graffiti artists named Bio, Nicer and BG 183, were hired by Coca-Cola to do mural-style ads in inner-city areas.
"These kids, they don't go to museums," Bio said of neighborhood youths. "This is their art. They come to see the walls on the streets."
 
IAMBIC PENTA-METER Marc Anthony Butcher, a cabby, sold a book of his poems to his passengers.
"I have a rotating captive audience," he said.
 
THE LITTLE MIXER BOY A 4-year-old disk jockey, 43-inch-tall Justin Spaulding of Southeast Queens, commanded hundreds of dollars at playground basketball tournaments and birthday parties.
He spins two hours a night, in between sessions with Barney and the Power Rangers.
 
TWO NATIONS Avenue A entrepreneurs decided that the best use of a space was to split its functions -- a coffee bar during the day, a plain old bar at night.
"If we had opened a bar during the day, we would have gotten hard-core drunkards," said Graham Winton, a part-owner.
 
LOCAL APPEAL Wendy's installed a piece of artwork to smooth over hard feelings with anti-commercialism locals at Broadway and Bleecker.
"Sure, I have mixed feelings about showing at Wendy's," said Bill Rabinovitch, a figurative expressionist painter. "But if no one ever sees your work, what's the point of being an artist?"
 
UPSCALE PUBS The dark, secretive Irish bars of the 1980's that served as entry points and job centers for illegal immigrants went upscale as the clientele assimilated. You were as likely to see a patron sipping a warm tulip glass of Irish coffee as a pint glass of dark beer.
"When you came here, you took an apartment the bartender gave you because you needed it," said Mary Collins of Sunnyside, Queens. "We're not illegal anymore. We don't have to hide."
 
CAFE LOS NEGROES An editor, a currency trader, a techie and an independent film maker joined to create a Web site for underground minority artists.
"We recognized that this is a divided society," said Charlotte Burley, the film maker. "People who know computers and people who don't."
 
POEMS, POTATOES Lynn Gerber, a poetry devotee, did a deal with Hee Min Kwon, owner of Picnic Place, a deli and salad bar on Fifth Avenue near 36th Street. The deli became the site of weekly poetry readings. Everyone pays $6 and gets a $2 Picnic Place voucher.
One reader, Patricia Chao, found the site unintimidating: "Once you get to the deli level, it's pretty democratic."
 
NEW AGE FITNESS The gym wars intensified with the opening of the cavernous Reebok facility near Lincoln Center. Some gyms expanded to include reflexology, hypnosis, rolfing (manipulation of muscle sheaths), and acupucture. Other New Age perks: gym pyschologists, gym personal shoppers and gym barbers.
"Fitness has always been perceived as a vanity," said Pat Manocchio, when he was about to open La Palestra on West 67th Street. "We see it as something more medical."
 
BIg vs. Little
 
THE megastores kept moving in, creating a culture of adjustment among the established smaller businesses.
 
TO ADJUST OR NOT TO ADJUST A beloved and defining Upper West Side landmark, Shakespeare and Company, counterpunched the whalelike Barnes & Noble. It moved into used, rare and antique books and specially ordered out-of-print books. It introduced author appearances and readings. It even took the offensive, deciding to start a mini-chain and open a new store at 69th and Lexington.
"If bookstores were movie critics," said Lee Minoff, a book shopper on Upper Broadway,. "Shakespeare would be Pauline Kael and Barnes & Noble would be Siskel and Ebert.," said Lee Minoff, a bookshopper on Upper Broadway.
 
DARWIN AND LATTE Starbucks, the Seattle-based coffee chain, opened on Columbus Avenue and 67th Street. The nearby smaller cafes, which had been invaders themselves only a short time before, had to scurry.
Timothy's posted a daily trivia question; the first 10 people who answered correctly won a free eight-ounce coffee. New World boasted of two espresso shots in its tall latte compared with one at Starbucks. Cooper's offered half-price happy-hour granitas ( coffee-flavored slushies).
 "We all benefit in a sense," said Andrew Resnick, vice president of Timothy's, which is based in Toronto. "Competition makes you better. We need to be sharper, cleaner, nicer."
 
MIXED USE King's, at 14 East 33d Street, was a traditional family-owned office supply store, facing intense competition from Staples, the large national chain.
So it installed a coffee bar, and began selling boutique items like ties, gifts and scarves. Soon it will open 24 hours a day.
"We were under siege," said King's president, Howard Pilmar. "With the advent of superstores we had to retool."
 
The Year in Hustling
 
SCALPER STRESS Responding to a police crackdown outside Madison Square Garden, older, established scalpers paid a hardier, more brazen breed of youthful scalpers to work the street.
"They keep coming over here and selling," said Officer John Vasquez. "They're bold. I guess they're desperate."
 
TRANSFER STATION In a rush-hour ritual at the Hub at 149th Street and Third Avenue in the Bronx, peddlers rummaged through trash bins, found bus transfers and then sold them to subway riders for 50 cents each, whispering "for the bus, for the bus" and "transfers, transfers."
"We provide a community service," said a peddler named John, who gave only his first name. "Many people here are on tight budgets. The police may not see it that way, but our customers do."
 
PRODUCT UPGRADE In Manhattan Valley, the police said they saw a resurgence in heroin use as cocaine dealers in brownstones and housing developments from 100th to 110th Streets began to diversify. Prices went down; quality went up.
"We're definitely getting more calls about it, and we're seeing more of it ourselves," said Tamar Lynn, an assistant of the West Side Crime Prevention Program, in March. "Heroin users have this glazed, haunted look, as opposed to crack users, who are kind of jumping around."
 
WALL STREET CONNECTION Cabbies looking for Wall Street fares queued up on East 79th Street and York Avenue, piling groups of riders in for a fare of $3.50 each. When the passengers asked to be taken somewhere else, the drivers refused, and the scene occasionally became violent.
"Are you kidding? There's a hassle out there every day," said Edward J. Kloos, a co-owner of the nearby Suburban Meats.
 
METER FEEDERS On Arthur Avenue in the Bronx, a ragtag group of men and women made a living feeding quarters, for a small fee, into expiring meters in lots and curbsides. Despite the illegality, they became a valuable resource for many shoppers and merchants.
"These guys are great," said Sharon Henderson, of Parkchester, clutching a couple of sagging shopping bags from Randazzo's Fish Market. "Very good service at a cheap price. It beats kicking the meter."
 
OFF and Off Off Broadway companies reverted to a time-honored tradition to sell tickets: the gimmick. Free food was popular. (One offered fine cuisine, another pizza.) Being quick and funny also helped. Here's a sampling:
 
"TOO MUCH LIGHT MAKES THE BABY GO BLIND"
Here, 145 Avenue of the Americas, at Dominick Street.
Still running.
 
 The Neo Futurists, a troupe from Chicago, performed 30 plays in a hour. The audience picked the plays from a menu of 2,000 mini-dramas.
Admission price was determined by a roll of the dice, and the company bought the entire audience pizza in the event of a sellout.
 
"POMP DUCK & CIRCUMSTANCE"
DeWitt Clinton Park, 11th Avenue and 53d Street
Still running.
 
 A surreal theater experience served up with gourmet soup, pasta and, of course, duck. An international company of acrobats, jugglers, musicians and clowns provided the Bauhaus atmosphere while the audience digested.
 
"GRANDMA SYLVIA'S FUNERAL"
Playhouse on Vandam, 15 Vandam Street.
Still running.
 
 A comic wake was staged on the streets of SoHo. The audience got to play the mourners. Fights between characters looked so genuine that the police intervened and local diners emptied restaurants to watch.
 
"O. J. WHODUNNIT MURDER MYSTERY"
The Ibis Club, 327 West 44th Street.
 
 Riding the wave of O.J.-mania, the playwright Larry Wolfe Horwitz created a constantly updated parody of the trial of the century. The audience played jury members for another mythical murder and picked a verdict. The show closed when the trial did.
 
"THE COMPLEAT WORKS OF WLLM SHKSPR (Abridged)"
Westside Theater, 407 West 43d Street

 This production presented all of Shakespeare's 37 plays and 154 sonnets in under two hours. This meant shrinkage: a 20-minute "Romeo and Juliet," and 16 plays stuffed into something called "The Love Boat Goes to Verona." The cast of three played 25 characters apiece.

HEADLINE: NEIGHBORHOOD REPORT: LOOKING BACK, LOOKING AHEAD;
An Economic Messiah for Harlem?

BYLINE: By ROBIN POGREBIN 

 BODY:
Skeptics could be forgiven for having little faith in the Federal Empowerment Zone; it has been kicked around as a concept since a group of conservatives introduced enterprise zones during the Reagan Administration. Since then, it has apparently amounted to little more than a political football -- alternately embraced and abandoned by each party -- and some studies have raised questions about its potential efficacy.
Now, after a year of planning, $300 million in Federal, state and city funds is about to be directed toward Upper Manhattan and a sliver of the South Bronx over the next 10 years. What difference this will make in a particularly impoverished swath of the city is still unclear. But the political benefits are clear: minority politicians can appear proactive and white Republicans like Mayor Giuliani and Gov. George E. Pataki can trumpet concern for the inner city while promoting free enterprise. 
 But in the absence of other bright spots, the zone has been embraced as at least a concrete hope.
"Is Harlem going to become a flowering oasis overnight?" said Richard D. Parsons, the president of Time Warner, who is chairman of the Upper Manhattan Empowerment Zone Corporation. "Are you going to be able to overcome 60 years of decline? No. But are we going to be able to build some investments that give the community more capacity to deal with its own economic destiny? Yes."
The empowerment funding is not a lot of money, Mr. Parsons said, but it is intended only to jump-start the renewal process. "We're talking about small local businesses," he explained. "Nobody's going to build a G.M. plant up there."
Proponents hope that the initial infusion of money and tax breaks will generate private capital. They say several banks and companies have expressed interest in investing in Harlem, but the hard part will be to teach local businesses how to attract and maintain such interest.
After some philosophical wrangling over the zone's emphasis, public officials now say that it strikes the right balance between stimulating private investment and strengthening social services. Public officials will have veto power over economic proposals and can demand accountability from businesses.
The zone arrives at a sensitive moment in the neighborhood's economic history. In the last year alone, controversy plagued the city's approval of a Pathmark supermarket in East Harlem, and Governor Pataki withdrew $64 million in Port Authority funds for the long-stymied Harlem International Trade Center, which was to be a zone centerpiece. Earlier this month, a clothing store on 125th Street was convulsed by racially charged arson in which eight people died.
It is time, said the Rev. Calvin O. Butts 3d, pastor of the Abyssinian Baptist Church in Harlem, to seize new opportunities, not nurse old wounds. "Let's not curse the darkness now," he said. "Let's run after this candle and see what we can do." ROBIN POGREBIN

HEADLINE: COPING;
The Year in Venting: Why It's Good to Blow Off Steam

BYLINE: By ROBERT LIPSYTE 

 BODY:
YOU live in a city where the Mayor has the courage to raise his own salary while cutting services to the barely coping. The least you can do in return is stand up for quality of life amid quantity of despair, as he does. Resolve in the new year to be one of Rudy's Rangers. Get out and be a monitor in your own nabe.
I will continue my own meager efforts, waving delivery bikers off the sidewalks and barking at dog owners who let their furry toys run loose or do not clean up behind them. I have been sustained in this mission by a trickle of whiny, defensive letters and calls accusing me of being misguided, stupid, hateful, racist, specie-ist and, worst of all, cranky. Like Rudy the Role Model, I gain strength from such criticism, especially since I believe that being cranky is a virtue in this city. 
 There is something assertive and consistent about being cranky; the alternative too often seems to be that passive-aggressive mode in which we sit and take far too much (We're New Yorkers! Suffering is cool and nonsuburban!), then overreact in often hideous ways. There are too many predictable explosions in the city. While we obviously need much more intervention and mediation, until funds are available a little crankiness, a daily venting, helps.
Just to be sure I was as right as I thought I was, I decided to poll Coping's Board of Advisers, which includes, among others, my family, friends, spiritual mentors (Father Vincent J. Termine, Dharma Mittra, Chief Oren Lyons), business consultants (Andrew Rasiej, Cindy Hawkins, Jong Chung), arts aides (Brother Rick Curry, Brendan Canny, Gene Howard, Johnny Colon), wise heads (Ruth K. Goldberg, Eleanor Papa, Al Weaver), inspirations (Beverly Kidd, David Klot, Ed Gallagher), legal counsel (Tonya Frichner, Arthur Schwartz, Judge Leslie Crocker Snyder), shrinks (Kathy Conway, Ilana Reich), Pet Patrollers (Carol Chesler, Peggy Smith, Sandy Rindner) and the Icons of the Nabe (Danny Meyer, three-legged Boots and Anton Dvorak, whose statue will soon be in, not near, Stuyvesant Square Park).
And then I decided not to poll them after all; what if they disagree and I have to fire them?
In any case, I had shaky scientific evidence on my side. My friend John Exel, cabinetmaker and chef, recently returned from a postcardiac stressbuster class and described how blood turns to sludge in times of fear and anxiety; this was in the Original Plan so you wouldn't bleed out after being bitten by a pterodactyl. I asked Mr. Exel if the same sludge effect couldn't result from being scared by a large unleashed dog, or a biker delivering takeout from Zen Palate. Who knows? answered Mr. Exel, which was good enough for me.
Fingering Zen Palate is easy because its riders wear the restaurant's name on their backs. They are not the only food pedalers on my local sidewalks. Then again, I have eaten there and found the food overwrought and overpriced (if you truly love vegetables, why disguise them as hamburgers?). Also, the riders become notably unsocial when I stand my concrete.
The worst offenders among walkers of dogs seem to be, at least in my nabe, those youngish men who look like the kind of yuppies who would order slaughtered baby corn from Zen Palate. Because their unleashed dogs seem to be extensions of their master macho, they tend to yell at my suggestions that they tie up or clean up. It seems that part of their pathetic pose is pretending absolute and exclusive control of their dogs, which is why they walk them loose.
Since I once rode for Western Union, one would think I would have more compassion for the delivery riders, if not the dog boys. But I don't, especially when I see how they all make little old ladies jump and tremble.
Mayor Giuliani must agree with me on that. As I understand his quality-of-life stand, allowing simple, streetside order to get out of hand is to invite a climate of chaos. Everyday people will feel defeated and become passive, and criminals will flourish. I can buy that as a start. But will the Mayor agree that we can't wait for the police -- or for him -- to maintain simple, streetside order unless we force them to, until we make that first move to clear our sidewalks of ice, litter, dog droppings, illegal bikers (if you are over 12, it is illegal to ride on the sidewalk), illegal vendors, prostitutes, menacing bums, zombies and their drug dealers and anything else that threatens our quality of life.
Small victories inevitably lead to the sense that larger victories are possible, that everyday people sweeping their own sidewalks can take political and economic control of their own neighborhoods, hospitals, schools, social services.
If the Mayor agrees with that, then Happy New Year to him as well as to you.

HEADLINE: NEIGHBORHOOD REPORT: LOOKING BACK, LOOKING AHEAD;
The Year in Sex Zones

BYLINE: By ROBIN POGREBIN 

 BODY:
To judge from the relative ease with which the new sex-zone regulations sailed through the Council this fall, one might think that the city had undergone some kind of puritanical transformation.
But critics of the new zoning, which restricts sex-related businesses to certain neighborhoods, say that the success of the legislation is more reflective of Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani's political mastery than the public's personal morality. 
 "This is clearly the Mayor's agenda," said Councilwoman Joan Griffin McCabe of Brooklyn, who voted against the regulations. "It's a very easy way for the Mayor to project that he is concerned about neighborhoods and family. This is an easy way to point the finger."
Under the new regulations, sex-related businesses will be restricted to manufacturing areas and commercial zones and will not be allowed to operate within 500 feet of residences, schools and houses of worship, or within 500 feet of each other. Businesses that violate these rules must close within the year, which could leave as few as 15 establishments, down from 177 citywide.
Not so fast, civil libertarians are saying. They are about to file a lawsuit on behalf of consumers and business owners that challenges the legislation as unconstitutional.
Norman Siegel of the New York Civil Liberties Union said the city had failed to prove that adult entertainment businesses have adverse effects, like a rise in crime or a decrease in property values, and to prove that consumers will maintain reasonable access to such establishments. "There is a chill in the air coming out of City Hall," Mr. Siegel said. "It is antithetical to the rich history of New York City's commitment to an open and robust freedom of expression."
Others object to the regulations on the grounds that they disproportionately affect certain neighborhoods, and that they threaten to shut down sex-related businesses that are important to the gay and lesbian community in areas like Greenwich Village. "It's very easy to beat up on erotica, but that doesn't mean that we should stomp it out," said Bill K. Dobbs, a gay advocate who has led much of the fight against the zoning. "This is the sweeping sterilization of New York City."
City officials who support the zoning, however, say it reflects residents' interests more accurately than its critics suggest. "It says that people are tired of a constantly eroding quality of life," said Charles Millard, who spearheaded the zoning regulations as a Council representative from the Upper East Side and who is now president of the city's Economic Development Corporation.
"I think New York City has always had greater middle-class sensibilities than the liberal Democratic ruling class has understood," Mr. Millard continued. "There are not that many parents who don't care about having their 11-year-old son or daughter walk past some of these stores."
Despite the still-simmering debate and upcoming court challenges, the city seems to feel confident that its new regulations will hold. "We are confident that the zoning meets all legal standards," said Joseph B. Rose, the chairman of the City Planning Commission. "Every community is more protected under these regulations."
 ROBIN POGREBIN

HEADLINE: NEIGHBORHOOD REPORT: LOOKING BACK, LOOKING AHEAD;
Brooklyn's Small Plants Filling Industrial Gap

BYLINE: By MICHAEL COOPER 

 BODY:
The shutdowns of big businesses in Brooklyn this year were well publicized: A. & S., Pechter Field's bakery, KB Electronics, each gone, along with hundreds of jobs. The economic base of Brooklyn, it seemed, was draining away fast.
But behind the headlines is a bit of hope. 
 As large companies continue to close, analysts say, they are being replaced by a host of smaller ones that produce specialty goods like food, clothing and furniture. The hundreds of small manufacturers that operate in the Brooklyn Navy Yard, the Bush Terminal in Gowanus and the Brooklyn Army Terminal in Sunset Park are slowing the rate at which Brooklyn is losing manufacturing jobs.
"Brooklyn has a vast industrial plant which has recently seen a transformation from big manufacturing to small manufacturers," said Mitchell L. Moss, the director of New York University's Urban Research Center. "It tends to be driven by immigrants, and they're making high-value goods, niche goods."
The overall industrial statistics are still sobering. Brooklyn, which had 115,900 manufacturing jobs in 1978, had 51,848 at the start of 1995, according to the State Department of Labor. And more large manufacturers shut down during the year.
Take Pechter Field's. In August, 400 workers lost their jobs when the giant Bedford-Stuyvesant bakery closed after months in bankruptcy. Potential buyers said they were scared off by the high price of doing business in the city.
The same concern led KB Electronics Inc. -- a 135-person outfit in East New York that manufactured motor-speed controls for treadmills, printing presses and pizza ovens -- to pack up and move to Coral Springs, Fla. Gilbert Knauer, the company's president, said that his electric bills are now 40 percent lower, his real estate taxes are halved and his employees do not need to pay state income tax. And he said there is no crime. "Moving was a no-brainer," he said.
But while Brooklyn continued to experience a net loss of manufacturing jobs -- it had 2.4 percent fewer at the beginning of 1995 than it did in the beginning of 1994 -- the rate of loss slowed because of the small companies. "I would argue that it is stable," Professor Moss said, "because so much is off the books anyway."
In addition, former industrial areas are being adapted for businesses outside the manufacturing field. Stretches of Williamsburg, Red Hook and the area dubbed Dumbo -- Down Under the Manhattan Bridge Overpass -- now appeal as much to artists as entrepreneurs.
Typical of Brooklyn's changing industrial scene is the return of a microbrewery to a borough that was once a beer capital. The Rheingold brewery, which once employed 4,000 workers, moved out in 1976 along with Brooklyn's other remaining brewery, F. & M. Schaefer.
Now the Brooklyn Brewery, which makes niche beers like Brooklyn Lager, is building a 25-barrel microbrewery on North 11th Street in Williamsburg. The new plant, scheduled to open by the spring, will have only about 10 employees at the outset, but its owners hope it will grow. "It makes sense to produce the beer here, because New York City is our main market," said Garrett Oliver, its brewmaster. "We can roll directly out of here."
The microbrewery's building itself tells the story of Brooklyn's manufacturing history: in previous incarnations, it was an ironworks, a warehouse and a matzoh bakery.
 MICHAEL COOPER

HEADLINE: New York's Medicaid Cuts Will Cost Working Families More

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Re Edwin S. Rubenstein's "More Medicaid Myths" (Op-Ed, Dec. 26): 
 Gov. George E. Pataki's proposed $2.9 billion health care cuts would devastate the system that New York's working people, their children, the elderly and the disabled depend on. These cuts will come on top of previous heavy reductions and additional cuts by Medicare and managed-care payers.
If Mr. Rubenstein hasn't seen serious damage yet, it's partly because the State Legislature has blocked the most severe cuts. As the economics analyst for National Review, he seems unaware of the health problems of ordinary New Yorkers.
Hospitals and clinics have been cutting staffs and services. Even the wealthiest patient arriving with a heart attack will face a longer wait and reduced services. Some people will die who could have lived. Their families probably won't realize they are sharing the burden of those cuts.
New York spends more on Medicaid than other states, but for several good reasons.
* In New York, Medicaid pays hospitals almost the same rates that Blue Cross does. Other states cheat their hospitals out of fair payment, forcing them to shift the cost to other payers, which raises the price of health insurance.
* We pay for much more home care than other states, which means family members can work instead of staying home to care for elderly parents.
* We include many items in our Medicaid definition that most states pay for through other programs, such as a large part of our mental health budget, partial payments to hospitals for care for the uninsured and support for public hospitals.
Our Medicaid costs have grown no faster than health care inflation generally in recent years.
Two-thirds of our Medicaid dollar pays for care for the elderly or disabled. But they are not "middle class," as Mr. Rubenstein calls them -- although some were before they started spending $40,000 or more a year for a nursing home bed. There are some who have "hidden" assets, but that is a minuscule piece of the pie.
Mr. Rubenstein says we should cut what we pay to hospitals. But New York State has for years tightly regulated hospital prices. Adjusted for wages and case severity, New York hospital prices are the third lowest in the country already.
If Governor Pataki's Medicaid cuts go through, health care providers will have to cut services. Working people and their families can't afford to pay extra to make up for those cuts.
 
RICHARD N. GOTTFRIED
Chairman, New York Assembly
Committee on Health
Albany, Dec. 27, 1995

HEADLINE: THE NATION;
Once Upon a Time, a Budget Surplus

BYLINE: By HAROLD FABER 

 BODY:
EVEN if Congress and the President actually balance the budget in seven years, the Government will still owe $4.3 trillion in outstanding public debt, according to the Congressional Budget Office. But suppose that in succeeding years, the national debt were paid off and the Government even ran a surplus.
This wildly improbable scenario happened once, and only once, in American history. From 1835 to 1837, the United States was not only free of debt, it actually had a surplus in the Treasury. 
 How did it happen and what did Congress do with the money?
The United States started out in 1789 with a debt of $75 million, a large sum for those days, which was incurred during the Revolutionary War. (For 1793, the first year for which complete figures are available, economists have calculated the ratio of debt to gross domestic product as 31 percent, as against today's ratio of 52 percent).
Year by year, the Government paid off the debt until the last installment -- a total of $57,000 -- was paid in 1835. President Andrew Jackson reported to Congress that "all that remains of the public debt has been redeemed," and then he revealed that the Treasury actually had a surplus of $19 million.
Where did the money come from?
In those simple days, before income and corporate taxes, the United States got its money from two major sources: customs duties and the sale of public lands. And there were only five categories of expenses: the Army, the Navy, pensions, payments to Indians and miscellaneous.
In 1836, the first year of a debt-free nation, Washington's income jumped to $50.8 million, mainly because of increased land sales in the West. Expenses rose somewhat, to $30.8 million, leaving $20 million more unspent.
 
Take the Money, Please
 The mounting surplus caused a problem in Congress. There was no easy solution as there might be today, for there were no taxes to cut. After several proposals were considered and discarded, Senator Henry Clay of Kentucky had what seemed to be the best compromise -- distributing the money to the states to use as they pleased, or, in other words, giving out block grants.
But there was a serious problem -- the Constitution. Disposing of surplus money was not one of the powers enumerated for Congress in the Constitution, and, in those days when the Constitution was taken far more literally than it is now, that was a major obstacle.
A proposal to amend the Constitution was discarded in favor of a simpler, more ingenious solution. Congress decided to deposit the nation's money in the states and announced that those deposits would be subject to recall in case of need.
It was a subterfuge recognized by all. "It is in name a deposit, in form a loan, in essential design a distribution," said Senator Thomas Hart Benton of Missouri at the time. "All of this verbiage about a deposit is nothing but a device and contrivance by those who have been for years endeavoring to distribute the revenue."
How much money was involved? On Jan. 1, 1837, after $5 million had been deducted as a reserve fund, there remained $37,468,859 (the accumulated surpluses from 1835 and 1836) to be distributed among the 26 states that then existed. The money was allocated in proportion to each state's electoral vote, in four equal installments throughout the year.
After the third payment, though, the euphoric bubble burst. In late 1837, President Martin Van Buren, who had succeeded Jackson, confronted a financial panic and the prospect of deficits and debt once again.
The panic was another legacy of Andrew Jackson. Before he left office, he had issued his famous specie circular, which required that public lands be purchased with gold and silver instead of paper. In 1836, the land sales brought in a revenue of $25 million; in 1837, $7 million.
 
Deep Pockets Depleted
 That sharp decrease led to problems for the banks that had been lending money for land purchases. Chaos ensued in the nation's financial system and finally the country suffered its first severe depression. By 1837, gross Federal revenue had dropped to $25 million, half of what it had been the year before, and a deficit of $12 million replaced a $20 million surplus.
As a result, Congress stopped the fourth installment of the surplus distribution. But by that time the states had already received a total of over $28 million.
New York, which had the largest population at the time, received the largest amount, $4 million. Pennsylvania got $2.9 million; Virginia, $2.2 million; Ohio, $2 million; North Carolina, Tennessee and Kentucky, each $1.4 million; Massachusetts, $1.3 million; Georgia, $1 million, and the other states lesser amounts.
And what did they do with the bonus money?
Maine distributed it to its citizens on a per capita basis (about $2.02 for each free person, at a time when a dollar a day was a good wage). Two states, North Carolina and Maryland, used the money to help pay off state debts; three states, Alabama, Georgia and Virginia, poured it into state banks; eight states, Arkansas, Illinois, Massachusetts, Michigan, Mississippi, New Jersey, Rhode Island and South Carolina, used the funds to pay state or county expenses, and 12 states Connecticut, Delaware, Indiana, Kentucky, Louisiana, Missouri, New Hampshire, New York, Ohio, Pennsylvania, Tennessee and Vermont, used most of the money for schools.
As predicted at the time, the Federal Government never did ask for the return of any of the money, though it was deep in debt by 1838. And never since then has the nation faced the delightful problem of having more money than it could use.

HEADLINE: No Free Rides;
Generational Push Has Not Come to Shove

BYLINE: By ROBIN TONER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
WAS 1995 the first skirmish in the great generational war? If so, many analysts suggest, Americans have little stomach for it.
For years now, an influential group of academics and political theorists has been predicting this clash: As a great wave of baby boomers begins to retire in the next 15 years, generational tensions will come to drive American politics just as race and class and ethnicity have in eras past. 
 It is a chilling vision of a not-so-distant future, as the first boomers, born in 1946, begin to turn 50 tomorrow: The elderly and soon-to-be-elderly angrily protecting their benefit programs; the young increasingly resentful as programs for the elderly take a greater share of Federal resources; the whole system growing more precarious as the population gets older, putting more and more of a burden on the people still in the work force.
To many on Capitol Hill, the interests of the old and the young already seem at odds in the Federal budget. The Congressional Budget Office, for example, projects that Medicare will grow about 10 percent a year, consuming more than 17 percent of the budget by the year 2005, up from about 12 percent in 1996.
Devotees of "generational accounting," like Lawrence Kotlikoff, an economist at Boston University, warn that even the Republican proposal to balance the budget and trim these benefit programs was far too little and much too late.
Yet the 1995 budget battle, which was dominated by Republican plans to squeeze $270 billion from Medicare, underscored what politicians have long suspected. Policy analysts may see the old and the young locked in an ugly struggle -- epitomized in a Newsweek cover this year showing a weary young man with an old woman in a wheelchair on his back -- but voters generally do not. Behind those nasty, budget-busting benefit programs are the parents and grandparents of the young (a sort of 1990's version of "Don't shoot, we are your children").
Mark Mellman, a Democratic pollster, said: "I think what we've learned about generational warfare is that it's largely a myth. Everything we've seen in our polling is that younger people are just as concerned about protecting Medicare and Social Security as their parents."
Frank Luntz, a Republican pollster and adviser to the House Republican leadership, agreed that there was little evidence of true generational conflict in 1995. "I don't think it's a skirmish because I don't think the younger generation is fighting back," he said.
There are some obvious reasons for this: For all the talk about the breakdown of the extended family, some scholars say, the bonds between the generations remain surprisingly strong. True, the Waltons are no longer the norm: In 1952, 38 percent of the people over 65 were living with their children; 35 years later, that figure had dropped to 20 percent, according to the American Association of Retired Persons (in part, because of Government programs that encouraged independence among the old). But the distance is often only physical; 70 percent of respondents talked to their mothers at least once a week in one recent survey, for example. And the political interests are often shared.
Senator Bob Kerrey, Democrat of Nebraska and a leader in the effort to overhaul benefit programs, describes the attitude of the young toward the elderly this way: "They want them to be secure. They want them to have quality health care. And they don't want them to be dependent on them."
As Mr. Kerrey suggests, there is self-interest at work; curtailing Government benefits for the elderly can translate, quickly and directly, into added burdens -- or at least added worries -- for their families. "Families are already pretty much doing what they can," said Vern L. Bengtson, a gerontologist at the University of Southern California who has studied intergenerational patterns. "Eighty-five percent of all care that is given to dependent older people is from family members."
Mr. Mellman added: "The middle-aged group feels they're part of a sandwich generation. They're caught between parents who need help and kids who need help. They don't want to see programs for either cut."
 
Greedy Geezer? Who Says?
 There are also broader, cultural attitudes that protect the elderly in any struggle over Federal resources. Much of the debate over generational warfare centers on the image of the "greedy geezer," living an affluent lifestyle in some balmy clime, heedless of the generations to come. But Bill McInturff, a Republican pollster who has specialized in Medicare, said this is not the image that emerges from his research with voters.
"When you look at the data about self-described financial security, and who has and does not have money, in the aggregate people over 65 are doing pretty well," Mr. McInturff said. "But the perception in focus groups is that these are people who have paid their dues, paid their taxes, and they're owed. And that they continue to be vulnerable. There is an enormous empathy in this country with wanting to do right by seniors."
All of these attitudes came to bear in the 1995 budget battle, which showed just how subtle and dangerous generational politics can be.
Republicans declared they wanted to balance the budget for the sake of the country's children and grandchildren, warning that deficit spending and out-of-control benefit programs will be crippling to the young. They desperately tried to play down the other part of the generational message: That it was time for old people to sacrifice for the sake of future generations.
 
Political Gains for Democrats
 Republicans took Social Security off the table at the very start, and presented their Medicare restructuring as an attempt to "preserve, protect and improve" the 30-year-old program.
But the interests of the various generations could not be neatly disentangled. Middle-aged parents may have worried about the deficit and its effect on their children, but they also feared what curtailments in Medicare and Medicaid would mean to their parents. The Democrats quickly cast themselves as the guardians of the elderly against a predatory Republican Party, and prospered in the polls with young and old alike.
Pundits and partisans denounced the Democratic campaign as demagogic and argued that Republicans deserved credit for at least tackling the issue of spending on benefit programs. Yet the Republicans' case was weakened because their budget was not a simple intergenerational transfer between the comfortable old and the needy young.
Nor was it simply about insuring the long-term stability of Medicare. Critics argued that the Republican plan would, in fact, curtail many of the existing programs for children and shift new resources, through a tax cut, to the rich.
By year's end, politicians like Mr. Kerrey were still puzzling over how to rally the public on these issues. Because, of course, simple demographics dictate that the issues will not go away. Before the baby boomers begin to hit the retirement rolls in force, most analysts say that Medicare and Social Security will have to be re-examined and, ultimately, substantially restructured.
"I don't think we can afford the contract we now have on the table for the baby boom generation," said Mr. Kerrey, who has proposed legislation that would gradually raise the retirement age to 70 and limit the cost-of-living adjustments. In other words, in the absence of any action, Americans may not be able to avoid an out-and-out generational conflict -- not over their parents and grandparents' benefits, but over their own.
They are hardly in denial about these long-range problems; many are worried that these programs simply won't exist by the time they retire. Mr. Luntz cites a statistic from one of his surveys: 46 percent of 18- to-34-year-olds believe in the existence of U.F.O.'s; only 28 percent believe that Social Security will still be around when they need it.
Yet given Americans' sensitivity to the old, it will require a special kind of politics to carry voters through major changes in these programs, many analysts say. Bipartisanship, of course, always helps. Dividing the nation on generational lines, judging from the evidence so far, will be far less effective in the nation's living rooms than it is in the nation's think tanks.
For all the talk about the fraying of American values, some endure. As former Senator Russell B. Long, Democrat of Louisiana, said 30 years ago, programs like Medicare were created in a powerful and ancient spirit -- the biblical injunction to "honor the face of an old man."
