SECTION: Section 1; Page 1; Column 2; National Desk 

LENGTH: 2249 words

HEADLINE: Buchanan, in Unfamiliar Role, Is Under Fire as a Left-Winger

SERIES: THE CONTENDERS: Sixth article of a series.

BYLINE: By JAMES BENNET 

DATELINE: BATON ROUGE, La.

 BODY:
Those who support Patrick Joseph Buchanan for President find themselves warding off unloved labels these days: "protectionist," "isolationist" and some that are a lot more unexpected.
"I'm no socialist, God knows," said Roy Luxemburg, a former supporter of Ross Perot, explaining that he had nevertheless relished listening to Mr. Buchanan rip into big business during a holiday gathering of Republican voters at an antebellum-style mansion here recently. 
 Something very strange is happening in Republican politics, and Mr. Buchanan's bare-knuckles campaign for the Presidential nomination is at the center of it. Within the party, the debate over economic policy has passed far enough through the looking glass that Mr. Buchanan, whose reviling of liberals built him a career as a Presidential speechwriter and a commentator, has been branded a left-winger by some of his increasingly peeved Republican critics.
To that attack, Mr. Buchanan is unyielding. "The policies I would put into place would attract Democrats and working people by the millions," he said in an interview. "Let me tell you, the group that would be alienated would be some of the financial community and some of the folks heading up the big corporations. They're the people that would be in the back of our bus."
Mr. Buchanan, who as a protest candidate ran unsuccessfully for the 1992 nomination against President George Bush, was expected this time around to vanish in a field jammed with seemingly like-minded conservatives.
Instead, he has mixed his calls for tax cuts and his bedrock social conservatism with a fierce economic nationalism that is rattling not only the Presidential campaign. With his dire warnings of a spreading "new world order," he is widening post-cold-war cracks among Main Street and Wall Street Republicans over foreign and economic policy.
"These fellows are committed to a quasi-religious free-trade ideology that is colliding with reality," Mr. Buchanan said of global-trade advocates, acknowledging that he once shared their view. "What's good for General Motors is not good for America if General Motors has become a transnational corporation that sees its future in low-wage countries and in abandoning its American factories. It's a new world -- a new world economically, a new world in foreign policy."
 
The First Battleground Will Be Louisiana
With his zest for harsh language and a record of infuriating Republican moderates, Mr. Buchanan is still alienating more voters than he is attracting, according to polls. But he has placed second in some polls in New Hampshire and has concocted a message and a strategy, keyed to Louisiana's first-in-the-nation caucuses on Feb. 6, that are causing headaches for his better-organized and better-financed opponents, in particular Senator Phil Gramm.
Mr. Buchanan is betting that a surprisingly strong showing in this economically distressed and increasingly Republican state -- where, he tells voters, he intends to "feed Phil Gramm to the alligators" -- will make him the only viable alternative to the right of the front-runner for the nomination, Senator Bob Dole.
As Mr. Buchanan travels early primary and caucus states like Louisiana, Georgia, Iowa and New Hampshire, he assures voters that Mr. Dole and Mr. Gramm are his friends. Then, ignoring the rest of the field, he proceeds to shred the two Senators with the take-no-prisoners style he honed on the CNN political talk show "Crossfire."
"Bob Dole is our Walter Mondale," Mr. Buchanan told voters in Georgia recently. "He's the weakest front-runner I've seen in a long time."
Although born and reared in Washington, and employed there almost his entire adult life, the 57-year-old Mr. Buchanan mocks Mr. Dole and Mr. Gramm alike as Washington insiders. And, noting his onetime job as "communications director for the Great Communicator," President Ronald Reagan, he offers himself as "the authentic conservative" in the race, planting suspicions that his opponents are closet moderates.
"Pat Buchanan was conservative before Phil Gramm ever said he was conservative, before conservative was cool," said Bryan Joy, a finance major at the University of Georgia who is chairman of that state's College Republicans.
 
The Spoiler of '92 Adds to His Message
Mr. Bush's campaign strategists partly blame Mr. Buchanan for costing them the 1992 election, first by distracting the President in the primaries, then by alienating moderates in rallying the party to a religious and cultural "war" during a prime-time speech at the Republican National Convention.
While he dwells more on economics these days, Mr. Buchanan has not softened his positions or language on social issues. At the Bethel Assembly of God Church in Lafayette, La., recently, he took the stage after a Christmas musical to denounce "the tragedy and atrocity of the systematic slaughter of the life of unborn children across America."
Surrounded by a living Nativity scene -- scores of people dressed as shepherds, wise men or the holy family -- Mr. Buchanan karate-chopped the air with each hand, a trademark gesture, as he mocked bureaucrats "in sandals and beads" at the Department of Education. He accused intellectuals, whom he did not otherwise identify, of replacing the Bible with Playboy magazine in public schools.
"They want the children of America to despise this country's past and its history, the way they despise America's past and its history," he told the crowd of about 1,000, which punctuated his attacks with cries of "Amen, brother!"
"We're going to put back into its rightful place the true God of the Bible," he continued, "and we will drive out of our public institutions the false gods of secular humanism."
He has also repeatedly promised Louisiana voters that he will push the Republican Party to retain its plank opposing abortion and to adopt one against gambling, which he calls "a pact with the Devil."
Mr. Buchanan's language has occasionally landed him in deep trouble over the years: critics from both the left and the right have accused him of anti-Semitism and homophobia. He has repeatedly dimissed the accusations, and his campaign aides say that only a handful of protesters and an occasional reporter have raised the issue this year.
 
Judicial Term Limits And Maybe Trade War
With more time on the trail during this campaign than he spent in 1992, Mr. Buchanan has filled in ideas that he barely touched on then. He would impose term limits on Federal judges, with reconfirmation required of Supreme Court justices every eight years. He would declare a five-year moratorium on all new Federal regulations, while requiring that all existing rules be either formally renewed after five years or dropped. He would also halt legal immigration for five years. And he would require that all federally financed institutions like the Smithsonian "manifest a respect for America's history and values."
Along with cultural war, Mr. Buchanan is now talking trade war. He has seized on his opposition to two accords -- the North American Free Trade Agreement and last year's broadening of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade -- to woo blue-collar voters who backed Mr. Perot in the 1992 campaign. The agreements are a weapon with which he attacks Mr. Gramm and Mr. Dole, who supported both, on two fronts.
The first is political nationalism. Mr. Buchanan declares that international agreements have resulted in "the continued surrender of American sovereignty to the institutions of what I call the new world order," whose members he has identified at various times as the World Trade Organization, the Trilateral Commission, the Council on Foreign Relations and the United Nations.
The second front is economic nationalism. Mr. Buchanan says the trade agreements are costing jobs. "It is immoral and it is un-American to force American textile workers who make $9 an hour to compete with Chinese workers who make 25 cents an hour," he declared at one rally. "Neither party up there seems to be concerned about the working people."
Mr. Buchanan would impose steep tariffs on some of the nation's biggest trading partners, including Japan, which he accused at a meeting in Georgia of having "targeted American industries as well as they targeted Pearl Harbor."
That message can be jarring to Republicans accustomed to hearing their leaders extol free trade. Indeed, it is perhaps a sign of how unconventional Mr. Buchanan's economic views have become that he felt compelled to declare to a supper club of Rush Limbaugh listeners in Shreveport, La.: "I believe in capitalism."
If so, he nonetheless has fundamental qualms about free markets, misgivings that he acknowledges can be traced to his schooling by Jesuit priests at Gonzaga High School in Washington.
"There's no doubt there is an inherent contradiction between conservatism and unfettered capitalism," he said in the interview. "Conservatives ought to be worshiping at a higher altar than the bottom line on a balance sheet. What in heaven's name is it that we conservatives want to conserve if not social stability and family unity?"
Because of free-trade advocacy, he said, such values "are being thrown up on the altar."
"For what?" he continued. "So that I can get 37 varieties of shirts? I mean, what is it all about?"
It is talk like this that led the conservative Weekly Standard to call Mr. Buchanan "America's last leftist."
"Buchanan has moved to the left of President Clinton," declared the article, by the conservative writer David Frum. "He has turned his back on the fundamental convictions that have defined American conservatism for 40 years."
Mr. Buchanan scoffs, noting that even his campaign opponents have not been defending the trade agreements. "If that's their philosophy, why don't they come out and defend it?" he said. "When you've got somebody who no longer defends his position or articulates it, the high ground's been conceded, and eventually you take it."
Indeed, Kevin Phillips, the publisher of The American Political Report, said that within the Republican Party "we've seen the commitment to free trade unravel."
"People just don't believe that's job-creating anymore," Mr. Phillips said. The party would have moved in such a direction without Mr. Buchanan, he added, but "Pat crystallized it."
Republican analysts say Mr. Buchanan is aiming for three core but sometimes conflicting constituencies: blue-collar voters who supported Mr. Perot, libertarians and social conservatives.
But Mr. Buchanan sees the potential for a much broader coalition, arguing that as many as three-fourths of Republicans would prefer a nominee other than Mr. Dole. The problem is that these dissatisfied voters are divided among several candidates. "Chop-chop-chop-chop-chop," he said. "That vote is chopped up five or six ways, and one of us has got to emerge early if we're going to beat Bob Dole."
That is why Mr. Buchanan has seized on Louisiana. This state stole a march on Iowa earlier this year, switching from a Presidential primary in March to caucuses a week before Iowa's, long the nation's earliest. Under pressure from Iowa Republicans, almost all the candidates promised not to campaign here, where Mr. Gramm, of neighboring Texas, is assumed to have locked up Louisiana's 21 delegates.
But Mr. Buchanan, as he gleefully tells voters here, refused to sign the pledge. And now he has what he thinks his campaign needs to catch fire: a head-to-head matchup with Mr. Gramm. "I can't fight him with eight guys there," he said, but now "we've at least got a place to fight."
Mr. Buchanan has all but ruled out an independent candidacy if he does not win the nomination. "I've always supported the Republican nominee, and I intend to this time," he said. Then he paused, grinned and appended an escape clause: "I intend to be him."

SECTION: Section 1; Page 1; Column 4; National Desk

HEADLINE: For Black Women, a Movie Stirs Breathless Excitement

BYLINE: By KAREN De WITT 

 BODY:
"Waiting to Exhale," the movie, is rapidly proving to be "Waiting to Exhale," the event, as African-American women across the country pour into theaters to see the film about the lives of four black professional women.
In a year when events like the O. J. Simpson trial and the Million Man March have focused attention on black men, along comes a movie that centers on black women. The film, the moviegoers say, celebrates the trials and triumphs of black career women -- a much-missed reflection of their lives, their concerns and their desires -- and offers a loving portrait that has nothing to do with racism, interactions with whites, or ghetto life. 
 Groups of women, largely black but including many whites, are buying tickets in blocs and holding sessions to talk about what they have seen. In the theaters, meanwhile, audiences have talked to the screen, with many women shouting "Amen" to the actresses' lines.
"This is our 'Million Man March,' " said Linda Tucker, a speech pathologist at the University of Medicine and Dentistry of New Jersey in Newark. She saw the movie about four contemporary women living in Phoenix last week with a small group and plans a larger outing in the next couple of weeks.
The movie, she added, "is based on a theme around which women can rally and find commonality."
Based on the best-selling novel by Terry McMillan, the 20th Century Fox movie stars Whitney Houston, Angela Bassett, Lela Rochon and Loretta Devine and is the first feature directed by the actor Forest Whitaker. It opened last weekend as No. 1 one at the box office, grossing more than $14.1 million and was expected to do well again this week. The novel, which made the number three spot on the The New York Times hard-cover bestseller list in 1992, is back this week as number three on the paperback list.
"I thought I'd get five or six women, but each time I'd think I had a final count, someone else would call," said Patricia Wheeler-Holmes, 44, a Howard University adjunct professor who issued an invitation to friends in the Washington area to see the movie together.
"I ended up buying tickets and arranging dinner for 41 women," she said.
In New York, a group of 25 black and Hispanic women, including several wives of New York Knicks players, joined an outing organized by Lisa Nkonoki, president of Nkonoki George Entertainment, and Gayle King, a Hartford, Conn., television personality.
Afterward the group went to Ms. King's house for dinner.
"We ate, talked about relationships and danced just like they did in the movie," said Deborah Williams, wife of the Knicks player Herb Williams. "The movie had a universal theme. It was about friendship and bonding and dealing with relationships."
Even though the movie is about black women, Sheila Harper, wife of point-guard Derek Harper, said: "The issues in the movie went beyond socio-economic status and race."
Tom Sherak, executive vice president for 20th Century Fox distribution, said the movie was drawing audiences that were 70 percent female. "It's attracting women 18 to 65, crossing over, up and down, with 65 percent African-American and 35 percent Caucasian audiences" he said.
"But what is amazing is that we're finding that people are buying blocks of tickets and getting together to talk about the movie," he said.
In Houston, Tony Null, manager of the Meyer Park AMC Theater, said one patron bought the whole theater for one screening, 300 tickets, at full price.
"We're doing phenomenal business," Mr. Null said. "We're selling out every performance every day. That's very unusual."
The story picks up a theme about the strained relations between black men and women that was explored in Alice Walker's 1983 novel, "The Color Purple" and Ntozake Shange's 1975 choreopoem, "For Colored Girls Who Have Considered Suicide/When the Rainbow is Enuf." But "Waiting" has provoked far less outcry from men than the two earlier works, which were hard-luck tales of women struggling to assert themselves versus the cads who would hold them down.
The witty, solidly middle-class heroines of "Waiting" seem less victims than shrewd professionals who can be faulted almost as much as their men for doing dumb things for love.
From New York to San Francisco, sororities and church groups, social clubs and book clubs, mothers, daughters, siblings and just plain girlfiends have marshalled themselves into groups, crowding urban movie theaters and filling suburban multiplexes, to see what one woman called "the first black-chick flick."
In Oakland, Calif., Tracy Smith and her mother, Joanna Johnson, who own Tente's Hair N' All beauty salon, went to the movie with 16 of the shop's clients.
"We'd talked about the novel in the shop so we just made it a salon outing because everyone wanted to see that movie," Ms. Smith said.
In Fairfax, Va., a dozen members of the alumni chapter of Delta Sigma Theta wore their crimson and cream sorority colors as a show of support for Ms. McMillan, the movie and "a realistic portrayal of African-American women," said Darlene Faltz, the organizer of the outing.
"We stood around afterward outside for a hour talking about it," said Crystal Dozier, 30. "There were little groups all around the theater doing the same thing."
In fact, some see the movie as a counter to the stereotypical portrayal of black women on the screen -- once described by Sheryl Lee Ralph, a black actress, as "the Fat Mama on the Couch syndrome or the Sassy Sister on the Corner syndrome."
In theaters across the country, women have engaged in a call and response with their cinematic girlfriends. When Savannah (Ms. Houston) tells her mother that she does not need a man to complete her life, several in one audience shouted "Amen to that" and "Tell her, girl." Whoops of delight erupted when Bernadine (Ms. Bassett) wreaked incendiary revenge on the belongings of a husband who dumped her for his very blond secretary. Groans ensued when vulnerable Robin (Ms. Rochon) went for the blandishments of yet another loser. And, at one screening, the audience chanted "Go, Glo. Go, Glo. Go, Glo" when zaftig single mother, Gloria (Loretta Devine) found Mr. Right right across the street.
Linda Burstyn, a former "Nightline" producer who is white, said she enjoyed the interplay between the audience and the movie.
"Having been to black churches, I know there is a real participatory culture so that was part of the fun of seeing the movie," said Ms. Burstyn, 33, who was a fan of the novel. "No movie could live up to my expectations, but it was fun to see, especially the parts of the movie that women reacted to strongest, usually where the women dis the men."
Ms. McMillan, who co-wrote the script with Ronald Bass, said: "Women have been hungry to see themselves accurately portrayed -- that's what it boils down to. But in addition to seeing ourselves on the screen, it's also about fun and entertainment."
Entertainment value is what makes movies successes at the box office, and 20th Century Fox is optimistic that "Waiting" will cross over, as did the book, attracting a white audience as well.
Diane Burr, a real estate broker in Washington, who is white and was with Mrs. Wheeler-Holmes' group, said the film appealed to her, particularly Savannah's declaration of independence from her mother's expectations.
"It's so relatable," Ms. Burr said.
The movie is hardly flawless, but many black women excused inconsistencies like Bernadine, who had an M.B.A. and helped her husband start his business but who remained totally ignorant that their holdings were in his name only.
"It was Arizona with lovely ladies in a gorgeous setting," said Emily Bernard, 28, an associate with the Faith Childs Literary Agency in New York. "The audience just left the movie uplifted. It was refreshing. It was not one extreme or the other.
"It was not Spike Lee, where it's racism, racism, racism," Ms. Bernard said.
Black men who have seen the movie in groups found it entertaining even as they criticized it, comparing it most often to Mr. Lee's "She's Gotta Have It."
Antonio Roberson, a graphics designer in Washington, said he found the depiction of some of the male Mr. Wrongs was all too accurate, but laid the blame at the feet of the women as much as the men. Mr. Roberson saw the movie twice with a group of male friends.
"If you're going out with a married man, that's trouble right there," he said. "I liked the movie, but the women's faults are glossed over."
"I went with an open mind," said Joseph Taylor, an architect who was waiting to get his hair cut at the Industrial Barbers Shop in Washington. "I saw men who were foolish and women who were foolish, doing the dating game. And both getting what they deserved. It was a good movie."
After viewing the film, Mrs. Wheeler-Holmes' group went to an early dinner at a nearby restaurant.
"I would have liked to see the growth of the men as you see with the women at the end of the film," said Saundra Barrett, a manager for a technology company.
Her friend, Valetta Evans, a social worker who had traveled from Richmond, agreed, but said: "I'll see it again, buy the video, because it will probably be another 10 years before there's a movie that has this much of a realistic portrayal of African-Americans."
Back at the theater, another screening of "Waiting" was about to let out. Several ushers readying for the crowd were cautioned by another usher to take off one of two buttons he was wearing that advertised the film.
"These women love this movie and they'll take it off you if they see you've got two," he said. "One woman offered me $25 for mine, but I've promised it to my girlfriend."

GRAPHIC: Photo: The new movie "Waiting to Exhale" is drawing groups of black women and black men, and large numbers of whites as well. One movie executive said, "It's attracting women 18 to 65, crossing over, up and down, with 65 percent African-American and 35 percent Caucasian audiences." (Philip Greenberg for The New York Times)(pg. 25)

HEADLINE: Budget Crisis Emptying Idled Workers' Pockets

BYLINE: By STEVEN A. HOLMES 

DATELINE: HYATTSVILLE, Md., Dec. 29

 BODY:
 There were fewer gifts under Gloria Miles's Christmas tree this year, and her family did without some of their usual Yuletide treats. Now, Ms. Miles is watching New Year's Day approach with little sense of celebration.
That is when the next mortgage payment on her small, red-brick house is due, and Ms. Miles, one of more than a quarter-million Federal workers who have been furloughed in the partial shutdown of the Government, has not seen a paycheck since Dec. 18 and has no idea when she will get another full one. 
 Her gas and electric bills are due as well, and she does not have the money to pay them either. Unless Ms. Miles, 48, is paid soon she also will not have enough in her account to cover checks already sent to pay the telephone bill, credit cards and the tuition for her grandson's martial arts school.
"They're going to bounce," she said, sitting on her living room sofa this week, while her other grandson, James, 5, played with a Spiderman toy. "Insufficient funds."
Congressional leaders have promised that workers will be compensated for their time off. But that pledge does not make up for the cash crunch many are facing.
To many in the country, the wrangling between the Democratic President and the Republican-controlled Congress that produced the partial closure of the Government may seem like a great ideological debate. To others it is political theater. But for furloughed workers like Ms. Miles, it is more tangible and has more immediate consequences.
"I'm a real person with a real situation and this is not a stage," she said, her airy voice belying her anger and frustration. "There is no curtain going up or standing ovation at the end. This is our livelihood. This is something that can be very stressful."
Even at agencies that have been closed, many of the higher-level, and higher-paid, employees have continued to work as emergency employees. The brunt of the two shutdowns is falling on those who can least afford it.
Ms. Miles is particularly incensed by reports that members of Congress, who have continued to be paid, are taking at least 25 foreign trips in the next three weeks.
"This is ludicrous and self-centered," she said. "I can't believe it. While we're sitting back here, stressed out, wondering where our next paycheck is coming from and how we're going to pay the bills, they have the unmitigated gall to want to get out and take a trip to other countries?"
When the Government was first shut down for four days in November, many Federal workers took their unplanned paid days off with nonchalance. But now, as the second shutdown heads into its second week, the mood among many Federal workers has shifted to worry over finances, anger at being made pawns in a political struggle and anxiety about what it all bodes for the future of the Federal work force.
Some fear that the shutdown is a prologue to large-scale layoffs. Others have begun to question their commitment to Government jobs, once considered some of the most secure in the country, and their own self-worth.
"Washington is a city dominated by workaholics," said the Rev. Amitiyah Elayne Hyman, a minister at the New York Avenue Presbyterian Church, which has been providing counseling and referrals to laid-off Federal workers. "So to furlough people is a threat to the core of how they see themselves as beings."
The paycheck Ms. Miles is expecting on Tuesday will cover the week before the shutdown, for which she will be paid, as well as the first week of the shutdown, for which she will not. But because the Government's payroll machinery is geared to two-week paychecks and cannot easily be altered, her regular deductions will be subtracted. She's not sure quite how much will be left, but does not expect that it will be much.
There are already signs that the financial woes of many more Federal workers are intensifying.
Today, officials of the Federal Employee Education and Assistance Fund, a private charity that helps Government workers in emergencies, said the fund was about to run out of money. The fund, which is financed by contributions from Federal workers, has a cash reserve of about $100,000. But it has been overwhelmed by demands for low-interest loans.
For Ms. Miles, life in normal times is already one of a precarious financial balance.
She declined to reveal her salary, but as a document control technician for the Federal Communications Commission in Washington with a GS-7 Civil Service classification she earns $24,441 to $31,770 a year. But she is the primary breadwinner in a household that has seen its share of struggles over the years.
She shares her home, in this suburb just northeast of Washington, with a daughter, Charnika, 26, a single mother, who was laid off last year from her job with a local bank and is now working part time as a nurse's assistant in a nursing home. Also living there is Ms. Miles daughter-in-law Rowena, 26, who was also laid off from her job at United Parcel Service and is attending cosmetology school. The household includes Charnika's son, Jerrell, 9 and Rowena's little boy, James, 5.
Charnika kicks in part of her salary and Rowena's ex-husband pays child support. But without Ms. Miles's paycheck, the family's delicately balanced house of financial cards is in danger of toppling. "I operate from paycheck to paycheck," she said. "There is no savings account to supplement my check. I don't get a check, I can't operate."
Coping in the short term has meant scaling back in Christmas giving. Ms. Miles bought presents for only her two grandsons, and even they did not get much. She bought the two boys a keyboard for the computer they share. She gave Jerrell a Monopoly game, a stretch toy and a basketball and James a puzzle and the Spiderman toy.
And while other relatives including the boy's mothers gave them a few clothes and other gifts, the space under the tree seemed empty. Ms. Miles even dug out a game, Operation, that she gave the boys last year but that they did not really use, and placed it under the tree to make the pile of presents look higher.
Beyond getting through the Christmas season, though, Ms. Miles's plans are less formed. "I guess, number one, I'll make contact with all my creditors and let them know what's going on," she said. "I'll probably apply for unemployment."
She paused and thought, but could not come up with any other way of getting through her predicament.
"I've just not got beyond that," she said, "because I'm hoping it doesn't come to that."

HEADLINE: Bare Pantries In New York, As Cuts Loom

BYLINE: By RACHEL L. SWARNS 

 BODY:
The demand for free food at soup kitchens and food pantries across New York City has surged in the last year, forcing centers to turn away the hungry even as proposed cuts in Federal and state aid further threaten the insufficient supplies.
The independent food bank that supplies the city has faced an escalating demand for emergency food since the middle of 1994, supplying 28 percent more food to the 789 city pantries, kitchens and shelters, food bank officials say. 
 But that has not been enough. The pantries and soup kitchens have been turning away tens of thousands of hungry people a month, local and national surveys show.
And, if budgets under consideration in Washington and Albany are approved, local food agencies expect to lose a quarter of their Federal money and about 40 percent of their state money. This would force many programs to shrink and some smaller ones to consider closing.
"Bottom line, it means that people are going to go hungry and there's a tremendous indifference to all of this," said Judith Walker, executive director of the New York City Coalition Against Hunger, an advocacy group that is to meet next week with representatives of the city's emergency food providers.
Across the nation, in places like Trenton, Denver and San Francisco, officials are reporting increases in the demand for free food. According to a national survey of 29 cities released by the United States Conference of Mayors this month, requests for emergency meals increased by an average of 9 percent between 1994 and 1995, with the biggest growth centered in urban communities.
New York City was not surveyed in the most recent study. But the previous Conference of Mayors report showed that the city's demand for emergency meals jumped by 42 percent between 1993 and 1994, the largest increase reported in the country. And experts say it continues to grow here as elsewhere, fueled by continuing unemployment, homelessness, shrinking welfare checks and cutbacks in government assistance.
"If you are cutting back on general assistance and other welfare programs, you're going to see an increased use of soup kitchens," said Douglas Besharov, a resident scholar at the American Enterprise Institute, a Washington research organization.
In communities with healthy economies, Mr. Besharov said, some of those people would find jobs. But in places like New York, where people continue to apply for food stamps in growing numbers, soup kitchens are likely to face heavier demand.
Emergency food providers across the city say they are already feeling the pinch.
In Bedford-Stuyvesant, Brooklyn, the St. John's Bread and Life soup kitchen says it now serves 1,100 people a day, 15 percent more than eight months ago. In West Harlem, the Community Kitchen says it serves about 650 meals a day, a 23 percent increase over the same time period. In Manhattan, God's Love We Deliver says it serves 950 meals a day, 36 percent more than in September.
And surveys conducted by the Conference of Mayors and local advocacy groups report that anywhere from 33,000 to 50,000 people are turned away from New York City soup kitchens and free food outlets each month.
"We might have to turn away people too," said Hossein Saadat, executive director of St. John's Bread and Life, describing the possible consequences of government cuts in both welfare and food programs. "That's the fear we have."
The soup kitchens, which rely on government funds and private contributions, order supplies almost exclusively from the Food for Survival food bank in Hunts Point, the Bronx, whose sources are Federal and corporate. They receive some food free; the rest they buy wholesale. Their clients include homeless people, the elderly and single parents with children, who wait in line for hot meals at soup kitchens or groceries at food pantries.
This year, New York's agencies served about 90,000 meals a day, and some 30,000 of them went to children.
"Mama doesn't have any food," explained 8-year-old Rachel Goodson, as she tasted vegetable soup at the Love Gospel Assembly soup kitchen in the Bronx.
The family's food ran out last Saturday, and now, she said, only a bag of sugar sits in the refrigerator.
"This is life, honey," said her 38-year-old mother, Theresa, who supports her two daughters with $686 a month in welfare and food stamps. "A week before our checks come, we're broke. I got no food at all."
"This place," she added, "the kids love it, I love it." Ms. Goodson comes often to eat the free lunch and to read the Bible verses on the walls. "It's the closest I can get to God right now. Once you walk out of the building it's all over. Nobody cares. Nobody's trying to help you."
Government officials say that funding cuts to these programs are painful but inevitable in an era when state and Federal politicians are struggling to balance their budgets.
Lynne Lemmer, controller of the Federal Emergency Management Agency's emergency food program, said: "Last year, Congress authorized a $130 million award. This year it's $100 million. We're just passing the cuts along."
That means New York City will most likely receive $3.6 million in 1996, she said, 26 percent less than this year. And the city's share of another Federal program, the Temporary Emergency Food Assistance Program, is expected to shrink by 9 percent.
"They'll just have to gear back what they're doing," Ms. Lemmer said. "They just won't service as many people."
Advocates for the hungry say that the state budget proposed by Governor George E. Pataki would hit the city's agencies even harder. Last year, the budget allocated $10.8 million to the state food programs. This year, it allocates $6.3 million.
The change would mean a 41 percent decrease in New York City's share of the funds, advocates say, leaving soup kitchens with $3.15 million, which they say is far too little to feed the hungry.
A spokesman for the State Department of Health, which administers the Supplemental Nutrition Assistance Program, said those numbers may change.
"Over the next couple of weeks, we have to figure out exactly what is needed," said Claudia Hutton, the department spokeswoman, who emphasized that the Governor does not want to hurt the food programs.
"If it turns out that our more recent data on expenditure patterns show that the number allocated is too low to maintain services," she said, "we'll make an amendment."
The proposed state budget would also turn over the nutrition program's money -- regardless of the amount -- to a general assistance fund to be administered by the Department of Social Services. That department would then distribute the funds in the form of block grants to the various counties around the state, for them to support their indigent communities as they see fit. The money would no longer be solely earmarked for food.
"By creating these block grants," Miss Hutton said, "the counties will be able to design programs that meet the needs of their population as opposed to the old cookie-cutter approach from Albany."
But advocates worry that the money will disappear and that private donations will not fill the gap.
"No white knights are coming through, riding on horses from major charities to give us that money for these people," said Liz Krueger, associate director of the Community Food Resource Center, which runs the Community Kitchen of West Harlem.
"We've been lucky enough to meet the needs of people who come to us," she said. "If all the cuts are approved, we will have to decrease the number of people we serve, and conceivably the number of days a week we're open. I don't believe we would close, but we would shrink."

HEADLINE: California Begins Easing Its Once-Strict Laws on Rent Control

BYLINE: By The New York Times 

DATELINE: SANTA MONICA, Calif., Dec. 30

 BODY:
 New Year's Day will bring a loosening of rent control in this bayside city and four other California cities, under a state law that takes effect on Monday.
Santa Monica, whose nearly 90,000 residents are nearly 80 percent renters, has offered cheap apartments with cool breezes and ocean views since voters approved a 1979 ordinance that controlled the rents of 31,600 apartments here. 
 Under this ordinance -- one of the strictest in the nation -- Santa Monica landlords could raise rents only once a year, and then at by only about half the inflation rate. This meant, landlords complained, that they were not getting a fair profit.
Under the new law, which also affects Berkeley, Cotati, East Palo Alto and West Hollywood, landlords will be able to raise rents on new tenants by 15 percent. Although landlords can take this increase only twice during the next three years, beginning in 1999 they can raise rents on vacant units to whatever the market will bear.
"We didn't want Santa Monica to be a rich, yuppie city; we wanted diversity," lamented Nancy Greenstein, who heads Santa Monicans for Renters Rights, a tenant advocacy group that has made rent control the core of local politics by backing candidates who support it. "It doesn't seem right that representatives from the rest of the state can take it away from us."
The California law is the latest in a series adopted by state legislatures that weaken or eliminate rent control. In recent years, the New York Legislature has eliminated rent regulation of vacant apartments that would rent for more than $2,000 a month and on occupied apartments that rent for more than $2,000 and whose tenants earned more than $250,000 in the previous two years. Moreover, an advisory panel last year recommended to Gov. George E. Pataki that rent regulation be phased out completely.
Only California, Maryland, Massachusetts, New Jersey, New York and the District of Columbia retain some form of rent control, although Massachusetts will phase out all rent controls by the end of next year.
Joan McDowell, who lives in a $450-a-month, one-bedroom apartment just two blocks from the beach in Santa Monica, says rent control enabled her to stay in the Los Angeles area, where she has lived for most of her 67 years. "I never thought I could continue to live in Southern California because the cost of living was so high and I had to live on my retirement income," she said.
While landlords and tenant advocates rarely agree, they both say that Santa Monica apartments rent for $200 to $500 less than they would without rent control.
But landlords have long argued that rent control benefits upper-income renters, rather than those who need it, and discourages maintenance and new construction because of limited rental profits.
Supporting that contention, Moody's Investors Service recently raised Santa Monica's bond rating, predicting that the new law would lead to "significant new investment in the city's rental housing stock."
John M. Rodriguez, a landlord and vocal critic of rent control, says the new law will bring additional revenue for landlords, more construction and maintenance jobs for local residents, and more taxes for state and Federal coffers. Under rent control, he said, "We all lose."
State Senator Thomas E. Hayden, who represents Santa Monica and was propelled into the Legislature by the coalition of voters who supported rent control, says liberals failed to mount an aggressive campaign to preserve it. Liberal Democrats, he said, have become "less interested in bread-and-butter issues."

HEADLINE: Ellis Island May Have Been Made for You and Me, but Who Owns It?

BYLINE: By NEIL MacFARQUHAR 

 BODY:
Judith T. Kramer has a lot of dirt on her mind these days. So does Joseph L. Yannotti.
They are both lawyers, and they are girding for the New Jersey-versus-New York showdown before the United States Supreme Court for control over the dirt in question -- Ellis Island. 
 The Supreme Court decision that emerges from the hearings and legal maneuvering over the next five months should finally put to rest about 200 years of sparring between the states over the island. In the tart haggling over what evidence is admissible and what is not, who is an expert and who a flimflam artist, it seems that the legal stampede for historical evidence has kicked up enough documents to create another island.
The dispute actually swirls around the title to the landfill portions of the island, or roughly 24 of the total 27.5 acres. (New Jersey accepts that the original chunk used to buttress various pieces of landfill, sometimes referred to in court documents as "prototypical Ellis Island," belongs to New York.)
Although the antagonists are marshaling obscure annals of history, law, immigration, geography, tidal flows and marine biology in their arguments, the basic fight boils down to who has the right to tax tourists.
New York, resting its case largely on longstanding historical ties between New York City and the island, has dredged up just about any agency or resident ever linked to the place. The varied players include the New York State Board of Lunacy, the poet Walt Whitman, the anarchist Emma Goldman and a man who appears to be one of the first serial killers executed by the state.
"What are we supposed to do -- carve up the island and free-float the bits?" rumbled Ms. Kramer, a New York assistant attorney general, occasionally glancing out her Manhattan office window at the island as if seeking reassurance that it was all still there. "They are creeping up. What more do they want? Next they'll want the Empire State Building. They can't have it."
New Jersey's case is grounded more on the intent of the initial effort to resolve its unhappiness with colonial charters. These set the border between the states on its bank of the Hudson River. An earlier unresolved Supreme Court case prompted an 1834 agreement to redraw the border line down the middle of the Hudson River. But New York retained jurisdiction over Ellis Island even though it was west of the line, while New Jersey got the underwater rights.
"We consider the filled portion of Ellis Island part of New Jersey," said Mr. Yannotti, a New Jersey assistant attorney general, "and we want that to be established."
The Federal Government took possession of Ellis Island for the famous immigration processing center built in the 1890's and started a massive landfill in the early 1900's to create an area for a hospital. At the time, New Jersey demanded that its stake be recognized; the Government paid it $1,000 for the right to complete that landfill, which constitutes the southern portion of the U-shaped island.
The question now is which state gets to exercise administrative and political jurisdiction over the various landfill bits. At present, New York gets all of the tax revenue, as well as almost exclusive use of the main symbol of the American immigration experience.
The sales tax from food and souvenirs amounted to $430,000 last year, said Richard Wells, a Federal parks official on Ellis Island. Court documents indicate that in 1993, employees paid $1.2 million in income taxes; New York has also collected tens of thousands of dollars in corporate franchise taxes for construction.
The sums at issue now are relatively small. (If New Jersey won, for example, it could claim only the sales tax from cash registers believed sitting on landfill.) But New Jersey wants to stake a claim to future economic benefit if long-postponed restoration plans lead to a hotel or convention center on the island. Winning would also strengthen New Jersey's hand in trying get a pedestrian bridge built, thereby siphoning off a larger share of the three million annual visitors to Ellis Island and the Statue of Liberty.
The Supreme Court, mandated to settle territorial disputes between states, appointed a law professor, Paul R. Verkuil, to make a recommendation in the case. Hearings are to start May 6 on the island itself. They were initially set for November, but were postponed largely because of the Alpine proportions of the documents.
The teams pawed through thousands of pages of old deeds and maps and lists in the National Archives, The New-York Historical Society, The New York Public Library, and the New Jersey Historical Society, plus at least half a dozen university collections.
"None of the participants are around anymore," Mr. Yannotti said, "so essentially this whole story is going to be told through the documents."
Interested in royalty? Read the June 24, 1664, charter from the Duke of York granting what is now New Jersey (sans islands) to two men, Lord Berkeley and Sir George Carter. Like maps? Pore over dozens, including the 1776 Ratzer Map published in London showing the islands within New York's boundaries or the early 20th century maps from the U.S. Department of War titled "Ellis Island, N.J."
No agency is too obscure, no detail too small.
New York's evidence includes excerpts from the Walt Whitman poem "Mannahatta": "The flowing sea -- currents, the little islands, larger adjoining islands,/ the heights, the villas,/The countless masts . . . City nested in bays! my city." Supposedly further buttressing New York's claim is that it used Ellis Island to hang a felon named Charles Gibbs after a life of pirate crime that included 100 murders.
Marriage licenses, death certificates, psychiatric exams, voting rolls -- even the view is part of the fight. New York argues that incoming immigrants stared almost exclusively toward its skyline. "Those looking at New Jersey were few and far between," its court papers say. And those being booted out via the island, like Emma Goldman, tried to block their expulsion through Federal District Court in Manhattan.
The basic documents from New Jersey fill twin binders, each as thick as the combined Manhattan yellow and white pages. A United States Government letter dated May 17, 1808, that was extracted from the archives at the Ruth Lilly Medical Library at Indiana University ridicules the idea of paying $10,000 for an island worth $4,500 at most. Pages and pages detail a decision during the Depression to hire bricklayers and other laborers from New Jersey for work on the island. And when anyone died, the undertaker came from New Jersey.
New Jersey is a little bit secretive about its case, agreeing to detail only a few greatest hits in its evidence and barring a reporter from listening to the cross-examination of the 10 expert witnesses. New Jersey is also a little bit dismissive about all the New York documents.
The lawyers often played counterintelligence games in the archives, scouring lists to see where their opponents searched and then digging through the same boxes.
Ms. Kramer went so far as to try to prove that the actual dirt in the landfill was part of New York City, excavated during the construction of the subway system. She could find no proof for what seems to be a long-cherished piece of urban folklore. The lone reference she uncovered was a speech by a Federal official poetically describing the landfill as ballast from the ships of many nations.
"What bunk!" Ms. Kramer said. "What bunk! He makes this speech like it is mud from France and mud from Italy and lava from Mount Vesuvius and I don't know where."

HEADLINE: Times Change. Times Square, Too.

 BODY:
The ball that drops above Times Square at midnight to mark the New Year will be sparkling new for this occasion. Down below, America's best-known intersection is undergoing a renewal of its own, from run-down seedy to modern glitz.
Mayors and urban esthetes have yearned for years to spruce up the area -- where Broadway intersects with Seventh Avenue in mid-Manhattan -- but only now is the transformation visible to the naked eye. Some people, no doubt, regret the passing of the tawdriness and sleaze, but even those elements survive in limited quantity. More people will be attracted to the brighter lights, bigger spectacles and cleaner, safer streets. 
 New Year's Eve is a key moment. Weather permitting, or even if it is forbidding, upward of a quarter-million people typically jam the blocks north of 43d Street to watch a lighted ball slide down a pole on the roof of No. 1 Times Square. One-quarter of them will hail from foreign lands, such is the lure of New Year's Eve in Times Square, and millions of would-bes will watch on TV.
The ball belongs to the building, but the 90-year-old midnight ritual is now in the hands of the Times Square Business Improvement District, a neighborhood coalition of businesses -- including this newspaper -- and real estate interests. This year the ball will glitter and gleam as never before.
The principal forces behind the Times Square upgrade are the state and city governments, the Times Square B.I.D. and a burst of new enterprises. The newcomers are as small as a candy store, as big as Walt Disney and as theme-driven as the Official All Star Cafe, a big sports restaurant and gift shop that opened just before Christmas.
 Alas, there are some obstacles to ideal development, among them the 50-year-old armed forces recruiting office -- an eyesore that squats on an island in the middle of the square. The Pentagon plans a new, livelier design. Better it should yield the site entirely and unclog the square.
The New York Convention and Visitors Bureau is planning a new tourist information center that will clog the same island. It will be so small -- 1,000 square feet -- that any visitor might well ask why the self-styled "capital of the world" cannot do better. It can and should, by arranging for a bigger space alongside, not in the middle of, the square. That requires more money, either from City Hall or private contributors. The city gives the bureau only $3 million a year, a paltry contribution for an economic sector that generates billions for business and hundreds of millions in taxes.
The need in Times Square is clear. The interested parties, including City Hall, should join in planning what should be where, rather than making decisions piecemeal. Open space among the skyscrapers is too precious to be cluttered with kiosks.
The new Times Square is not the old one that lives in memory, and never will be. The new showiness will inevitably drive up rents and drive out some good neighbors along with the crummy ones. But any urban conversion as massive as this one is bound to be imperfect. The city, the state, the private developers and the businesses and theaters in the neighborhood must take care to see that the essential excitement is not dimmed and, above all, that Times Square is a welcoming place.

HEADLINE: Foreign Affairs;
14 Big Macs Later . . .

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN 

 BODY:
This ends my first year as foreign affairs columnist, a wonderful job for which The New York Times actually pays me to be a tourist with an attitude. Reflecting on this past year (during which I ate McDonald's in 14 different countries and can report that they all REALLY DO taste the same), I have a few impressions:
Free trade and an integrated global market are inevitable, but they spread their blessings unevenly: When I was last in Mexico City, I kept seeing a man and his two children dressed in clown outfits, entertaining motorists at a stoplight and begging for handouts. I wondered: What could be more degrading? That man represents for me all of the "left-behinds" -- all those who have not shared in the benefits of the global marketplace. 
 The brutal global competition for investment dollars and trade is forcing companies and workers to tighten their budgets and constantly upgrade their skills in order to compete. Those who have the savvy to keep up are thriving; those who don't are panhandling at stoplights. Unfortunately, this same global competition is also forcing governments to downsize, and they are doing it by shredding their social safety nets and junking their welfare programs. So the left-behinds are left in free fall.
The political resurgence of the Communists in Russia and Poland, the Welfare Party in Turkey, the unions in France, the unemployed in China, are all signs that the left-behinds are starting to challenge the winners. In other words, now that the free market is triumphing on a global basis, the most interesting conflicts are between the winners and losers within countries. It is these internal battles that will increasingly shape international affairs.
Never trust a country with high walls and tinted windows: One of the most striking things about Mexico and Haiti is how the wealthy live behind high walls with tinted windows. What those walls and windows say is not only "Keep Out." They say, "I don't care about you on the other side." That absence of a sense of community, that absence of a sense that we are all in this together, is going to seriously inhibit these states from developing into stable democracies -- particularly as economic forces widen the gap between their winners and losers.
Democracy is great, but trees are better: Haitians are so poor they chopped down most of their forests for firewood. This resulted in massive land erosion, which filled the Haitian coastline with silt and ruined the fishing. Treeless, the Haitian mountainsides heated up more quickly and that altered the weather. Rainfall patterns changed and rivers dried up.
During the recent Haitian elections, I watched a Haitian man slaughtering a pig outside a voting booth. He wasn't the least bit interested in voting. He was interested in selling his pig. It's great that the U.S. is promoting elections in Haiti, Bosnia and Gaza, but what these people need just as badly are pigs to slaughter and trees to plant. If we don't invest in their economic development, these new democracies will erode as fast as a Haitian hillside.
No country heals in a year: During the cold war Americans were ready to sacrifice for foreign policy because its aim was fighting Communism, which was both a vital interest and an idealistic cause. Increasingly, though, U.S. foreign policy has focused on healing failed states -- Somalia, Haiti, Bosnia, Mexico -- where we have some important interests, but where we are driven more by an idealistic impulse to help others in distress at a time when America alone has the power to make a difference.
To sell this new mix of realism and idealism to an ambivalent U.S. public, the Administration has said it would only send some soldiers or some money for a year or so, to give these states a chance to straighten themselves out. Then the U.S. will leave -- whether they succeed or not. That approach was easy in far-away Somalia. But Haiti, Bosnia and Mexico are different. You can't just give them the old college try and walk away: Haitians can sail to Florida, Mexico owes us $12.5 billion and Bosnia is now a huge NATO issue. In 1996 Americans will discover that 12 months isn't enough time to make a lasting difference in these ailing countries, but walking away isn't an option either. That's why how to help these countries over the long term, at a price the U.S. public will tolerate, will be the next big U.S. foreign policy debate.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: COLLEGE FOOTBALL;
A Big Purple Haze Covers Hollywood

BYLINE: By TOM FRIEND 

DATELINE: PASADENA, Calif., Dec. 30

 BODY:
They have sportcoats, E-mail and no suntan. Northwestern will not be playing its look-alike in the Rose Bowl; Harvard isn't eligible.
The Wildcats are here for the first time in a half-century, thanks to either running back Darnell Autry or their longtime alum, Moses. A year ago this week, one of their leading tacklers was giving Penn State a Disneyland tour; a year ago this week, the school had only a top 5 ranking in theater and communications; a year ago this week, the city of Beverly Hills was not painted purple. 
 "A year ago, we'd take a lot of back-handed slaps," said the junior defensive end Casey Dailey. "Like, 'Northwestern -- by the national anthem, they're already down two touchdowns.'
"Of course, now I hear they're calling this Rose Bowl, 'Revenge of the Nerds.' "
Alumni are not only coming out of the woodwork for Monday's game against Southern California, they are coming out of Mount Sinai. Charlton Heston, the star of "The Ten Commandments" who spent two years at Northwestern before enlisting in the armed forces in World War II, parted the Red Sea again Thursday, especially for the team.
The Northwestern players were touring Universal Studios that day, found their tram stranded in front of a man-made lake and heard a voluminous voice.
"Fear not, old Wildcats!"
Heston appeared, once again portraying Moses, and actually carrying the original staff from the movie. "Let my Wildcats go," he said, and, on cue, the lake parted (Universal does this five times a day) and turned purple.
The actor subsequently presented Coach Gary Barnett with a stone tablet, inscribed with a play from scrimmage and a commandment.
"Thou shall not lose," Heston told him.
"I should bring this to practice," the coach answered.
Heston, later mobbed by reporters, revealed he had not seen a Northwestern game in person since Otto Graham was the quarterback and was even posed a question as Moses.
"If God can help Northwestern win," Heston was asked, "can he make Nebraska and Florida tie?"
Actually, there is an overtime provision for this year's bowl games. But the Northwestern players were amused, although, by now, they already knew they were Hollywood's team. The city of Beverly Hills had earlier organized a Northwestern Day, and, it turns out, half of the Screen Actors Guild seems to have a tie to Evanston. For instance, David Schwimmer, an alum and star of the sitcom "Friends," attended a Rose Bowl practice, and Cindy Crawford, the model, was featured on a team highlight film.
"On tape, she wished us, 'Good luck,' " Dailey said. "Seriously, she went to Northwestern for a while. I think she took one econ course and decided enough was enough. She figured out supply and demand and bailed. I mean, she realized her earning potential at Northwestern didn't compare to being a supermodel. I'd be gone, too."
The team, meanwhile, has persistently treated Dailey as a tour guide because he is one of their few native Californians. Last December, in fact, he was working part time at Disneyland when Penn State arrived there for its Rose Bowl festivities.
"I was dressed up like Indiana Jones," Dailey said. "Um, I didn't exactly make it known I played for Northwestern."
Other than two-a-day practices here, the team has been preoccupied mostly with eating and O. J. Simpson.
They attended the 40th annual Beef Bowl at Lawry's Prime Rib restaurant, where they outate and outdressed U.S.C.
"I must say," said the restaurant executive Richard Frank, "I don't think I saw one suit and tie on any of U.S.C.'s players."
The team was also fascinated to know that Marcia Clark, the prosecutor in the Simpson case, used to be a Lawry's waitress. Half of them, earlier in the week, had already stopped by the Simpson crime scene on Bundy Drive and by Simpson's home in Brentwood.
Because it is semester break, no one brought any books -- not even the team academic, Ryan Padgett. The players unanimously voted the senior guard as most likely to be a brain surgeon, and Padgett does not dispute them.
"They think that because I don't go out with them at night most of the time," Padgett said. "Because I don't play Sega 24 hours a day with them."
But every team member is definitely computer-fluent -- or else.
"Are you kidding me?" outside linebacker Geoff Shein said. "At Northwestern, if you don't have E-mail, you might as well not go there. They won't even admit you."
Which is why when they were on the road this season, Michigan fans suggested they accept a bid to the Brain Bowl.
"I'd rather be called brain than hooligan or convict," linebacker Pat Fitzgerald said.
Nevertheless, they are 10-1, ranked No. 3 nationally and still underdogs to an underachieving 8-2-1 U.S.C. team. They own road victories over Notre Dame and Michigan, but the miracle label apparently cannot be removed without surgery.
"You know," Barnett said, "they gave us a T-shirt that said, 'Do you believe in miracles?', and it had 'Notre Dame 15, Northwestern 17' on it. Well, we immediately took those shirts and put, 'This was no miracle' on the back. That's how we feel."
On the other hand, since Moses is already here and has a front-row seat, the players do have a simple request.
"Think he can part the U.S.C. defense, too?" Dailey said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Northwestern players crossing Universal Studios' Red Sea Friday. The Wildcats will play Southern Cal in the Rose Bowl tomorrow. (Jan Sonnenmair for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
Yanks Give Rogers Rich Deal For 4 Years

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS 

 BODY:
Evidently opting for age over economics, the Yankees yesterday added Kenny Rogers to their starting pitching rotation in an attempt to keep pace with the busily building Baltimore Orioles.
The Yankees announced no terms, but they are believed to have given Rogers a package in the neighborhood of $19.5 million for four years plus an option for a fifth year. 
 The fifth year will become guaranteed, it was believed, for $5.5 million if Rogers pitches 200 innings in the fourth year. That's the type of formula the Yankees put in David Cone's $19.5 contract for a fourth year, and that's the provision the free agent Rogers had sought from his previous employer, the Texas Rangers.
In reaching agreement with the 31-year-old left-hander, the Yankees eschewed the opportunity to sign another free agent left-hander, Chuck Finley. Finley would have cost less, $14 million for three years, but he is two years older than Rogers.
"I was flopping back and forth," Manager Joe Torre said in a telephone interview when asked why the Yankees chose Rogers over Finley. "I had plus things on both sides. There was two years age-wise but leadership qualities on the other side. A guy like Rogers has a lot of weapons to go after you with. Chuck has been a mainstay on that staff, a grinder. It was a tough call."
Rogers immediately becomes the No. 2 starter in Torre's rotation behind Cone. Andy Pettitte, Dwight Gooden and Scott Kamieniecki will be the other starters, with Jimmy Key and Melido Perez looking for spots when they prove themselves fully recovered from injuries.
George Steinbrenner, the Yankees' principal owner, set out determinedly to get another pitcher after the Orioles snatched David Wells, whom he wanted, in a trade with the Cincinnati Reds last week.
Negotiating with Rogers's agent, Scott Boras, Steinbrenner, according to a member of his cadre of advisers, quickly offered four years, a term Boras had been seeking in an era when few clubs will give a pitcher four years, let alone a reachable five.
Eighteen other starting pitchers have signed new contracts as free agents, and none gained more than three years. It was questionable that anyone else would have given Rogers four years.
The Rangers, for whom Rogers has played his entire seven-year career, offered four years and $17.5 million earlier this month, but Rogers and Boras rejected it, countering with a request for $19.25 million for four years plus an option for a fifth year that would vest for $5.5 million at 200 innings pitched in the fourth year.
According to the Steinbrenner adviser, the owner made a mistake offering four years so quickly, but he certainly caught the attention of the pitcher and the agent, who spent three days in Tampa, Fla., while they hammered out the deal.
The adviser said negotiations with Boras became tense at one point and threatened to break down.
Finley and his agent, Randy Hendricks, also met with Steinbrenner but left Tampa on Friday night. At the time Hendricks left, he had apparently not been told that the Yankees had made their choice. Later in the evening, Boras said the Yankees told him they no longer were negotiating for Finley.
Hendricks did not return telephone calls seeking comment on the talks or the Yankees' decision. Steinbrenner and General Manager Bob Watson also did not return calls.
Just before leaving Tampa to return home yesterday morning, Boras acknowledged the two sides had a "central theme worked out" but said some issues remained to be resolved. He said they probably wouldn't talk again until Tuesday or Wednesday.
One of the matters that remained or was the last item worked out was the deferral of part of Rogers's salary in the first couple of years of the contract.
Rogers was basically a relief pitcher for most of his seven years in the minor leagues and his first four years in the majors. But he became a full-time starter in 1993 and has compiled a 44-25 record and 3.95 earned run average in his three years in that role.
Last season, he had a 17-7 record and a 3.38 e.r.a., fifth lowest in the American League and his first sub-4.00 e.r.a. as a starter.

HEADLINE: NOTEBOOK;
Budget? What Yankees Budget?

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS 

 BODY:
Damn the payroll, full speed ahead.
Taking a cue from Admiral David Glasgow Farragut, who was associated with ships about 100 years before he was, George Steinbrenner sent that signal late last week as he took off in full-blown, high-speed pursuit of a free agent left-handed pitcher. 
So what if signing Kenny Rogers will cost $5 million a year or close to it? If that's what it would take to keep up with the Angeloses and the Gillicks, so be it.
Payroll, after all, is in the pocketbook of the spender. The owner or general manager who says his team can't afford this player or that most often means he doesn't want to spend that kind of money.
A few weeks ago, Bob Watson, the Yankees' general manager, said the club didn't have enough money to sign both David Cone and Jack McDowell. A flurry of Baltimore Orioles moves later, the Yankees have enough money to sign Cone and Rogers.
The other day, when the Yankees announced the acquisition of Tim Raines, Watson was asked if he had reached his budget. "I'm close," he said. Evidently, he was speaking in relative terms. Moreover, he never has said what his budget is. The Yankees apparently have enough room in the budget for another $5 million pitcher.
That's why, when he was asked if the Yankees would seek another starting pitcher, Watson could say, "If it makes good baseball sense, good economic sense, I'll listen." And when he was asked if the Yankees would pursue another free agent pitcher, he could say, "I won't rule out anything."
When Watson was answering those questions, Steinbrenner already had met with Rogers and his agent, Scott Boras, in the Yankees' winter headquarters in Tampa, Fla. General George Washington at Valley Forge; General George Steinbrenner in Tampa. Admiral Farragut at Mobile Bay; Admiral Steinbrenner in Tampa.
Last season, the Yankees had a baseball-record $55 million payroll. Realistically, it was $6 million less because Cone's two months cost the club only about $2 million, not the $8 million that was attributed to the Yankees under the method used by the clubs and the Players Association to compute payrolls.
The 1996 payroll has reached $37 million for 14 players, including Jimmy Key, whom the Yankees expect to be on the disabled list at the start of the season. The five players eligible for salary arbitration are expected to emerge with salaries totaling $8 million to $9 million, raising the total to about $46 million for 19 players.
Throw in $1 million or slightly more for six younger players, like Andy Pettitte and Derek Jeter, add $5 million or so for Rogers, and the payroll, without bonuses earned during the season, will be about $52 million, or $3 million more than the end of the season payroll less the inflated Cone charge.
"We had the highest payroll in baseball and lost a lot of money," Steinbrenner said earlier this month. "That's O.K., but you don't want to keep doing it forever."
The owner's all-out effort to add a pitcher surprises no one; executives from other clubs predicted it as soon as the Orioles beat the Yankees to David Wells last Tuesday. But the puzzling part of the Yankees' off-season pitching plans is the way they shunned McDowell.
"This one was a decision made by the baseball people," Steinbrenner said. "The business side entered into it some."
The Yankees, according to a club official, didn't want to offer McDowell more than one year because they felt he had a chronic hip problem that eventually could undermine his pitching ability. But if they had offered him salary arbitration, the worst scenario they would have faced was his acceptance.
In that event, they wouldn't have had to sign him for more than one year, and his salary most likely would not have exceeded his 1995 pay of $5.4 million by much. That one-year salary would have been far cheaper than the long-term commitment they are making to Rogers.

 
 
Raines vs. Henderson
 Tim Raines, who long has felt he has taken an unwarranted back seat to Rickey Henderson, laughed but was offended the other day when a reporter on the Yankees' conference call announcing Raines' acquisition mentioned that Henderson was a couple of years younger than Raines.
Henderson, in fact, observed his 37th birthday on Monday; Raines won't put that number next to his name until Sept. 16, two weeks before the end of next season. The Yankees could have pursued Henderson, a free agent, to play left field but opted to put Raines there.
Henderson is the career stolen base leader with 1,149. Raines is fourth (post-1900) with 777.
"Tim's a switch-hitter," General Manager Bob Watson said, explaining his reason for choosing Raines. "He's a better fit in our lineup. I did not seriously consider Rickey mainly because I didn't think it would fit budget-wise."
Henderson, who has played his entire 17-year career in the American League, has $4 million guaranteed contract the next two years with his new team, the San Diego Padres. The Yankees have guaranteed Raines $2.8 million for the next two years.

 
 
Praise for Ochoa
 Livan Hernandez, who defected from Cuba last summer, is the most attractive 20-year-old free agent pitcher looking to begin his professional career in the United States. With a fellow defector, also a pitcher, he has been visiting cities of prospective teams.
When Hernandez was at Yankee Stadium on Friday, he said, in response to a question, that when he was pitching in the Dominican Republic winter league the past month or so, the most difficult hitter for him to get out was Alex Ochoa, the 23-year-old outfielder the Mets obtained from Baltimore in the Bobby Bonilla trade on July 28.
"He is a very intelligent contact hitter with power," Hernandez said through an interpreter.
Ochoa batted .297 in 11 games with the Mets in the last two weeks of the season but had only one extra-base hit, a double, among his 11 hits.

 
 
A Different Pitching Rotation
 The game of musical free agent pitchers is in full swing.
Randy Myers, the Chicago Cubs' closer, moved to Baltimore; Doug Jones, the Orioles' closer, signed with the Cubs.
Al Leiter left Toronto for Florida, and when Boston signed Tom Gordon of Kansas City, Erik Hanson, who worked for the Red Sox last season, went to Toronto.
Cleveland signed Jack McDowell instead of re-signing Ken Hill so Hill signed with Texas when the Rangers were unable to sign Kenny Rogers, who now will move to the Yankees.
Pitching clients of Scott Boras always seem to be changing dugouts. This off season, Kevin Brown has moved from Baltimore to Florida and Andy Benes from Seattle to St. Louis. Rogers is on his way to New York, Jim Abbott is a week away from having his negotiating rights expire with the California Angels, Seattle has let Tim Belcher leave and the Orioles did not tender a contract to Ben McDonald.
Often Boras's pitchers change teams in their -- or his -- quest for top dollar. That, of course, is their prerogative, but the philosophy doesn't always work to the pitchers' benefit.
Three years ago, when Abbott was two years away from free agency, the Angels offered him $16 million for four years, but Boras rejected the offer and sought another million or two. The Angels, instead of continuing to negotiate, abruptly traded a stunned Abbott to the Yankees, where he didn't want to be.
Abbott had two disappointing seasons with the Yankees, then split the past season with the Chicago White Sox and the Angels after the Yankees chose not to tender him a contract.

HEADLINE: MUTUAL FUNDS;
Tracking the Dow, but Trailing Its Gains and Lacking Investors

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

 BODY:
GIVEN the popularity this year of index funds, several dozen of which mimic the broad United States stock market, an index fund linked to the Dow Jones industrial average sounds like a sure hit.
And an entrepreneur with that slice of the market almost to himself sounds lucky indeed. 
 Not so, says a rueful Steven H. Adler, who runs the struggling ASM Fund in Tampa, Fla. The tiny fund, whose name is an abbreviation for America's Stock Market, bills itself as the only mutual fund to mirror the Dow industrials, the gauge that's almost always invoked when somebody asks "How's the market?"
ASM's strategy is simple, to buy an equal number of shares in all 30 industrial companies in the Dow Jones average; its minimum investment is very modest, $500 or $1,000, and it has no sales fees.
After nearly five years, though, the fund has yet to attract a significant following. Even at the end of a year in which the Dow average soared, ASM needs to swell to five or six times its $13 million in assets just to break even and end the need for subsidies from Vector Index Advisors, controlled by Mr. Adler. ASM has been much larger -- with up to $85 million in assets -- but most of that growth came in the form of hot money from investment advisers who put clients into the fund for short spells. When the money fled, it proved disruptive and costly to the fund. Now ASM imposes a stiff fee -- 0.75 percent on the first $100,000 -- on anyone who makes more than six exits a year.
Despite being largely ignored, Mr. Adler has hopes of making his company into a mini-version of the Vanguard Group, of Valley Forge, Pa., the huge funds complex that is known as an indexing pioneer.
While the fund industry's main trade group, the Investment Company Institute, counts some 70 index funds, the Vanguard Index Trust 500 Portfolio dominates the category, with more than $17 billion of assets, and has attracted a rush of money this year. The fund is tied to the Standard & Poor's 500 index, composed of leading companies in each industry and weighted by their market capitalization.
"We really consider Vanguard the only other active fund company looking for index business," Mr. Adler said in an interview. Charles Schwab & Company, the discount brokerage firm, has created its own index of 1,000 stocks, but the industry in general frowns on indexing because it undermines the marketable notion that smart managers can outperform robots.
Mr. Adler, whose early training was as an actuary, certainly makes no claim of being able to "outfox" the market, as he puts it. But he says he thinks he has found in the Dow industrials a useful, even superior, alternative to the S.& P. 500. He figures the 30 Dow stocks operate in some 70 industries and get about half of their sales from abroad, providing plenty of diversification.
The Dow and the S.& P. have a 95 percent statistical correlation, Mr. Adler said, meaning that only 5 percent of the time they move in opposite directions. But over time there is a less than a percentage point of difference in their performance, an amount he considers trivial. With that in mind, the Dow should be favored, he said, in part because it is less volatile in the short run.
A related attraction of the Dow industrials, which now include service companies like American Express, Disney and J. P. Morgan, is that the index enjoys what Mr. Adler calls a "qualitative edge," because it is periodically updated with thriving companies. Although the composition of the S.& P. 500 is also fluid, its changes are based chiefly on market capitalization.
Gus Sauter, who oversees Vanguard's equity index funds, contends that indexing to the Dow means an arbitrary allocation of the investment by price, reflecting the fact that more money goes into high-priced stocks than into cheaper ones.
Moreover, he says, "There is the necessity to rebalance whenever there's a stock split or spinoff, thereby creating artificial turnover for no clear portfolio objective."
Mr. Adler counters that splits are easily handled and that neither gauge is more arbitrary than the other.
When ASM accumulates enough money to buy 100 shares of each stock in the Dow average, now about $170,000, it buys. Because the average is weighted by price, a $1 rise in I.B.M. at $92 has the same impact on the average as a $1 rise in Bethlehem Steel at $14.
ASM did trail the Dow in 1995, however, posting a gain of about 27 percent (28.5 percent with dividends reinvested), compared with the Dow's 33 percent advance.
Mr. Adler attributed the drag to relatively high expenses, because the puny fund lacks the economies of scale of a bigger fund, and to the portion of money uninvested -- at one time 5 percent, but now 2 percent -- to meet possible redemptions. Expenses now total 1.1 percent of assets, but at $50 million in assets, Mr. Adler says, the fund's expenses would fall to 0.75 percent.
Marketing has been hampered by the fact that Dow Jones & Company does not permit others to use its averages or to market a Dow Jones Fund. Mr. Adler has also resisted adding a sales charge that would give brokers an incentive to sell the fund's shares to investors.
A native of Jersey City, Mr. Adler, 57, sold his New York pension consulting firm in 1983 -- because of "the cold, the noise and the hassle" -- and moved to Florida. After a brief stint with brokerage houses that have since vanished, he decided in 1989 to create a fund tracking the Dow industrials.
He was not the first to do so. One Dow-oriented fund, sponsored by Templeton, carried a hefty sales charge and was liquidated a decade ago. Another, Lexington Corporate Leaders, has been around for half a century but still has the same companies that were in the Dow 50 years ago. A third, sponsored by Van Kampen Merritt, buys the 30 Dow stocks but puts an equal number of dollars in each company instead of buying an equal number of shares, meaning it does not exactly track the average, Mr. Adler said.
Before long, Mr. Adler plans to offer other indexed products, probably one to track medium-term Treasury securities followed by one for foreign stocks.
First, though, he needs to make ASM a success. "I've got to get one up on its feet," Mr. Adler said, "before I start adding."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Steven H. Adler started an index fund with a simple and seemingly foolproof strategy, but after four years it is not breaking even. (Bobby Sanchez for The New York Times)
