HEADLINE: DIARY

BYLINE: JOSHUA MILLS 

 BODY:

BANKING
Tough Talk in Tokyo
The Ministry of Finance announced changes in how Japanese banks are regulated, hoping to end a series of financial disasters and restore foreign confidence. The guidelines call for banks to bolster their internal inspection and control systems and to draw up more detailed policies for risk management and control. Announced by the Finance Minister, Masayoshi Takemura, the guidelines also address how much of a bank's assets can be lent to a single borrower.
 In New York, a Federal grand jury indicted the former manager of Daiwa Bank's New York branch, Masahiro Tsuda, on charges that he had conspired with other bank officials to defraud the Federal Reserve by concealing trading losses. Federal and New York State regulators had previously ordered Daiwa to shut its United States operations.
 
RETAILING
Merchants Are Glum
The constant sales, the promotions, the ferocious competition of every sort for shoppers' attention and dollars left retailers with a holiday season they'll be happy to forget. Indeed, industry watchers said it was the worst holiday season in years, maybe decades. And the salvation, they said, will come only when shoppers have fewer choices. "The nation is clearly over-stored," one consultant said.
 
LOSSES
'Inappropriate Trades' at Pier 1
One of the few retailers to enjoy a strong year through its sales of wicker chairs and other decor, Pier 1 disclosed that it had lost $20 million from an outside consultant's "inappropriate trades." The company declined to name the consultant, but a lawyer for Capital Insight, a money management firm in Beverly Hills, Calif., said it was the firm in question. By week's end, Capital Insight and its president, Jay Goldinger, were telling clients they were going out of business.
 
ALTERNATIVE ENERGY (I)
Setback for Electric Cars
California is backing away from a requirement that 2 percent of all cars sold within the state by 1997 be electrically powered. The auto industry hailed the change as a recognition that market forces were more efficient at setting standards than government regulation; air-quality advocates called the proposal a sellout to the oil and auto industries.
 
ALTERNATIVE ENERGY (II)
In the Doldrums
Once the proud symbol of the coming age of wind power, Kenetech, the largest builder of wind turbines, has been battered. Problems range from blades snapping off its wind turbines to shareholder suits asserting that corporate officers misled analysts.
 
MANAGEMENT
Skipping Grades
Toyota said younger staff members would find it easier to gain promotion, a step that was widely interpreted as a test of whether seniority could become less important in choosing the auto giant's leaders. The change was part of a restructuring announced four months after Hiroshi Okuda was named president and was asked to deal with Toyota's falling share of the domestic auto market and the harm that a strong yen was doing to sales abroad.
 
ACQUISITIONS
Betting on Annuities
GE Capital Services agreed to pay $960 million for the Life Insurance Company of Virginia, a subsidiary of Aon. The acquisition would make GE Capital one of the five largest sellers of annuities.
 
LANDMARKS
Shuffling Out of Buffalo
The Corn Exchange of Buffalo, once a key inspection point for American agricultural trade, will be closed in early 1996 after 150 years in business. The St. Lawrence Seaway, which was expected to bring boom times when it opened in 1959, wound up providing ships with a means to bypass Buffalo.
 
THE BUSINESS PRESS
Bill Who?
Striving to provide broad and deep coverage of high technology, The Wall Street Journal mentioned the Microsoft Corporation in 1,033 articles during 1995 (an average of 2.8 articles a day). The New York Times had only 868 articles (2.4 a day) mentioning Microsoft, but had more articles bearing the name of William H. Gates, the company's chairman -- 168, compared with 149 in The Journal. By contrast, the chief executive of General Motors, appeared in only 26 articles in The Journal and 21 in The Times. His company was mentioned in 910 articles in The Journal and 666 in The Times.

HEADLINE: Visions of New York's Future

 BODY:
Of course, New York isn't the only city that attracts dreamers. But having organized its port a century-and-a-half ago with a savvy and ruthlessness that left Boston and Philadelphia dazzled in defeat, New York perhaps has a birthright to predict the future.
Ask for visions and themes emerge. The computer is a major focus, as if it were a torpedo thrust at our vanity and our reason to be -- physical proximity. There is fascination with the future shape of the city. Will it be vertical? Will our waistline center remain the inland of Manhattan, or will development move toward the rivers, or even skip over the waters, into Queens and the South Bronx? 
 Some fear a loss of idiosyncrasy; others see fringe groups gaining more influence in an information age. There is intense concern about racial and class fragmentation and the uneducated in a city that will demand more education. There is also hope and excitement.
 
THEATER TRAUMA
 
George C. Wolfe
 Artistic Director of the Public Theater
The nightmare version is that New York City becomes one giant Andrew Lloyd Webber theme park with theaters showing all Andrew Lloyd Webber shows, with tubes connecting the theaters. Oh yes, and revivals -- maybe in the tunnels you could pass the revivals as you go from one Andrew Lloyd Webber show to the next.
Now, my fantasy is there is no concept of uptown and downtown theater, but just theater showing extraordinary American plays by playwrights that reflect of all American realities, and people trying to reinvent the musical form and that the New York audience looks like all of New York City, because right now it doesn't.
It's a very bleak time. At this point in time there are people in power who don't understand why culture is important, and that's scary because it's become cultural survival of the fittest.
There's a Russian proverb that goes: you must support the most talented people because untalented people will support themselves. And that's important. Right now the biggest challenge is economic; theaters are just going to die. The ecology of New York theater is intricate. Off Off Broadway houses -- the theaters that tend and grow playwrights and artists for a year or two before a larger theater like the the Public or Lincoln Center can help -- a lot of those theaters could conceivably die.
If that does happen, the nightmare I described is more likely to happen. And if you continue to nurture, then my fantasy will happen.
 
Meredith Monk
 Performance Artist
I do think you'll see decentralization. I've had this vision of being an art farmer, where I'd plant my seeds and do my gardening elsewhere, and then just come into city at the harvest. I don't know if artists have to be here to do the work itself, but you need the city, the audience to share the work. To have the experience of the city in reality will always be part of the artists' lives, but it may not be the only way of going.
One thing I sense happening now is that because of adversity, young people coming here have to have a lot more imagination. When I first came in '64, there was a lot of energy, a lot of performing spaces opening, but the financial aspect wasn't the prime aspect. But now it's very hard for young people. What would be ideal would be all kinds of gathering places where there would a kind of energy and exchange. Maybe take down some buildings and put down some grass. Sometimes I feel like a Luddite. "Get out that hammer for the computer!"
The dystopic view is that everything closes down and the gatekeepers become more and more powerful and the young people get so discouraged they leave and then there'll be no energy or imagination. You have to build the courage to take the risk. Past, present, the future. That's the beauty of art, to risk the unknown.
 
VERTICAL CITY?
 
 Hugh Hardy
 The architect who restored the New Victory Theater, the New York Botanical Garden, the Rainbow Room and Windows on the World.
One of the things that is lasting about New York is the vertical density. Things are squashed together here by the 19th, well it's really an 18th century street grid. So it won't be possible to change that. And this will always make New York a great place with a lot of elbowing and shoving. Even virtual reality can't do that for you!
Wall Street has to be reinvented because its role as a financial center is changing. With computers there has been an enormous shift in the daytime population downtown. In the future, lower Manhattan will be a place where people live, work and play. The city is actually encouraging this with tax abatements.
I think about what the city will look like all the time. I do think there will be fanciful large structures made with new technology. Buildings will be built, for instance, with glass that changes opacity with the sunlight. So you will be able to see into them when it's dark and not when it's light. And there will be ever greater extremes between the old and the new. No architect in town has challenged the visual status quo much, but they will.
For one thing there won't be the need for large, boxy buildings with many workers in the future. In the 1980's people built large buildings with open floors. There will still be large buildings but they will be mixed use between residential and business. Or many businesses. The era of the single-owned office building is ending.
I can well imagine new subways completed and a connection to the airports constructed. And there might be water taxis to the airports. How funny! An old idea adapted to new technology. We won't have jet packs, but I can imagine a trolley system on the streets. Americans always want the future to be made out of something they have never seen before, but I think the future could be using old things in a new way.
 
Kevin Kelly
Executive Editor of Wired magazine
My suspicion is that in the short term it's bad news for a place like New York City. Skyscrapers are over. There's so little reason for them because people don't need height. The question is going to be what to do with all those tall buildings. People don't need to be that tight anymore. Skyscrapers were emblematic of achievement, but we don't believe in that kind of progress any more. It was high-tech because we could do it, with telephone and elevators, but there are other kinds of technology now -- fiber optics and overnight delivery, so you're going to end up with something that more resembles edge cites. Skyscrapers might end up as storage units or something.
The future of New York City may be an inversion, with the outskirts being the engine, driving the city. And the intellectual and cultural centers might move to places that are not that dense. I mean, people write off the Bronx, or write off Queens, that nothing consequential is happening there. But I could imagine in 25 years something happening like in San Francisco, where what to do with downtown is a problem. Maybe they become the projects; Manhattan as the projects and Queens as a multi-media alley or multi-media gulch.
Cities will live on as long as meeting someone face to face is more powerful than an electronic encounter.
 
FITNESS
 
 Doug Levine
 Owner of Crunch, the fitness conglomerate
 In two to three years the gyms will be full of virtual reality technology. It's going to be huge. You're going to be able to get on a bicycle and say, "I'm going to race the second day of the Tour de France," and depending on your mood you can race against Greg LeMond and be able to say "Greg I'm going to beat you," and he'll answer you back.
There will be a screen surrounding you on the bicycle on three sides. This will immerse you in the image.
Right now there is virtual reality, but the simulators that are out there are very crude. It's like looking at a television screen. It's developing, it's about two to three years away until it's cool.
The second thing that is going to happen much sooner is that boxing, which started as a fad two years ago (when white-collar boxing and women for the first time entered the sport), will become mainstream. Boxing used to be a sport full of cigars and corruption and all of sudden it started to be cool to do martial arts and to box. Now it's past the cool stage and it's become a legitimate exercise category with people hitting speed bags, heavy bags and jumping bags.
And, there will be a molding of entertainment and fitness, where you're going to see people watching first-run movies while running on a treadmill.
The whole population has moved from working 9 A.M. to 6 P.M. to 9 to 9 and they are stressed out. I think there will be a return to the eastern disciplines like Yoga, Meditation and Pilates, an old system of posture developed by a dancer to teach people alignment.
In New York you're going to see -- as the paths along the river develop -- people will be using the waterfront more. You're going to see people skate boarding, roller-blading and running and biking around all of Manhattan.
 
FASHION
 
Carolina Herrera
 Fashion designer
 I think New York has taken over a lot, especially now that all the fashion shows are in one place in Bryant Park, and everyone doesn't have to run around.
New York fashion is going to go very far. If we keep it together, New York will become a real competitor with Paris. Well, it already is, but Paris is still the fashion capital of the world.
In New York, there's a lot of talent. A lot of the main European designers are inspired by the sportswear of the Gap and Banana Republic. It's very American. The designers are inspired by the running shoes, the T-shirts, the jeans and khakis. It's very New York. As for all the black clothes in New York, it's very international. It's a sea of black.
Still, it's very difficult for New York fashion designers to be accepted in Europe. Ralph Lauren is an exception.
I think New York is fantastic. All the European designers want to do business in New York -- that's what they aspire to.
Clothes are going to continue to be timeless, classic but with a modern twist. The younger generation is getting more elegant and more fashionable. There will be a return to classic elegance. People will try to look less grungy.
Hopefully there will be less of the tennis sneakers with the skirts in New York. That's so unflattering, as are those leggings that everyone wears with the big jackets. Unless you're very young and have a great figure, you're simply a fashion victim.
There will be a lot of sportswear in the day but the young women will be more elegant and glamourous at night.
There will be less excessive makeup. Women look so much better when they don't wear too much makeup. I hate everything that is in excess.
Americans are getting better looking, great figures, great looking.
 
DEVELOPMENT
 
 Arthur Imperatore
 Developer and chairman of the board of NY Waterway.
I believe there will be a great impetus, a kind of a wave of future growth in ancillary mass transport. New ways just to get to the highways, to the tunnels, to the bridge networks, to park and rides. Lots of ferry locations on the Hudson, on Staten Island -- in places like Kill Van Kull, and in places like South Jersey, where you wouldn't think. . .
And where this happens you're going to engender development, you're going to see a change in quality of life, in improved property value and commercial development in those areas, and it will open the economy of the region.
For example, Kill Van Kull is not the most picturesque place, but 50 years from now the old industrial sites will likely change into sites for recreation, and residential housing. I see that. You have to envision a whole change in the transportation pattern.
There will be an increased emphasis on intermodality -- buses to boats, rails to buses. People will change modes; we've proven that. And transportation is the catalyst that has to precede any sort of development.
Even in Queens, for example, and in Manhattan, in the TriBeCa area, you're seeing everyone building out toward the river. The city 150 years ago was different -- they filled the river and land area was made. The sites were operated by major transportation sources, seagoing commerce. But that's all changed. I anticipate development building toward the river and utilizing land that had been unavailable. Twenty-five, 50 years in the future you'll have office buildings on what is now the edge of river. Look at Battery Park.
It will be an evolution of uses and zoning and economic opportunities used to gentrify many parts that are in disuse. The South Bronx could be a wonderful opportunity, cheap land taken over by the city. I think you'll see new hubs in Queens, and redevelopment of parts of Brooklyn.
I see service big time on the East River, stops at 78th Street, 61st Street, 51st Street, 34th Street, all up and down the East Side, with boats going down to the financial district. Communities -- you bet they'll grow.
It's the enhancement of travel. Incrementality, interconnectivity, intermodality, those are the key words.
 
CLASS, RACE, SECURITY
 
 
 Sharief Shabazz
 Nation of Islam community security force
 Bedford-Stuyvesant
 I see the future as economically worse. The future looks very chaotic. People are walking around with no hope, despair. They are walking time bombs. Two years from now it will be worse. Race relations will be worse. There used to be economic programs for black people back in the 60's. Now there are no more programs. Black people have nothing. Do not be shocked in a year or so. Blood will be running in the streets like water from that fire hydrant. It's reality.

 John Devine
 Adjunct Professor of Education at New York University.
 Expert in troubled schools
 If the present trend continues, you're going to find more and more schools with metal detectors. The security guard force is going to get bigger and bigger. If you look at it, it's a straight line curve going up; in 1969, we had zero security guards. Now, the present number, last time I counted, is 3,000 plus and growing, which is slightly less than the entire police force in Boston. It would be the ninth largest police force in the country. More metal detectors are going to be moving up to the middle-class schools, to the more integrated high schools. The knee-jerk reaction is going to be metal detectors.
 The public space in these schools, and by public I mean everything but the classroom, is being patrolled by security guards. More importantly, and significantly, there's been a change in the role of the teacher outside of classroom space. There's the absence of the superiority gaze. That role has been taken over by the guards. Guards have an influence that can surpass that of the teachers, both positively and negatively.
 The security guards are mostly community people, but the trend has been to have them just thrown into a school. They are the dominant adults in the public spaces -- they're hanging out with the kids; they are much closer to them in a human way than most of the teachers are. They're giving kids guidance, preventing suicides, giving religious advice. Maybe in the future they should be trained as counselors. But educators are not focused on this. It's not chic to run programs for security guards. Most advanced thinking thinks that they should be trained by police.
 
Saskia Sassen
 Professor of urban planning at Columbia University, and author of "Cities in a World Economy" (Pine Forge-Sage, 1994)
 This is no longer the era of an imperial capital, like Amsterdam in the 1600's, and London in the 1800's or New York after World War II. Things have really changed. Its going to be a while before these new ways of thinking come about. The fluidity of this time is immense. In the last 10 years there's been a radical reconfiguration. That's not going to be easy for New York -- not being No. 1 any more. That doesn't mean there's another city that's No. 1, but this is an era of the network. This is the era of telecommunications, of highly mobile culture.
 I'll tell you the weak point. It doesn't lie at the top, it lies at the middle and the lower middle. In New York, you can get the best, and you can also get the low-wage dirty work. The immigrants do that work and they will work, hard, hard, hard. What you don't have enough of is the solid middle. This is what you hear employers say again and again. The Achilles heel might well be in the public education sector. That whole infrastructure, public education, that allows lower- and middle-class to survive in the city needs far more resources, more attention. It creates a real void in the middle of this labor market -- you need good secretaries, good workers, good service and administrative workers in the middle, and they're likely to come out of the public schools.
 My gut feeling is that I've been in enough meetings with business managers to know there is a growing awareness, in elite business sectors, that the lower half of the city matters, that public education matters. But recognition is one thing; doing it is another. The whole civic milieu needs to be mobilized.
 
THE FAMILY
 
Tom Stoddard
Law professor, civil libertarian and AIDS patient.
 I'm certain gay marriages will be fully recognized. In fact, the state will recognize all forms of family. Families will be defined by the people living in them. Say, two senior citizens living together. Or two men or two women will be a family. Whatever form of relationship nurtures and promotes love. The statutes will have to be rewritten, so that family isn't just a matter of blood or marriage.
 We will evolve to a kinder, more realistic world by 2010.
 The media dominates everything, and it will even more so. By 2010, we'll do all our communication by computer. And we'll have many more voices than now. I think it'll make the world more democratic.
 By 2010, we will have reached such a crisis in health care that it will have to be totally reconfigured. The crisis will be the inability of most Americans to receive the kind of care they need. That's what managed care will bring them. Middle-class people will face the kind of problems that poor people face: not being able to go to the doctor of their choice, not being able to obtain necessary services, having to pay for their own medication. Plans won't include some medicines, or mammograms.
 We'll have a cure for AIDS before 2010. The progress is incremental, so it's hard for people to see how much progress has been made. The progress over the last six months has been amazing. I'm in much better shape than I was six months ago.
 Isn't that the great irony? Our health care will become less accessible just at the moment that medical knowledge is exploding beyond our wildest dreams.
 
 Bill Hartgrove
Night manager, Frank E. Campbell Funeral Chapel
 One thing I see, and I know it's going to be a growing idea: a lot of people are coming in to preplan, prearrange and prepay their funerals.
 It's beginning to be very important to people to say, down the line, things might be different. Many times, the people are sick for a long time, or their financial situation changes. They think, "I have the opportunity now to pay for what I want," and then the price is frozen.
 People are becoming more accepting of death. I think they know, in our world today, that people are all over, that families aren't together anymore. You have the older people moving to Florida, and they have to think: What are we going to do? You used to stay within the family, and the family took care of things.
 As far as where people will be buried, there's still a lot of land around when you get out of the city. You might go to cemeteries in Westchester, Long Island and New Jersey. And many cemeteries are building mausoleums and crypts. So there is space.
 
INFORMATION, THE SOCIAL CITY
 
Robert J. Lifton
Distinguished Professor of Psychology and Psychiatry at City University
The availability of information is going to increase, so everybody in New York City is going to have even more opinions. That's sort of a scary idea. The mass media technology, including computers, changes the setting of the city. You can't isolate groups -- groups that are suffering can be more quickly outspoken, and more quickly heard. It's not clear where that leads; everything is open-ended. It depends on what we do with it.
The city will continue to be part of the nerve of America, a nerve that's easily irritated and can lead to nasty behavior, but it's an active nerve nonetheless.
One of my more recent books, "The Protean Self: Human Resilience in a Time of Fragmentation," talks about the relatively more flexible many-sided self that each of us has much of, that we somehow make work. That's in opposition to the fundamentalist self, the sort of all or nothing self. New York has a lot of the Protean, collectively and individually. It's there in the literature and art. And while many artists and writers have moved away, they still have a foot or a hand or an easel or a computer in New York, I mean some form of connection with New York.
I hear it again and again, from friends from Europe, and friends from Japan. They say they feel more alive in New York than everywhere else. Something is touched or awakened or threatened -- all of that -- by its very intensity.

 Judith Regan
 Head of Regan Books
 Publisher of Howard Stern's "Miss America"
The publishing business is centered in New York, and it's not going to change anytime soon. All the talent is in New York. Book publishers and editors are going to act as readers for the film and television industry. We're the ones who will be doing the hard labor. Our own little world is coinciding with all these other worlds.
I think the publishing industry is going become more marketing-driven. Books will be more celebrity-driven. But try to find people to write! Try to find people who can work in publishing houses. People graduating from college don't know anything. It's very shocking. They can't read, and they can't write. The level of writing has gone down and down.
I think that this is going to change because our values are becoming more conservative, and instead of doing what feels good there's going to be restructuring of the education.
Yes, the Internet will be a presence, but the Internet is the written word. You still have to sit there and read. The old news is the new news. What it comes down to is the story. It's whether you have a good story or you have important information to tell.
 
Michael Musto
Gossip columnist for The Village Voice
I see no reason why gossip mania won't continue to grow, to the point where, in 2010, it will not only have supplanted hard news, it will have obliterated it.
The Post will have news only on Page 6, and gossip on every other page. And we will know more about celebrities than celebrities will know about themselves. Recently the meaning of the word celebrity has broadened to include somebody who crashed on the couch of someone who's tried for double murder. Celebrities can now be ordinary, or subordinary, people who fall into extraordinary circumstances. And that trend will continue.
 
 Deb Parker
Co-manager of the Beauty Bar, on East 14th Street
In 10 years, if I'm still in New York, New York might be the most pathetic city around. All the coolest people are moving away; all my friends are moving elsewhere, to smaller towns. So New York might get to be like a bunch of little small towns, too. Nobody ever goes beyond a 20-block radius anyway.
The city will probably be really expensive to live in, so the only people living here will have some scam, like a $200 apartment or great windfalls of money.
In 10 years, it'll be a city of people who were something, has-beens, and people starting to come up. And I'll have 'em all in my bar. Some are going to be slumming, and some are going to come in to ask me questions. And I'll still be there, making money. I'll have the Donald Trumps and the coked-out strippers. That's what I like -- a mix. That's what New York is good for.

HEADLINE: CONNECTICUT GUIDE

BYLINE: By ELEANOR CHARLES

 BODY:

ALL ABOUT HOLOGRAPHY
"The Nature of Holography," which opened yesterday and will continue through Feb. 11 at the Art Cellar in Clinton, features more than 40 holograms and 15 panels explaining how this three-dimensional, laser-produced imagery works. 
 It started back in 1947 when Dennis Gabor, a Hungarian physicist, was working on the potential of focused light. But it was not until the invention of the laser in the 1960's that holography began to mature.
Twelve images in one section of the display are of flowers, birds, insects and other small natural species. Pulsed laser holograms of lion cubs, a python, tiger and other wildlife creatures are in a second sector. The third and final section contains the 15 explanatory panels.
Admission is $3 for adults, $1 for children under 13, free for pre-schoolers. The gallery, in the Clinton Center Mall on Route 1, is open Tuesday through Saturday from 11 to 4 P.M.; Sunday from 1 to 4 P.M., and Thursday evenings until 9 P.M. The number for more information is 860-669-1235.
 
FIRST NIGHT DANBURY
Not all First Nights in Connecticut have survived, but Danbury will celebrate its seventh alcohol-free birthday today from 4 P.M. to midnight.
The afternoon is devoted mainly to children, with an entertainment lineup that includes Bubble-Mania, Dr. Rock's Dinosaur Rock Revue, Juke Box Mania, Gale Alexander's magic, Ted Lawrence's mini-circus and lots of music and dance.
There is a train exhibit at The Galleria, and Zero Gravity's play gym is open for children up to age 12, featuring a super bouncer, amazing maze and balloon typhoon.
The First Night Resolution Wall on the green is up again for those dauntless souls who are willing to record their 1996 New Year's resolutions in public, and a Midnight Finale starts at 11:30 P.M. with music, dancing, street performers and a surprise as the year turns over.
Art exhibits are at the Women's Center, West Street Co-Op and Gallery 13, other exhibits are at the Scott-Fanton Museum, and a roster of entertainers includes the Danbury Opera Campany, Danbury Actors Repertory Company, Blue Ribbon Cloggers, Amy Gallatin and Stillwater country duo, Salt and Pepper gospel ensemble, the comic Bob Stanhope, Vinny and Ray Orchestra, and the Greg Wall Klezmer Band.
Admission to all events is by First Night Button, which costs $10 for adults, $7 for children under 12 and adults over 62, available at numerous downtown locations. The number is 792-5095 for more information.
 
FIRST NIGHT HARTFORD
Hartford's sixth First Night, scheduled from 2 P.M. to midnight, features about 60 performances in various downtown locations culminating in a People's Procession with musicians, dancers, costumed revelers, and city officials marching from City Hall to Bushnell Park for midnight fireworks by Grucci.
A few of the highlights include mask-making workshops, treasure hunts and entertainment for children at the Wadsworth Atheneum from 1 to 5:30 P.M.; the Neilds Rock 'n' Roll Trio; the Boston Baked Blues Ensemble, and the Nifty Fifties Band.
Admission buttons good for all events ranging from puppet shows to clowns, theater, storytelling and dance, cost $9 for adults, $1 for children under 12. They may be purchased at dozens of locations around town. The number is 728-3089 for more information.
 
BRITISH PRINTS
Photography buffs will want to stop by the Yale Center for British Art in New Haven to see its exhibition of contemporary British printmaking by the Paragon Press, on display through Feb. 4. Founded in 1986 by Charles Booth-Clibborn, Paragon established a reputation as a prodigious and innovative publisher of 34 portfolios of images by British artists.
Emphasizing suites of related material rather than individual prints, and giving artists free rein to set up the sequences in collaboration with studio printers, Paragon has been credited with reinvigorating British printmaking. Richard Long's "Rock Drawings" (1994) exemplifies that process.
About 150 works are on view, some by artists who had not previously been involved in prints. Examples are Antony Gormley's various body parts in his collection "Body and Soul" (1990) and Victor Burgin's "Fiction Film" (1991), a video-computer originated work.
Established Scottish figurative painters like Stephen Campbell and John Bellamy are represented, as well as British school painters including Hamish Fulton, Therese Oulton, Christopher LeBrun and Anish Kapoor.
The exhibition was organized by the Scottish National Gallery of Modern Art in Edinburgh, where Paragon Press was originally located before moving to London, and the Yale Center for British Art.
Hours are 10 A.M. to 5 P.M. Tueday throguh Saturday, and noon to 5 P.M. Sunday. The center is located at 1080 Chapel Street and the phone number for more information is 432-2850.
 
FOREIGN POLICY
This season's Darien Community Association Academic Lecture Series focuses on "Election 1996: Perspectives on Foreign Policy." The talks will be given on four consecutive Thursdays beginning this week at 10 A.M. in the association's headquarters, 274 Middlesex Road.
Stanley B. Greenberg, the author of "Middle Class Dreams: The Politics and Power of the New American Majority," will speak on "Overview: Defining the Current International Issues and Voter Reactions for the 1996 Elections."
Derek Boothby, director of the European division of the United Nations Department of Political Affairs will speak on "Global Hot Spots: The Challenge to Peacekeeping" on Jan. 11; and on Jan. 18 Peter Skerry, visiting fellow in governmental studies at the Brookings Institution will explore "The Immigration Debate: An Economic, Political or Human Rights Crisis?"
The final lecture, titled "Issues in International Trade and Finance: Why Every Voter Should Care," will be given by Richard C. Breeden, chairman of international financial services of Coopers & Lybrand.
Series tickets are $40, individual lecture tickets are $10 and $12 at the door, and $4 for students. Baby-sitting may be arranged by advance registration. The number for more information is 655-9050.
 ELEANOR CHARLES

HEADLINE: ON POLITICS;
Mrs. Whitman So Far: Good, With an Asterisk

BYLINE: By Iver Peterson; Iver Peterson is Trenton bureau chief of The New York Times

DATELINE: TRENTON

 BODY:
Today is the last day the state may legally withdraw money from the Unemployment Insurance Trust Fund to help pay the hospital costs of the working poor, and it also brings to a close the first half of Christine Todd Whitman's remarkable term as Governor. Charity care is an obscure issue, but one anchored in the muck of the law, while the anniversary is evanescent. It is too bad that the legal problem is likely to overshadow the celebratory one as we go into the new year.
When the Governor began her campaign in the early summer of 1993, she disdained the idea of pledging a tax cut. The idea was raised by some Republicans who were aware that, by then, the "no new taxes" promise had begun to lose some of its strength as a philosophy and that more potent medicine was needed. 
 When her campaign seemed to be stalled and the voters were looking for reasons to vote for this likeable woman (and against Gov. Jim Florio), Mrs. Whitman relented and took the pledge, and the rest is history: she rocked the state in her inauguration by making part of the cut retroactive, and the next year completed the full 30 percent cut in income taxes a year ahead of schedule. The National Republican party loves her, latecomers to the tax-cutting scene call themselves Whitman Republicans, the party leaders in Washington court her support.
So is tax cutting the real history of the Whitman administration at half time? Would we have anything else to talk about today if they had not happened? If Mrs. Whitman had never cut a tax, we would still be talking about how she confronted New Jersey's preposterously expensive state and local government and educational apparatus, one that has given the state the second-highest property taxes in the country, in the name of home rule. No Governor took on this juggernaut of the status quo as aggressively as she, by conducting public audits of local books, by penalizing wasteful school districts, and, yes, by starving many prosperous, high-spending communities of state funds that had for years been taken for granted.
We would be talking about how the Governor used her national prominence to sustain the Republican Party's traditional Eastern wing, with its moderation, its polite and tolerant regard for others, against the rough-and-tumble types beyond the Delaware Bay.
And we would be talking about how Christie Whitman and a generally distinguished cabinet managed to connect state government to the voters to an extraordinary extent, using public appearances, radio and television call-in shows and the hearings on issues like school funding and juvenile crime to open government up to public comment.
No moment summarized Mrs. Whitman's rare public touch more perfectly than at the rally held in memory of Megan Kanka, the murdered 7-year-old from Hamilton, when the Governor instinctively enfolded Megan's weeping mother in her arms and held her in the name of a grieving state.
It is a pretty good record to build on, a principled, foundation-laying first two years that has put down the base for two more years, or six more years, to hear the Governor talk, of moderate, incremental reform. But first the Whitmanauts have to weather the charity-care issue, and its unwelcome echoes of the tax-cutting promise.
For it was largely on the strength of a diet of anti-tax sentiment from the Governor that prompted her own Republicans to reject her preferred solution, an increase in cigarette taxes and a continued but diminishing raid on the Unemployment Insurance Trust Fund.
The Republican leadership, grown bold on the Governor's own anti-tax rhetoric, refused even to vote the bill out of committee.
Meanwhile, Mrs. Whitman overplayed her hand with the Democrats who, after four years in the minority in both houses, were in no hurry to carry water on a tax increase for Republicans.
The Democrats' resistance was, if anything, hardened when the Republicans argued that because many charity hospitals are in urban (read Democratic) districts, the Democrats had a greater obligation thant suburban Republicans to support a tax increase.
"We told the Governor, 'You have 53 votes in the Assembly, you only need 41, why aren't your people voting for your tax package?' ", said Neil Cohen, a Union County Assembly Democrat and minority whip. The unspoken answer is that taxes are anathema in New Jersey these days, thanks to the Governor's campaign pledge. The irony is that charity care taxes, more than any subject, will overshadow the celebrations and commemorations of a new Assembly term on Jan. 9, and the start of the second half of the Whitman governorship.

LENGTH: 1013 words

HEADLINE: ROAD AND RAIL;
Toward Greater Efficiency and Safety in a State Perpetually on the Move

BYLINE: By DAVID W. CHEN 

 BODY:
Few issues stir as much passion in so many communities as the simple act of moving from place to place: from home to work to the mall and home again. So 1995 brought sharp disputes, in cities, towns, counties and the state as a whole, over a host of transportation issues: new highway construction, train connections, light-rail plans, pedestrian access, employee car pools, the 55-mile-per-hour speed limit.
But there was not much controversy over a technical but major change in the way the state pays for transportation. In November, voters overwhelmingly approved the dedication of a bigger portion of the gas tax to the Transportation Trust Fund, the pool of money from which the state pays for highways and mass transit projects. 
 As a result, 9 cents of the gas tax, up from 2.5 cents, will find its way to the fund, which provides half of the $1 billion a year needed to build and maintain New Jersey's transportation infrastructure. Passage was crucial, transportation advocates said, because the trust fund was running low on cash.
"Transportation can be a huge drain on the state budget, and for that reason, it needs to have a stable base of funding," said Janine Bauer, executive director of the Tri-State Transportation Campaign, a coalition of environmental and planning groups. "This is just good planning and sound fiscal policy."
The state also issued a blueprint for transportation, identifying eight initiatives to guide the state over the next two years. The North Jersey Transportation Planning Authority, responsible for approving federally financed projects, outlined the needs and problems of 13 northern counties in the face of chronic congestion and increasing commuter traffic over the next 20 years.
To discourage inefficient travel, the Department of Transportation opened a car-pool lane on Route 287 this month; it was five miles long and is eventually to expand to 20. Even so, motorists will no Even so, motorists will not be able to drive any faster: despite the repeal of national maximum speed limits, and the recent moves by New York and Pennsylvania to raise the limit to 65 m.p.h. on many rural stretches of major highways, Governor Whitman remains committed to the 55-m.p.h. limit, citing the state's density and safety concerns.
Safety concerns also gained in several other areas. The Department of Transportation released a bicycle and pedestrian master plan that was praised by the department's usual critics. More municipalities tried to pamper pedestrians, adding zebra-striped crosswalks, brick markings and signs to remind drivers that pedestrians have the right of way.
The train should be a better alternative in North Jersey soon. This year, construction began on the Kearny Connection, a $69 million rail link that will offer direct access to New York City and shave an average of 17 minutes off the trip; the link should be finished by spring. And this month, New Jersey Transit bought 29 commercial and residential properties in Montclair to clear the way for the long-awaited Montclair Connection, which would connect the Boonton Line and the Montclair branch of the Morris and Essex Line.
Both projects have met some opposition: Kearny for its plans to add thousands of parking spaces in towns like Maplewood and Summit; Montclair for its impact on the esthetics and property values of the surrounding neighborhoods.
Ground was broken for a park-and-ride in Jersey City for the Hudson-Bergen Light Rail Transit System, a $1.3 billion project that would send trolleys from Bayonne along the Hudson River waterfront to the Vince Lombardi park-and-ride in Ridgefield. The 20.5-mile system would be built in two stages; the first, from Bayonne to the Hoboken train terminal, could be completed by 1999.
Wider-ranging in scope was a plan unveiled by Chester P. Mattson, Bergen County's director of planning and economic development. In November, he produced a plan, five years in the making, that would integrate trolleys, trains, ferries and existing rail lines to connect most of Bergen County. The estimated cost would be $1 billion; the timetable is uncertain.
In South Jersey, the Department of Transportation intends to decide what to do about mass transit linking the suburbs in Gloucester County to Philadelphia via Camden, with a possible extension to Burlington County later. There are two major options: one would offer direct service to Philadelphia as a branch off the existing Patco line, which now runs through the Camden County suburbs; the other would feature a light-rail system winding along the Camden waterfront and require a transfer. Either way, the project is considered crucial for the area, which has not had commuter rail service in 25 years.
There will also be decisions on rail service to Atlantic City; Amtrak pulled out in 1995 after six years of service, but New Jersey Transit has so far picked up the slack. And there will most likely be a decision on the West Shore Line in Bergen County. New Jersey Transit is studying plans to restore service along a 30-mile stretch from West Nyack, N.Y., to Hoboken by building new tracks along the old right of way, now used by Conrail for freight.
As with most transportation projects, though, the West Shore must resolve questions of financial viability. Initial estimates are that the line could cost $500 million; as a result, the line may have to be built in stages, with the first most likely to run to the Meadowlands sports complex.
For all the plans, financing prospects and talk about transportation, one thing commuters may notice next year is the introduction of more advanced technology to the highway system. In 1996, the state plans to introduce an electronic system on the state's three toll roads that would allow vehicles to zip through tollbooths and have money automatically deducted. The technology should be in every toll barrier by 1998.
"It was an extremely busy and productive year for us," said Frank J. Wilson, the State Commissioner of Transportation. "There's a sense of urgency to get things done."

LENGTH: 1025 words

HEADLINE: THE GREAT OUTDOORS;
Several Strides Forward, and an Explosive Setback, for the Environment

BYLINE: By ANDY NEWMAN 

 BODY:
Not nearly as bad as it could have been: that's how environmental groups in New Jersey summed up 1995, even as a national trend toward deregulation threatened to weaken the programs responsible for much of the drop in pollution (and proliferation of red tape) of the past two decades. Here are some environmental developments in the last year.
 
DEVELOPING THE MEADOWLANDS In April, officials proposed a new management plan for one of the state's most bitterly contested environmental battlefields, the 20,000-acre Hackensack Meadowlands. The new plan calls for filling in 840 acres of marsh to build several large commercial projects, with the developers paying for the restoration of 3,400 acres of damaged wetlands. 
 Although the 840 acres to be filled is less than half the amount earmarked for development in the existing 1972 master plan, environmentalists argue that there is no reason to fill in any of the region's precious open space when nearby cities -- Newark, Jersey City, Elizabeth -- are starved for economic development. The proposed plan is still under discussion.
EXPLOSION IN LODI An April 21 explosion at the Napp Technologies chemical factory in Lodi killed 5 people and injured 41. Phenols in the runoff from firefighting scoured all life from a three-mile stretch of the Saddle River. Napp was later fined $127,000 by the Occupational Safety and Health Administration, a figure that officials said would have been much higher if Federal safety standards had specifically warned against mixing the two chemicals that caused the explosion.
GOLF COURSE DENIED Governor Whitman killed a proposal to build a 150-acre golf course in the shadow of the Statue of Liberty. Although revenue from the course would have been used to open up some of the 400-plus acres of the 578-acre Liberty State Park that are contaminated and off-limits to the public, course opponents argued successfully that dedicating a third of the park to golfers was too high a price. The Green Acres bond passed in November included $10 million for improvements in the park.
NEW TEST FOR CARS A tough auto emissions bill was passed to help bring New Jersey's smoggy, ozone-laden air into compliance with Federal standards. The inspection program, which officials said would result in the single largest improvement ever in the state's air quality, includes testing for nitrogren oxide, which causes smog. Officials estimate that 50 percent more cars will fail the new test than the current one.
LOOKING AT ANTI-POLLUTION LAWS Governor Whitman's 1996 budget cut $11.5 million and eliminated 219 jobs in the Department of Environmental Protection. The Governor also signed an executive order requiring state agencies to conduct cost-benefit analyses to justify any regulations that are tougher than minimum Federal standards. Many of New Jersey's anti-pollution laws exceed Federal standards.
A LONG DRY SPELL New Jersey's worst drought since 1985 shriveled reservoirs, crops and wildlife and prompted water restrictions in the northern half of the state for six weeks in September and October.
GREEN ACRES The $340 million Green Acres bond issue approved overwhelmingly by the voters in November was the largest ever. More than $60 million will be used by the state to purchase land for conservation, with another $50 million to pay for farmland preservation. The bonding gives local governments $120 million to buy and improve open space.
The bond also sets aside $15 million to buy 250 houses along the flood-prone Passaic River. The Army Corps of Engineers wants to build a $1.9 billion, 21-mile tunnel from Wayne to Newark Bay to prevent flooding, but environmentalists and Governor Whitman say it would be cheaper and more environmentally sensible to let the river run its course and buy out those who built alongside it. The corps produced figures showing that the flood tunnel would be cheaper in the long run, but the Whitman administration has rejected them.
SLUDGE DUMPING A Federal judge in Newark ruled that the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey may not dump contaminated sludge dredged from Port Newark and Port Elizabeth shipping channels in the Atlantic Ocean off Sandy Hook.Even before the ruling, a panel appointed by the Governor proposed burying the sludge in underwater clay pits in Newark Bay instead, a plan endorsed by environmentalists and shippers.
WETLANDS REDEFINED Many of the government actions likely to have the biggest impact on the state happened not in Trenton but in Washington. Proposed changes to the Federal Clean Water Act, for example, would redefine "wetlands" in such a way that as much as half of New Jersey's wetlands would no longer be protected, the New Jersey Audubon Society said. Wetlands cover nearly a fifth of the state.
The Congress's favored tactic, however, was to reshape policy simply by cutting budgets.
"When the endangered species program takes a 38 percent hit and the Environmental Protection Agency takes a 21 percent hit, this is a procedure that weakens laws without debating their merits in the legislative forum," said Richard Kane of the state Audubon Society. The society said that a huge cut in allocations to the Federal Land and Water Conservation Fund meant that 30,000 acres adjoining wildlife refuges in New Jersey that were supposed to be purchased this year were not.
The only encouraging news out of Washington came earlier this month when Speaker Newt Gingrich endorsed a plan for the Federal Government to help buy Sterling Forest, a pristine 20,000-acre tract just north of the New Jersey border in New York that supplies a quarter of New Jersey's drinking water. For years, the states had been searching for a way to raise about $50 million to buy out the forest's owners, who wanted to build a community of 13,000 homes, commercial buildings and golf courses. Governor Whitman had pledged $10 million to buy land in New York, a move environmentalists hailed as far-sighted.
But New Jersey's gain was another region's loss: to raise the $20 million to buy Sterling Forest, the Government wants to sell 56,000 acres of Oklahoma grasslands.

LENGTH: 821 words

HEADLINE: GOVERNMENT AND POLITICS;
In Trenton: Cut Now, Pay Later?

BYLINE: By JENNIFER PRESTON 

DATELINE: TRENTON

 BODY:
Governor Whitman began 1995 with a triumph: she delivered on a campaign promise to cut state income tax rates by 30 percent in two years instead of three. By doing so, she bolstered her standing in the Republican Party and surprised some New Jerseyans by becoming a politician who keeps her word.
By year's end, however, Mrs. Whitman was confronting some of the fiscal perils that her adversaries had warned lay ahead. To help replace the state's present system of using unemployment insurance funds to pay for hospital care for the poor, she proposed a tax increase on cigarettes. The tax, which the Governor calls a "user's fee," failed to win support in the Legislature, and in the last few weeks Mrs. Whitman has found herself portrayed in an anti-tax group's radio campaign as just another tax-and-spend politician. 
 Until now, the Governor, who paid for much of the tax cut by reducing the state's contribution to the pension fund, has been able to avoid some of the tough political choices that often accompany tax cuts. She has not had to reduce state spending sharply, and she has deflected criticism about rising property taxes by suggesting that municipal officials need to better manage their budgets.
Working with the Legislature, she signed several significant bills into law: a package of tort reform law that would place a cap on punitive damages in civil liability cases; mandatory life sentences for repeat violent offenders, and the creation of a new juvenile justice commission and other juvenile justice programs, like a military-style boot camp for young offenders. The Governor was also able to win some health care concessions from the state's largest public employee union. Both Mrs. Whitman and her husband, John, who was appointed to work out the details, can also share the credit for helping to keep the Stanley Cup champions, the New Jersey Devils, in the Meadowlands.
The Governor and the Legislature reduced some environmental regulations, as promised, and eliminated a surcharge on the corporate business tax and the sales tax on advertisements published in telephone directories.
But the contentious debate and the failure of the Governor and the legislators to reach agreement on how to come up with $400 million to pay for the state's charity-care program are signs that 1996 is not going to be easy.
In the new year, leaders will also try once more to settle the question of how to fairly distribute school funds so students in rich and poor districts alike can receive the "thorough and efficient" education the state constitution says they deserve. In more than two decades, no Governor has succeeded.
With no burning statewide issue to capture voters' attention, the off-year Assembly election Assembly produced the lowest voter turnout in almost 20 years. Democrats picked up a couple of seats, but the Assembly remained firmly in the hands of Republicans, who also control the Senate.
Voters did, however, approve a constitutional change that requiring the Legislature and the Governor to provide money to local governments to cover the costs of putting state laws into effect. For example, the state recently considered legislation requiring playgrounds around the state to meet tighter safety guidelines. Once the new law takes effect, the state will have to provide school districts and municipalities with money for new playground equipment to comply with the state law.
Senator Bill Bradley's announcement in August that he would retire at the end of 1996, after three terms, didn't surprise veteran political observers who noted earlier in the year that he was not aggressively raising campaign funds. Saying he was weary of party politics, Mr. Bradley, a Democrat, did not rule out a 1996 Presidential bid. His retirement, meanwhile, allowed the state Democratic Party to shuffle its deck.
Now, Representative Robert G. Torricelli of Bergen County is seeking the Democratic nomination. He has already raised $1.4 million, and since he doesn't have to worry about a primary contest, he can spend most of it in the general election.
Representative Dick Zimmer, a Republican from Hunterdon County, is leading the pack in his party. Mr. Zimmer's challengers are Richard DuHaime, a Freeholder from Passaic, and State Senator Richard J. La Rossa, a Republican from Mercer County.
In 1996, New Jerseyans will get the answer to one of the most frequently asked questions in Trenton: will she or won't she? Will Governor Whitman be asked to join the Republican standard-bearer as a vice presidential candidate? And if so, what will she say?
Mrs. Whitman, who last month endorsed Senator Bob Dole of Kansas for the nomination, dismisses such talk, saying that she wants to serve two terms as Governor. She did say, however, that she believed a moderate Republican would help Mr. Dole win in November, and she offered to try to persuade Colin Powell to sign on.

HEADLINE: NEW JERSEY & CO.;
Confidence Rose, Wariness Prevails

BYLINE: By ABBY GOODNOUGH 

 BODY:
Widespread confidence in the state's economy prevailed last January as many businesses celebrated their most robust gains since the recession. But the beginning of 1996 will be marked by uncertainty in New Jersey as a recent series of mergers and restructuring threaten thousands of jobs in banking, insurance and communications.
Probably the most significant business development to affect New Jersey came in September, when AT&T, based in Basking Ridge, announced that it would break into three smaller companies to cut costs and simplify its operations. By 1997, the communications giant is to divide itself into a long-distance telephone company, an equipment company and a computer manufacturer. 
 Initially, it seemed that the change would not threaten most of the 50,000 New Jersey residents employed by AT&T. But in November, AT&T sent severance packages to 77,800 managers nationwide -- half of the company's supervisory work force. Not nearly enough people have accepted the buyout, and AT&T is expected to announce in January how many jobs it will have to eliminate to prepare for the restructuring.
As AT&T was planning the largest dismantling of a corporation in American history, several bank corporations were merging in New Jersey, raising the possibility of several thousand layoffs at banks around the state. In June, the North Carolina-based First Union bought First Fidelity Bank Corporation in Newark. A few weeks later, PNC Bank Corporation of Pittsburgh acquired Midlantic Bank, based in Edison. And in November, UJB Financial Corporation, based in Princeton, bought Summit Bancorporation, based in Chatham. It is still unclear how many jobs will be lost in the mergers, but Midlantic has said it will cut about 1,600 jobs in New Jersey.
"With all these changes, the immediate impact is going to be painful," said Chris Biddle, a spokesman for the New Jersey Business and Industry Association. "Anyone who works for a large corporation has got to be feeling very nervous because a lot of companies are going through more than one restructuring."
Although New Jersey economists are forecasting some growth in 1996, they say it will not rival that of 1995, when almost every sector of the state economy expanded. Mr. Biddle predicted that about 40,000 private-sector jobs would be created in 1996 but said overall growth would be sluggish. Companies with 50 to 90 employees will probably experience most of the growth, he said, while the largest and smallest businesses will either stagnate or cut back.
The hospital, communications, manufacturing and utilities sectors are expected to suffer the most next year, according to the Rutgers Economic Advisory Service.
Mr. Biddle said that in a recent survey conducted by his association, about 32 percent of about 2,000 companies surveyed said they expected economic conditions to improve in the first half of 1996, compared to 46 percent last year. In addition, 44 percent expect conditions to stay the same, and 25 percent expect them to get worse.
While the short-term forecast is not dazzling, Mr. Biddle said many business leaders had high hopes for the next five years as Governor Whitman's regulatory reforms take effect. Last summer, after Mercer County alone lost more than 5,000 jobs as a result of corporate and government retrenchment, the Governor signed bills to attract new companies to New Jersey by making it easier for them to obtain loans and capital and by easing environmental and other regulations.Although that legislation has not yet brought any windfalls (witness the last-minute decision of the Coffee, Sugar and Cocoa Exchange to stay in Manhattan rather than relocate to Jersey City, a move that would have brought 3,500 jobs), Mr. Biddle said it will probably help retain and attract dozens of businesses in the next two to five years.
One place where deregulation has made a difference is Atlantic City, where the gaming industry continued to thrive last year and the outlook for 1996 is even more promising.
In January, Mrs. Whitman signed a casino deregulation bill that ended state supervision of hundreds of non-gambling functions. Now, a $270 million convention center is under construction, financed by the state's Sports and Exposition Authority. A group of casinos is spending $580 million to add 3,000 hotel rooms and build new parking and retail space. And Steven Wynn, the chairman of Mirage Resorts, who left Atlantic City eight years ago because of local and state regulations, is returning to build a $1 billion two-casino complex.

HEADLINE: NEWS AND TOPICS: DEVELOPMENT;
'Big Boxes' Are Going Strong; Homes and Offices Barely Pass the Gate

BYLINE: By DAVID W. CHEN 

 BODY:
It was a slow year for development in New Jersey, the latest in a string that began during the recession of 1991-92, from which the state has never fully recovered.
After a frantic boom in the mid- and late 1980's, new housing slowed to a crawl, and office complexes sprouted "for lease" signs by the dozens. Only in 1995 did development start to pick up a bit, thanks in part to a recovering economy, lower mortgage rates and changing consumer demands. But there are no predictions of a boom anytime soon. 
 To some, the long slowdown has been a useful respite. "I think this is a good time," said Eileen Banyra, principal of EFB Associates, an environmental and community planning group in Hoboken. "I think it gives everybody a bit more chance to be smarter and more thoughtful about the developments being proposed."
In 1995, the retail sector made the most noise -- not so much for what was completed, but what was approved or started. "You're talking about millions of square feet of retail space that's going to be built in '96 and '97, so they must have a lot of confidence in the market," said Richard J. Brunelli, the president of R. J. Brunelli & Company, a retail leasing concern in Old Bridge.
Especially prominent were "big box" retailers, giant stores that exceed 15,000 square feet and offer discount prices and no frills. At the same time, older, smaller discount stores struggled to hold their own against Wal-Mart, Home Depot and other big boxes: witness the liquidation of Jamesway and the bankruptcy filings of Caldor and Bradlees.
In the office sector, new construction was minimal. But vacancies dropped. Last year, there was a net gain of four million square feet of office space filled; this year, there figures to be five million, predominantly by small service companies, said David T. Houston, president of Colliers, Houston & Company in Bloomfield. There is still a surplus of 20 million square feet -- a product of the speculative overbuilding of office space in the late 1980's -- but the numbers are decreasing.
"We thought the oversupply would last into the next century; now it looks like there's going to be a demand for space within this decade," said Jeffrey A. Horn, executive director of the New Jersey chapter of the National Association of Industrial and Office Properties, in Edison.
In the housing market, though, the numbers were less encouraging for builders. In 1986, a record 57,000 homes were built in New Jersey; this year, that number was be about 25,000.
"It's sort of puzzling because mortgage rates are as good as they've been," said Peter S. Reinhart, senior vice president of K. Hovnanian Enterprises in Red Bank, the state's largest construction company. He attributed the sluggish results, in part, to the long approval process most municipalities require, but he said he hoped some regulations would be standardized in 1996.
Of the areas that did construct or approve new housing, Ocean, Middlesex and Morris Counties led the way, and Dover Township in Ocean County was the busiest municipality, with 574 housing units approved in 1995.
But far from acceding to suburban sprawl, more residents are pining for a small-town feel that incorporates sidewalks and public spaces. And they are becoming more involved.
"We're finding more and more municipalities updating their master plans and working through a public participation process," said Tom Dallessio, public affairs manager for the Office of State Planning. "In particular, we're finding that the marketplace is requiring smaller lots and that people want the amenities of smaller towns. What we're strongly suggesting is that municipalities and developers looking at today's site plans anticipate tomorrow's needs. The parking lots of today may become the urban space or pedestrian plazas of tomorrow."

HEADLINE: THE LIVES THEY LIVED: JAMES RESTON;
A Reston Sampler

 BODY:

ON THE COLD WAR - I have no doubt whatsoever about the outcome of the cold war. No handful of men in the world are smart enough to run this vast Communist empire. The thing is against human nature, and its inner contradicions will bring it down if we are strong, vigilant and patient.
 But this is a marathon, and we're running it like a hundred yard dash. (Feb. 22, 1955)
 
ON J.F.K. - The irony of the President's death is that his short Administration was devoted almost entirely to various attempts to curb this very streak of violence in the American character.
 When the historians get around to assessing his three years in office, it is very likely that they will be impressed with just this: his efforts to restrain those who wanted to be more violent in the cold war overseas and those who wanted to be more violent in the racial war at home. (Nov. 23, 1963)
 
ON RACIAL INTEGRATION - On the tenth anniversary of the Supreme Court's public-school-integration decision, the paradox and the tragedy of the American Negro are fairly clear. He is gaining legally but falling behind economically. He is slowly getting the rights but not the skills of a modern computerized society. He is getting a better chance at unskilled jobs, but unskilled jobs are being wiped out by the new bossman, the machine...
 This is the problem. History is both the Negro's friend and his enemy. (May 15, 1964)

HEADLINE: A Course to Fulfill Students' Dreams

BYLINE: By MERRI ROSENBERG 

DATELINE: VALHALLA

 BODY:
THE notebooks and briefcases of the half-dozen adults sitting in a Westchester Community College classroom here weren't merely filled with homework. What was on display in presentations to their instructor and colleagues were their dreams.
Visions of selling Mary Kay cosmetics danced in their heads as did visions of computer learning centers for children or starting a collectibles store. 
 The students were taking part in a program called "The American Dream Series," offered under the auspices of the Westchester Center for Training and Development, a White Plains-based organization that provides workshops and classes in employee training, youth leadership and entrepreneurial skills. The program, targeted to women and minorities (although other students are welcome), is designed to help entrepreneurs start businesses.
In an economic climate characterized by corporate layoffs, restructuring and downsizing, owning a business is seen as an alternative to being at the mercy of a corporate boss. According to the Small Business Administration, there are 21.5 million small businesses in the country, which account for 52 percent of all sales; employ 54 percent of the private work force and produce half of the private gross national product.
"Our instructors are all entrepreneurs," said Carole A. Jones, founder and executive director of the Westchester Center for Training and Development. "By being business owners themselves, they will be giving participants inside information. We're especially encouraging women and minorities because they usually don't have the background or information they need. Many times, they get a business up and running, but the businesses fail. We can give them information on resources they will need to help them get capital for their businesses."
As part of the eight-week course, offered at the campus here and in Yonkers at 525 Nepperhan Plaza, students cover topics like developing a business plan, designing a marketing program, learning how to obtain loans and understanding how to hire, pay and dismiss employees.
For Judy Douglas, a Greenburgh resident who works as a sales representative for the Omni Berkshire Place Hotel in Manhattan, the course provides insights she can use to develop her own business, Baskets Beaucoup, featuring Caribbean gourmet gifts. "I'm finding that natives don't want the stuff," said Ms. Douglas, who already has the Jamaican Tourist Board as a corporate client. "My market is tourists."
The course's practical applications caused Ann Kessler to rethink what she admits were vague concepts of her own business. "I came in really blind," said Ms. Kessler, who works for Nynex. "I have a burning desire to do my own business. I was thinking of running an organizing service, but I really didn't know what was involved. I didn't know anything about the accounting part, doing a business plan or selecting a location. There's a lot to reconsider. I'm learning what it is to run your own business. I still want to do it, but now I'm thinking more of doing a specialty shop."
The instructors don't play down the difficulties of pursuing an entrepreneurial path. "You have to want to compete," said Dr. Shirley Munoz, a senior partner in the Cora Group, a Manhattan-based consulting concern, who gave a presentation on marketing and selling to one class. "It is going to be tough because you are going to lose lots of times. You should understand your competition. To be in your own business is to test yourself all the time. You've got to learn how to brag."
More information about the next series of classes can be obtained from Ms. Jones at 949-2104.
 
Where Women Network
 
WHITE PLAINS
IF successful entrepreneurship depends in part on successful networking, a recent Women's Services Fair at the Y.W.C.A. here offered an opportunity for women in various organizations to share resources and information.
"This was an outgrowth of the Women's Table that started last year," said Dale Wang of the Westchester Jewish Community Services' organization, which was a co-sponsor of the Nov. 16 event, in cooperation with the White Plains Y.W.C.A., the Westchester County Office for Women and Women's News, a local publication. "There are a lot of organizations that are doing a lot of different things, but we didn't know what we were all doing or providing for women in the county. The purpose is to make our constituents aware of what's out there."
Thirty-five organizations sent representatives to the event. Among the participants were health organizations, government agencies, political groups, social services providers and self-help organizations.
"It's really a symbiotic situation," said Susan Pollet, president of the Westchester Women's Bar Association. "Many of our members already serve these organizations. This is a good opportunity to have some knowledge of what these other organizations do."

SECTION: Section 13WC; Page 1; Column 1; Westchester Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 1347 words

HEADLINE: Optimism Tempered by Uncertainty

BYLINE: By PENNY SINGER 

 BODY:
PROSPERITY or peril? At year's end, seven business leaders peering at the economy through a crystal ball, see reasons for optimism, tempered by a shadow of uncertainty.
Here are their answers to the question, From your vantage as an industry leader, how would you rate Westchester as a place to do business in 1996?
Carl DiMaio, president of the Builders Institute of Westchester and the mid-Hudson region, a 1,200 member building management professional organization:
Despite lowering interest rates, the building and construction industry will find 1996 a difficult year. Dollar-value building estimates range from $500 million to $700 million, a poor year by pre-1990 standards. Underemployment among the building trades will be about 10 percent higher in May 1996 compared to 1995. 
 With the state and the Federal Government in the midst of fiscal-policy debates, the result is likely to be less money for local projects, including infrastructure. While vacant commercial space is showing signs of a mini-rental resurgence, it may still be four or five years before new commercial buildings are built in volume.
On the positive side, demand for housing, both owner and rental, remains strong, and projects are being proposed in numbers not seen since the early 1970's. Job growth in the state, as well as Westchester, also bodes well for the future. The state's economic development agencies have been restructured to improve services, and the net result should benefit new and existing businesses. State tax cuts and regulatory reforms have contributed to job growth, and more can be expected. On the negative side, restrictive land-use regulations and local government and environment excesses continue to dampen building production.
Westchester and the New York area still have impressive strength in the concentration of large corporations, international trade and technological resources. But from a building and development point of view, real growth in 1996 will be elusive.
Norman Sherman, president of the Hudson Valley chapter of the American Society of Travel Agents and a co-owner, with his wife, Vera, of the Suburban Travel and Cruise Center in Larchmont:
For an objective answer, I would have to ask, "Compared to what?" One year ago there were 231 authorized travel agencies in Westchester. The airlines and cruise lines rate Westchester among the most affluent markets in the country for leisure and business travelers. In spite of this engaging domain, at last count, due to closings, acquisitions and mergers, there were only 203 travel agencies still in business.
We are faced with the reality that new times also bring business perils. Westchester is no longer detached from New York City with a captive clientele. Old-fashioned customer loyalty is fading, and as with most of suburbia, two-income city commuting families, discount malls, saturation national advertising and promotional sales gimmicks have all resulted in diverting local clientele. For example, our travel agency is experiencing a surge of pseudo travel agents and seemingly attractive but fraudulent telemarketing.
Prosperity, more than ever, is predicated on personal service, established local reliability and availability.
Westchester, compared to most areas, can continue to be a desirable place to do business to prosper in today's economy. It is a matter of adjusting and coping with a rapidly changing world.
Steven Cohen, president of the Westchester Bankers Association and vice president of the Republic National Bank Yonkers.
With downsizing and bank mergers, the most recent being Fleet Bank, and taking over NatWest, which will eliminate jobs and branches in Westchester, more and more people are looking to start their own businesses. We see the Small Business Administration working with banks to offer guaranteed loan programs for start-up and financing of small businesses.
As we look toward 1996, we will see that these programs have a positive effect on the small-business community in Westchester County. Small businesses in 1996 will stimulate and drive the economy as well as create new jobs.
In September, the United States Export Assistance Center opened its new office in Westchester County. This office helps business with export marketing and trade finance assistance throughout Westchester. Most people don't know that companies like Federal Express and Intel started out as small businesses with the assistance of the S.B.A.
As we enter 1996, we will probably see more banks merging, but if 1995 was an example of how banks were doing with regard to profitability, I'm sure that 1996 will also show record profits.
Brad W. Berger, president and chief executive officer of the Robert Martin Company, Elmsford.
Westchester, along with the rest of the country, experienced a commercial real estate depression from 1989 to 1993. During 1994 and 1995, the market improved significantly; 1996 looks promising as employment and productivity are high, interest rates are low, the stock market is booming, many large companies are hiring again and start-up companies are abundant.
The residential, retail and hotel sectors have already shown dramatic improvements. We believe the office and industrial markets will follow suit because vacancy rates are down, financing is both inexpensive and plentiful and rental rates have strengthened in both New York City and Fairfield County, two contiguous markets with which Westchester competes.
Robert Martin believes that the commercial real estate market will continue to improve in the 90's. We intend to be a significant player in this area as we grow our core business of high-end flex, industrial and office properties via development, construction and acquisition.
Carl Austin, president, Austin Corporate Properties, White Plains.
I see prosperity ahead, as our own company's recent activity would indicate. For example, this past year we were the broker in a number of significant transactions, namely Ciba relocation from Ardsley to Tarrytown.
And this month we are completing the acquisition as broker of 322 acres for Centennial Golf Properties in Putnam County, on the Westchester border, for a 27-hole golf course development.
We are also the broker in the acquisition by Reckson Associates Realty of Halpern Enterprise Realty Corporation on a $83 million transaction. These deals are symbolic of renewed energy and growth in the greater Westchester market, which I feel confident will continue in 1996.
Antoniette A. Emerson, president, Westchester County Board of Realtors, and a realtor at Houlihan Lawrence in Bedford.
Many areas of our state and country continue to feel the effects of these vast changes in somewhat uncertain times. And so do we in Westchester, but to a lesser extent.
Why? Because of the diversity of jobs within the county and nearby areas, the variety of housing options, the excellent educational facilities and the quality of life.
Be it city, suburban or rural, Westchester remains a place where people want to stay if they live here, or to move to if they don't.
Helped by low interest rates, all this has created a stable and growing real estate business. We have experienced three consecutive years of record-breaking residential real estate sales, and we can expect the momentum to continue during 1996.
Marcia Dmochowski, president of the Westchester County Hotel Association and executive manager of the Tarrytown Hilton, Tarrytown.
Nineteen ninety-six should be a healthy year for Westchester. After three to five years of little growth and corporate downsizing, Westchester is now attracting new companies to the area, such as Mastercard and Heineken's.
As such organizations as the Westchester County Association and the Westchester Business Partnership continue their successful efforts to lure more companies to Westchester, the growth trend should continue.
Tourism will also continue to grow through efforts of various organizations to sell Westchester as a leisure destination and an alternative to New York City.
