PUB-DATE: December 31, 1995

SECTION: Section 3; Page 11; Column 4; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 802 words

HEADLINE: VIEWPOINT;
Next Stop, the Statehouse

BYLINE: By BENJAMIN J. STEIN; Benjamin J. Stein, a lawyer and economist, teaches law at Pepperdine Law School in Malibu, Calif.

 BODY:
NOW for a few words about the limits of federalism.
Capping one of the most successful lobbying campaigns in the history of the Republic, the accountants and high-tech companies of America received the ultimate Christmas present this month. Congress passed into law -- over President Clinton's uncertain-trumpet veto -- their beloved "tort reform" as it applies to stockholders' lawsuits contending securities law violations. The accounting firms and high-tech companies asserted that they had been beaten down by meritless lawsuits, and their stupendously effective campaign has resulted in changes in Federal law that largely undo the disclosure requirements and antifraud rules of the last 62 years. 
 Under the new legislation, public companies have the explicit ability to omit material facts in forward-looking statements about their companies' prospects. Accountants are largely freed of liability for approving a company's fraudulent statements, even if those statements cost stockholders their life savings (unless it can be proved that the accountants knew of the fraud). There are also requirements in the law for courts to consider punishing law firms that sue and lose, and drastic cutbacks on what can be discovered by lawyers in early stages of a lawsuit against supposedly transgressing managers of public companies.
So now you might think, it's a big country -- if we can't sue in Federal courts, at least we can sue in state courts. There, we can still find antifraud laws that require full disclosure, no misleading statements or material omissions of fact, and the panoply of protections we assumed were part of American securities law. So what's the problem?
It is twofold. First, Federal courts often have more knowledgeable judges than state courts, and litigants usually want the smartest, best-informed judges possible.
Second, the same juggernaut that gave us Federal restrictions on securities tort cases is now moving to the state level. Flush with the knowledge of what organization, money and publicity can achieve, the accountantsand the high-tech lobby have succeeded in getting a measure, euphemistically called the Shareholder Litigation Reform Initiative, placed on the ballot here in California. If enacted, it would place even worse limits on California state securities litigation than those found in the Federal law.
The initiative would institute loser-pays provisions, thus drastically penalizing stockholders who dare to sue management and fail for any reason -- including running out of money to litigate. The loser-pays arrangements would even more radically inhibit plaintiffs' securities firms that dare to take on entrenched management -- the management fighting the stockholders, as always, with stockholders' money.
This litigation "reform" is widely believed to have an excellent chance of passage. If enacted, it will firmly shut the doors on most securities cases in state courts in California except the most unlikely ones -- in which a defendant admits up front that he maliciously lied, for example, or an accounting firm confesses to having knowingly committed fraud.
Such a law would be an invitation to accountants in other states to mount the same effort. As these laws become a national phenomenon, the field will become hopelessly tilted against the small investor. Most important, the accountants' paradox will have been established: accountants will no longer be fully accountable.
THIS anti-investor movement at the state and Federal levels comes as Social Security looks fragile and Americans increasingly fret about putting more money into savings and investments and worry whether their money is being fairly managed. It comes as the supposedly wounded high-tech companies attract huge capital to their projects. It comes as corporate profits boom. In other words, it comes just as investors need a leg-up most and management needs it least.
Two weeks ago in Tucson, Ariz., at a conference of securities lawyers discussing tort reform, a study revealed that payments to stockholders for Federal securities fraud cases of the last 25 years totaled about $2.5 billion, while corporate profits in the same period were roughly $5 trillion. As I pointed out to the conference, the $2.5 billion is such a relatively small sum to make stockholders whole that it hardly seems worth the fuss.
"It's not small to the accountants," answered one of the authors of the study. And thereby hangs the whole tale. To save accountants from being held accountable, the whole national structure of securities laws is under a highly successful attack. Money talks, especially when it is talking the language of campaign contributions. At the end of 1995, it drowned out the voices of the savers and investors of America -- and it's getting louder.
